Restricting Absolute Ownership (Milk Muṭlaq) via Contractual Conditions in Hanbali Fiqh
Explains Hanbali view on restricting absolute ownership via sale conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanbali position on restricting absolute ownership through conditions in sale contracts and compare it with the Hanafi view that such conditions are void.

Core Idea
Hanbalis permit conditions that limit the buyer's ownership, while Hanafis insist ownership rights are fixed by the Law.

The Hanbali school allows parties to restrict the buyer's ownership in a sale contract through conditions that limit resale, retain usufruct, or require specific actions. This lesson explores that ijtihad and contrasts it with the Hanafi principle that ownership rights are determined by the Law, not by parties.

Guided Path
1
List the permitted restrictive conditions
From the passage, identify examples: seller stipulates buyer not to sell a slave-girl (but keep for concubinage), right of first refusal, retaining usufruct, requiring waqf or emancipation, etc.
These conditions limit the buyer's absolute ownership.
2
Understand the Hanbali rationale
The Law granted parties authority (sulṭān) to determine contract effects according to their interests; ownership becomes restricted (muqayyad) not absolute.
No explicit prohibition exists, so conditions are valid.
3
Contrast with Hanafi position
Hanafis reject these conditions because the effects of ownership are fixed by the Law; parties cannot alter them. Absolute ownership gives the buyer full disposition.
Conditions like 'no resale' contradict the purpose of sale and create uncertainty.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Hanafi: buyer can resell despite condition; Hanbali: condition is valid, seller may rescind if breached.
The practical outcome differs significantly.
5
Evaluate the underlying legal philosophy
Hanbali: party autonomy is broad; Hanafi: rights are pre-determined by Sharīʿa. Both are valid ijtihad.
This illustrates a fundamental difference in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٤/٤٢ - الناحية الثانية :
الفصل (٤٢) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
وقد تفرع عن تلك الناحية الأولى أن جاءنا الاجتهاد الحنبلي بمبدأ جديد هام في قاعدة الشروط العقدية، وهو أن الشرط قد يكون في ذاته وبحسب طبيعته غير ملزم شرعاً للمشروط عليه فهو في الشروط كالعقد ذلك يصح اشتراطه ولا يلغو فتكون ثمرة غير اللازم في العقود، ومع صحة اشتراطه، رغم عدم لزومه، أنَّ الطرف المشروط لمصلحته يحق له فسخ العقد عند عدم وفاء الطرف الآخر بالشرط.
•
ففي هذا النوع من الشروط يكون الطرفان مخيرين: فكما أن الطرف المشروط عليه مخيَّر في الوفاء بالشرط وعدمه يكون الطرف المشروط له مخيراً بين الاستمرار وفسخ العقد في حالة عدم الوفاء بالشرط.
وذلك كما لو اشترطت المرأة في عقد النكاح على الرجل أن لا يسافر أو لا يتزوج عليها»، فإنَّ الرجل حينئذ إنما يستحسن له شرعاً أن يفي بهذه الشروط ما استطاع لكن لا يجب عليه الوفاء ولا يجبر عليه قضاء، لأن الشرع أطلق له حرية السعي والسفر والانتقال، إذ هو أعرف بمواطن رزقه وتتبع حاجاته، كما أطلق له حق التزوج بحدود رسمها الشرع. ولكنه إذا لم يف بالشرط فسافر أو تزوج غيرها كان لها فسخ النكاح بينها وبينه . - حتى إن الحنابلة جوزوا على هذه الطريقة شروطاً مكروهة شرعاً تمس غير العاقدين، ما دامت النتيجة في هذا النوع من الشروط ليست هي الإلزام بتنفيذ الشرط، بل هي حق الفسخ عند عدم تحققه.
وذلك كما لو اشترطت المرأة الثانية في عقد النكاح على زوجها أن يطلق امرأته الأولى. فقد اختلفوا في صحة هذا الشرط، والمحققون على صحته (۱)، (۱) لأنه من مصلحة المرأة الشارطة وإن لم يُلزم به الزوج، فيحق لها فسخ النكاح إذا لم يف الزوج بتطليق ضرتها كما شرط (ر: كشاف القناع شرح الإقناع بحث الشروط الصحيحة في النكاح).
١٥/٤ - الناحية الثالثة :
إنَّ الاجتهاد الحنبلي سوّغ تقييد الملك المطلق عند مباشرة عقده بشروط تحفظية تمنع بعض تصرفات المالك، أو تقيد طرق الانتفاع، أو تستثني بعض الحقوق، أو توجب على المالك بعض الواجبات .
- فجوز لبائع الأمة الرقيقة أن يشترط على المشتري عدم بيعها، بل يختصها بالتسري والاستيلاد، لأنها قد تكون عزيزة على البائع فيريد أن يحفظ لها الصيانة والكرامة .
- وكذلك جوز اشتراط البائع على المشتري أنه إذا أراد بيع المبيع فالبائع أحق باسترداده بثمنه فليس للمشتري عندئذ أن يبيعه على غير بائعه.
- وجوّز أيضاً بيع الشيء مع شرط احتفاظ البائع بمنفعته مدة معينة.
- وجوز أيضاً بيع العقار على شرط أن يقفه المشتري، وبيع العبد على شرط أن يعتقه، ونحو ذلك.
وهذه الشروط وأمثالها لا تقبلها الاجتهادات الأخرى غير المذهب الحنبلي، لأنها عندهم تعارض ما يقتضيه عقد البيع، وما يستلزمه الملك المطلق من حرية تصرف المشتري فيما اشتراه فإنَّ ثمرات الملك وحقوقه لا يرتبها العاقد ؛ وإنما يرتبها الشرع ترتيباً يمنع تجاوز الإنسان فيها على حقوق غيره.
ولكن نظر الاجتهاد الحنبلي في ذلك أن هذه الشروط الإرادية لها تأثير في تحديد آثار العقد بسلطة منحها الشرع العاقدين، وفوضهما بمقتضاها هذا التحديد بحسب مصالحهما. فلا يكون الملك المنتقل بعقد البيع مع هذه الشروط ملكاً مطلقاً بل مقيداً.
14/42 - The Second Aspect: Chapter (42) Ijtihadat on the Authority of Contractual Will. And from that first aspect branched the Hanbali ijtihad bringing an important new principle in the rule of contractual conditions: that a condition may be in itself and by its nature not legally binding upon the one on whom it is placed; it is like the contract in that it is valid to stipulate it and it is not void, so its effect is that of non-bindingness in contracts. And despite the validity of stipulating it, despite its non-bindingness, the party for whose benefit the condition was made has the right to rescind the contract when the other party does not fulfill the condition. So in this type of condition, both parties are given a choice: just as the party upon whom the condition is placed is free to fulfill it or not, the party for whom the condition is made is free between continuing and rescinding the contract in case of non-fulfillment of the condition. For example, if a woman stipulates in the marriage contract upon the man that he not travel or not marry another woman, then it is recommended for the man legally to fulfill these conditions as much as he can, but he is not obligated to fulfill them nor is he compelled by the judiciary, because the law has granted him freedom to seek livelihood, travel and movement, as he knows better the places of his sustenance and pursuit of his needs, and also granted him the right to marry within the limits set by the law. But if he does not fulfill the condition and travels or marries another, she has the right to rescind the marriage between them. Indeed, the Hanbalis permitted by this method conditions that are legally disliked which affect third parties, as long as the result in this type of condition is not the enforcement of the condition, but rather the right of rescission when it is not fulfilled. For example, if the second wife stipulates in the marriage contract upon her husband that he divorce his first wife. They differed on the validity of this condition, and the verified position is its validity (1), (1) because it is in the interest of the stipulating woman even if the husband is not bound by it, so she has the right to rescind the marriage if the husband does not divorce her co-wife as stipulated (see: Kashshaf al-Qina' sharh al-Iqna', section on valid conditions in marriage).
13/4 - The Third Aspect: The Hanbali ijtihad permitted restricting absolute ownership at the time of concluding its contract by protective conditions that prevent some actions of the owner, or restrict methods of usufruct, or exclude some rights, or impose some obligations on the owner. So they permitted a seller of a slavemaid to stipulate on the buyer not to sell her, but rather to keep her for concubinage and bearing children, because she may be dear to the seller and he wants to preserve her dignity and honor. Likewise, they permitted the seller's stipulation on the buyer that if he wants to sell the purchased item, the seller has the priority to repurchase it at its price; then the buyer may not sell it to anyone else. They also permitted selling a thing with the condition that the seller retains its usufruct for a specified period. They also permitted selling real estate on condition that the buyer endow it (waqf), and selling a slave on condition that the buyer emancipate him, and similar conditions. Such conditions are not accepted by other ijtihadat besides the Hanbali school, because according to them they contradict what the contract of sale requires, and what absolute ownership entails of the buyer's freedom of disposition over what he purchased; for the effects of ownership and its rights are not arranged by the contracting parties; rather, the Law arranges them in a way that prevents a person from transgressing upon the rights of others. But the view of the Hanbali ijtihad is that these voluntary conditions have an effect in determining the effects of the contract by an authority granted by the Law to the contracting parties, and it delegated to them this determination according to their interests. So the ownership transferred by a sale contract with these conditions is not absolute but restricted.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage presents a comparison between the Hanbali madhhab and other schools (primarily the Hanafi school) regarding the scope of party autonomy in contracts. The author discusses two aspects of Hanbali ijtihad:

First aspect (al-nāhīyah al-thāniyah): The Hanbali school introduces the concept of non-binding conditions (shurūṭ ghayr lāzima). These are conditions that are valid to stipulate and do not render the contract void, but they are not legally enforceable; fulfillment is commendable but not obligatory. However, the party for whose benefit the condition is stipulated has the right to rescind the contract if the condition is not fulfilled. This gives a choice to both parties: the one bound by the condition may choose to fulfill it or not, and the beneficiary may choose to continue or cancel. Examples are given from marriage contracts: a woman may stipulate that her husband not travel or not marry another wife; if he does, she may seek dissolution of the marriage. Similarly, a second wife may stipulate that the husband divorce his first wife; although this condition is disliked (makrūh) because it harms a third party, the Hanbalis consider it valid because it only gives the right to rescind, not to compel the husband. The footnote mentions a difference of opinion within the Hanbali school, with Ibn Qudāmah holding invalidity but the majority accepting validity.

Second aspect (al-nāhīyah al-thālithah): The Hanbali school permits restricting absolute ownership (al-milk al-muṭlaq) through conditions in the contract of sale. These conditions may limit the buyer's freedom to dispose of the property, such as prohibiting resale, requiring the buyer to keep the item for a specific purpose, granting the seller a right of preemption in case of resale, retaining usufruct for a period, or requiring the buyer to make waqf or emancipate a slave. Other madhhabs (including the Hanafis) reject these conditions because they oppose the inherent effects of a sale contract, which grant the buyer absolute ownership. They argue that the effects of ownership are determined by the Law, not by the parties. The Hanbali position, however, is based on the principle that the Law has given the contracting parties authority to customize the effects of their contract according to their interests, as long as no specific prohibition is violated. Therefore, ownership under such conditions is not absolute but restricted.

The text highlights the underlying legal philosophy: the Hanafi school (and others) see ownership rights as fixed by the Law and not subject to private modification, while the Hanbali school allows greater contractual freedom to tailor ownership rights to the parties' needs. This is part of a broader discussion on the authority of contractual will (sulṭān al-irādah al-ʿaqdīyah).

Key Terms
ملك مطلق (milk muṭlaq): Absolute ownership that entitles the owner to unrestricted disposition, as established by Shariʿa.
ملك مقيد (milk muqayyad): Restricted ownership where the owner's rights of disposition are limited by condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanbali school, which of the following conditions in a sale contract is permitted?
A) Seller stipulates that buyer must resell the item only to the seller's relative.
B) Seller stipulates that buyer cannot resell the item until after five years.
C) Seller stipulates that the buyer must divorce his wife.
D) Seller stipulates that the buyer must never use the item.

Key Takeaway
Hanbalis allow conditions that restrict the buyer's ownership in sale; Hanafis reject them because ownership rights are fixed by the Law.

Hanbali Classification vs. Hanafi: Conditions in Contracts
Summarizes the Hanbali classification of conditions into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and describe the four types of conditions in the Hanbali school and distinguish the Hanafi view on the binding nature of valid conditions.

Core Idea
Hanbalis set valid conditions into binding and non-binding; Hanafis hold all valid conditions as binding.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam outlines the Hanbali classification of conditions into four types. While the Hanafi school does not recognize a 'valid but non-binding' category, understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti when encountering stipulations from Hanbali-influenced contexts or when comparing the two schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source and its relevance
This passage is a summary from a modern comparative fiqh work. It presents a Hanbali qawida (maxim) about conditions. As a Hanafi mufti, you need to be aware of other schools' classifications to properly address questions that may arise from different legal traditions.
2
Extract the four types from the passage
The passage states there are four states (ahwal) of conditions in the Hanbali school. From the text, we have Type 1: valid and binding (sahih lazim); Type 2: valid but non-binding (sahih ghayr lazim). The remaining two types (void and defective) are not described here but are generally known from Hanbali sources.
The text cuts off; only two types are fully described.
3
Understand the basis for Type 1: valid and binding
Type 1 is the default rule: any condition that does not contradict the contract's purpose (muqtada al-aqd) is valid and binding. Both Hanbalis and Hanafis agree on this principle.
Hanafi example: stipulating that the sold item be of a specific quality.
4
Analyze Type 2: valid but non-binding
Type 2 applies when the condition restricts a right that the Sharia is keen to protect, such as a wife stipulating that her husband not divorce her. In Hanbali law, the condition is permissible (sahih) but not enforceable. If the husband divorces, the wife has no legal recourse. Hanafis consider such a condition void because it contradicts the nature of marriage, which allows divorce.
This is a key difference between the two schools.
5
Compare with the Hanafi classification
In Hanafi fiqh, conditions are either sahih (valid and binding) or batil (void). There is no category of sahih ghayr lazim. If a condition is contrary to the contract's essence or Sharia, it is batil. Otherwise, it is binding. This distinction shows the methodological difference: Hanbalis sometimes prioritize individual liberty in stipulations, while Hanafis emphasize the contract's integrity.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence): under Hanbali, a wife can ask for such condition (though not enforced); under Hanafi, the condition is void and the husband may divorce without consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلاصة عن أحوال الشرط في المذهب الحنبلي : ١٩/٤٢ - يتضح مما تقدَّم أن للشرط في المذهب الحنبلي أربعة أحوال تجعل منه أربعة أنواع: ۱ - شرط يصح ويَلزَم، وهو الأصل في حكم الشرط الذي لا ينافي مقتضى العقد. العاقد ۲ - وشرط يصح ولا يلزم وهو الذي يمنع من حق يحرص الشرع على بقائه كاشتراط المرأة على الرجل في عقد الزواج أن لا يطلقها
Summary of the states of conditions in the Hanbali school: 19/42 - It becomes clear from the foregoing that the condition in the Hanbali school has four states, making it four types: 1 - A condition that is valid and binding, and it is the original ruling for a condition that does not contradict the requirement of the contract. 2 - And a condition that is valid but not binding, and it is what prevents a right that the Sharia is keen to preserve, such as a woman stipulating to a man in the marriage contract that he should not divorce her.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage provides a four-part classification of conditions in Hanbali fiqh. Type 1: Valid and binding conditions that do not conflict with the contract's purpose. Type 2: Valid but non-binding conditions that restrict a Sharia-protected right, e.g., a wife's condition that her husband not divorce her—the condition is valid (permissible to ask) but not enforceable as binding; if the husband violates it, the wife does not have the right to annul the marriage. The passage then lists two more types (likely void and voidable) but the text cuts off. This classification is a qawida with furuat: the general rule that conditions are binding unless contradicting Sharia (Type 1) and exceptions for non-binding conditions that infringe inalienable rights (Type 2).

Key Terms
شرط صحيح لازم (shart sahih lazim): A condition that is valid and legally binding; it must be fulfilled
شرط صحيح غير لازم (shart sahih ghayr lazim): A condition that is valid (permissible to stipulate) but not enforceable; if violated, the other party has no recourse
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-aqd): The essential purpose and legal effect of a contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanbali classification, what is the ruling on a condition that is valid but non-binding (sahih ghayr lazim)?
A) It is void and has no effect
B) It is valid and must be fulfilled like any other condition
C) It is valid but not enforceable; the party is not legally compelled to fulfill it
D) It makes the entire contract void

Key Takeaway
Hanbalis classify conditions into four states, including a valid-but-non-binding type for restrictions on Sharia-protected rights, whereas Hanafis hold that valid conditions are always binding.

Three Types of Contractual Conditions: Ta'liq, Taqyid, Idafa
Classifying three types of contractual conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ta'liq, taqyid, and idafa and identify the legal effect of each type on a contract.

Core Idea
Conditions in contracts either suspend existence, modify effects, or defer commencement.

Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa presents a fundamental taxonomy of contractual conditions. Understanding these three categories is essential for correctly analyzing any contract clause: does it suspend the contract's formation, modify its obligations, or simply delay when its effects begin? This parsing skill is the first step in fatwa drafting for muftis.

Guided Path
1
Examine the two examples given
Read the seller's condition (use/repair) and the lessor's future start. Identify whether the contract is formed immediately or not.
The first example shows taqyid, the second shows idafa.
2
Derive the three types from the examples
Match each example to the definition provided: ta'liq (formation conditioned), taqyid (effects modified), idafa (effects delayed).
The author explicitly names them after the examples.
3
Understand ta'liq (suspension)
Focus on the definition: linking the occurrence of the contract to a future event. Contrast with tanjiz (immediate).
Example: 'If your debtor travels, I am guarantor' – the guarantee is suspended.
4
Understand taqyid (restriction)
The contract is formed immediately but obligations are added or changed. The condition does not affect formation.
Example: sale with condition of use – the sale is valid, but the seller retains benefit temporarily.
5
Understand idafa (deferment)
The contract is formed immediately, but its legal effects are postponed to a specific future time.
Example: lease starting next month – the lease contract exists now but rights begin later.

Source Text

۲ - ولو قال البائع : بعتك هذه السيارة بكذا على شرط أن استعملها شهراً قبل التسليم أو بشرط أن أقوم بتصليحها كلما طرأ عليها خلل إلى سنتين، فَقَبِل المشتري، يكون الطرفان قد اعتمدا تنجيز البيع، وإنما أرادا تعدیل آثاره بقید قیداه به يحفظ للبائع حقاً في منافع السيارة مدة شهر بعد خروجها من ملكه أو يلزمه بإصلاح ما يطرأ عليها من خلل. فقد كان مقتضى البيع لولا الشرط هو أن تنتقل ملكية السيارة ويستحق تسليمها فوراً ثم لا يكون للبائع حق في شيء منها أو في منافعها، كما لا يكون مسؤولاً عما يطرأ عليها من خلل حادث في يد المشتري.
- ولو قال المؤجر آجرتك داري هذه سنة بكذا اعتباراً من أول الشهر القادم، فَقَبِل المستأجر، يكونان قد أخراً حكم الإجارة وأضافاه إلى زمن مستقبل ولولا هذا القيد المشروط لبدأ حكمها وثبتت حقوق المستأجر من فور العقد :
·
٤٣ / ٢ - وبهذه الأمثلة الثلاثة يتضح أن الشروط التي يلقي بها العاقد على عقده قيداً من القيود يعدل به عن طريق التنجيز والإطلاق هي أنواع : ثلاثة
الأول : الشرط الذي يعلق انعقاد العقد ونحوه من التصرفات القولية، فيجعل وجوده مرتبطاً بأمر آخر.
وهذا يسمى : تعليقاً على الشرط، كما في المثال الأول.
الثاني : الشرط الذي لا يعترض سبيل الانعقاد، وإنما يقيد حكم العقد تقييداً، فيعدل آثاره الأصلية بإيجاب التزامات بين الطرفين لم تكن لتجب لو صدر العقد مطلقاً عن الشرط.
وهذا يسمى: تقييداً بالشرط كما في المثال الثاني. وقد يسمى: اقتراناً بالشرط، ويوصف العقد معه بأنه مقيد بالشرط، أو مقترن به في مقابل العقد المعلق».
الثالث: الشرط الذي ليس فيه تعليق وجود العقد، ولا تعديل مقتضياته وأحكامه الأصلية، وإنما يقصد به تأخير سريان تلك الأحكام إلى زمن مستقبل معين .
وهذا يسمى: إضافة إلى المستقبل كما في المثال الثالث .
وسنبين في المطالب التالية من البحث :
أولاً: مجمل الأنظار والاعتبارات الفقهية في كل من هذه الأنواع الثلاثة (التعليق والتقييد، والإضافة).
ثانياً : الفوارق بين مقتضيات هذه الأنواع.
ثالثاً : قابليات أنواع التصرفات والعقود لهذه الأنواع من الشروط .
المطلب الأول
مجمل الأنظار الفقهية في التعليق والتقييد والإضافة أولاً: التعليق على الشرط
٣/٤٣ - إنّ التعليق على الشرط، كما تفيده تعاريف الفقهاء، هو:
ربط حصول أمر بحصول أمر آخر .
صدوره.
فهو عكس التنجيز الذي يكون فيه العقد مطلقاً ساري الحكم منذ
فعندما يقول الإنسان لآخر مثلاً : إن سافر مدينك فأنا كفيل بما لك
2 - And if the seller says: I sold you this car for such-and-such on condition that I use it for a month before delivery, or on condition that I repair it whenever a defect occurs for two years, and the buyer accepts, both parties have relied on the contract being immediate (tanjiz), and they only intended to modify its effects by a restriction they imposed that preserves for the seller a right to the car's benefits for a month after it leaves his ownership, or obligates him to repair any defect that occurs. For without the condition, the requirement of the sale would be that ownership of the car transfers and it is due to be delivered immediately, then the seller has no right to anything in it or its benefits, nor is he responsible for any defect arising in the buyer's hands.
- And if the lessor said: I leased you this house for a year for such-and-such starting from the beginning of next month, and the lessee accepted, they have delayed the ruling of the lease and added it to a future time, and without this stipulated restriction, its ruling would have started and the lessee's rights would have been established immediately from the contract.
·
43/2 - By these three examples, it becomes clear that the conditions by which the contracting party places a restriction on his contract, modifying it from being immediate and absolute, are of three types:
First: The condition that suspends the formation of the contract and similar verbal dispositions, making its existence dependent on another matter. This is called: suspension upon a condition (ta'liq 'ala al-shart), as in the first example.
Second: The condition that does not impede formation, but rather restricts the ruling of the contract, so it modifies its original effects by imposing obligations between the parties that would not be obligatory had the contract been issued absolutely without the condition. This is called: restriction by a condition (taqyid bi al-shart), as in the second example. It may also be called: conjunction with a condition (iqtiran bi al-shart), and the contract is described as restricted by a condition or conjoined with it, as opposed to a suspended contract.
Third: The condition that does not involve suspending the existence of the contract nor modifying its requirements and original rulings, but rather intends to delay the effectiveness of those rulings to a specific future time. This is called: addition to the future (idafa ila al-mustaqbal), as in the third example.
We will explain in the following sections of the research:
First: The summary of juristic views and considerations in each of these three types (suspension, restriction, and addition).
Second: The differences between the implications of these types.
Third: The susceptibility of types of dispositions and contracts to these types of conditions.
The First Section: Summary of Juristic Views on Suspension, Restriction, and Addition.
First: Suspension upon a Condition
43/3 - Indeed, suspension upon a condition, as indicated by the definitions of the jurists, is: linking the occurrence of a matter to the occurrence of another matter. Its issuance. It is the opposite of immediate execution (tanjiz) where the contract is absolute and its ruling takes effect from the moment when a person says to another, for example: If your debtor travels, then I am a guarantor for what you have...
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work on general fiqh principles provides a taxonomy of contractual conditions. After giving two examples (a sale with a condition of use or repair, and a lease starting from a future date), the author classifies conditions into three categories. 

1) Ta'liq (suspension): The condition suspends the very existence of the contract upon a future event. The contract is not formed until the condition occurs. This is the most fundamental type, affecting the core of the contract.

2) Taqyid (restriction): The contract is formed immediately (tanjiz), but its effects are modified by additional obligations or rights. The condition does not affect formation, only the parties' obligations. This is also called iqtiran (conjunction).

3) Idafa (deferment): The contract is formed immediately, but its legal effects are postponed to a specific future time. The condition neither suspends existence nor modifies effects, but merely delays their commencement.

The passage then outlines the plan for further discussion: juristic views, differences between types, and which types of contracts can accommodate each condition. It begins the discussion of ta'liq with a definition: linking the occurrence of one matter to another, contrasting with tanjiz where the contract is immediately effective. An example is given of a guarantee conditioned on the debtor's travel.

This classification is fundamental in Hanafi contract law. For instance, a sale suspended on a future event (e.g., 'If my father returns, I sell you this car') is invalid in the view of Abu Hanifa as it introduces uncertainty (gharar) in the contract's existence, whereas a sale with a condition restricting its effects (e.g., 'I sell you this car on condition that you repaint it') is valid as long as the condition is not contrary to the contract's nature. The deferment of a lease's start is also permissible.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): ربط وجود العقد أو التصرف بوجود أمر آخر مستقبل
التقييد (al-taqyid): تعديل آثار العقد الأصلية بإضافة التزامات أو قيود دون تعليق وجوده
الإضافة إلى المستقبل (al-idafa ila al-mustaqbal): تأخير سريان أحكام العقد إلى زمن مستقبل معين مع انعقاده فورا
التنجيز (al-tanjiz): كون العقد مطلقا غير معلق ولا مضاف، ساري الحكم فورا
الشرط (al-shart): قيد يضعه العاقد على عقده يعدل به عن التنجيز والإطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the classification in this passage, what type of condition is illustrated by a sale where the seller stipulates he will use the car for a month after sale?
A) Ta'liq (suspension)
B) Taqyid (restriction)
C) Idafa (deferment)
D) Tanjiz (immediate)

Key Takeaway
Contractual conditions are classified into ta'liq (suspension of existence), taqyid (modification of effects), and idafa (deferment of effects), each with distinct legal consequences.

Distinction Between Suspensive and Restrictive Conditions in Contracts
Analyzing two types of contractual conditions: ta'liq and taqyid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a suspensive condition (ta'liq) and a restrictive condition (taqyid) in a contract and identify the correct legal effect of each.

Core Idea
A suspension delays the contract's effect; a restriction adds an obligation to an already effective contract.

Contracts often include conditions that modify their operation. Understanding whether a condition suspends the contract's existence or merely adds an extra obligation is crucial for issuing correct fatwas. This lesson, drawn from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal, clarifies the conceptual and practical differences between ta'liq and taqyid, two key terms in Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of condition
Determine if the condition is suspending the contract's effect (ta'liq) or adding an obligation within an already existing contract (taqyid). Look for phrases like 'if' for ta'liq and 'on condition that' for taqyid.
A suspensive condition makes the contract contingent on a future event. A restrictive condition assumes the contract is already in effect but modifies its terms.
2
Analyze the formulation
Examine the wording. Ta'liq often uses 'in' or 'idha' (if/when) and the contract is not yet binding. Taqyid uses 'ala an' (on condition that) and the contract is immediately binding.
Example: 'If you pay, I sell' vs 'I sell on condition you pay in installments'.
3
Apply the governing maxim
For ta'liq, apply the maxim: 'What is suspended on a condition becomes established upon the establishment of the condition.' For taqyid, the contract is already effective and the condition is an additional commitment.
The maxim is from al-Majallah article 82.
4
Determine the legal effect
For ta'liq, the obligation only arises when the condition is fulfilled. For taqyid, the obligation exists immediately but is qualified by the condition.
In ta'liq, the condition is a cause (sabab) for the obligation; in taqyid, it is a modifier.
5
Check for impossibility
If the condition in ta'liq is impossible, the statement is void and considered an exaggeration. For taqyid, an impossible condition may render the condition void but the contract remains if the condition is not essential.
The passage only discusses impossibility for ta'liq, not taqyid.

Source Text

الفصل (٤٣) أنواع الشروط العقدية

٧/٣ - وأما التقييد بالشرط أو الاقتران به، فهو التزام في التصرف القولي لا يستلزمه ذلك التصرف في حالة إطلاقه.
وذلك كما لو باع الإنسان بضاعةً على شرط أن تكون محمولة على حسابه إلى محل المشتري فالبائع هنا قد التزم بالشرط في ضمن عقده وجيبة حمل المبيع إلى محل المشتري. وهذا الالتزام لم يكن ليقتضيه البيع المطلق، أي الخالي عن الشرط، لأنَّ البيع المطلق إنما يوجب مجرد انتقال الملكية بعوض، ثم إنَّ المالك الجديد - المشتري - هو المكلف بنقل مشتراه على حسابه

فالمقيد هنا هو حكم العقد المنشأ، والقيد هو الشرط الذي التزم العاقد ما التزم علاوة على الحكم الأصلي للعقد.
ويلحظ هنا أن العقد في حالة التقييد هو مُنْجَزِّ مُبرم ليس معلقاً وجوده على شيء، لأن معنى التقييد يشعر بوجود الأمر المقيد

(۲)
ففي المثال المذكور قد التزم البائع أمراً لم يقع، وهو حمل المبيع على حسابه وإن التزامه هذا حصل في ضمن أمر قد وجد وانبرم، وهو عقد البيع. وهذا التعريف يقابل قولهم في تعريف الشرط التعليقي بأنه : ترتيب أمر لم يوجد على أمر لم يوجد كما تقدم (ف ٣/٤٣ الحاشية).

Chapter (43): Types of Contractual Conditions
If the condition is already existing at the time of suspension, the statement is a direct execution in the form of suspension, such as someone saying: 'If you are alive, I have sold you this thing.'
And if the condition is impossible to occur, the suspension is void, i.e., an exaggeration in expressing refusal and lack of intention, such as his saying: 'If your debtor comes back to life after his death, I am his guarantor.' (Ref: al-Ashbah by Ibn Nujaym, third art, 2/224).
6/43 - And this: Suspension necessitates the occurrence of the suspended matter upon the realization of the condition on which it is suspended, and it also necessitates its continued non-existence as long as the condition is absent. That is, the conditioned thing is linked to the condition in existence and non-existence, as the condition becomes like a cause for the conditioned thing - by the will of the actor - after there was no connection between them.
And on this basis, the maxim was formulated: 'What is suspended on a condition must be established upon the establishment of the condition' (al-Majallah, article 82).
If the creditor says to the debtor: 'If you pay me half my debt today, I have released you from the remainder,' or if he says to another: 'If the judge rules in my favor against my debtor so-and-so, I have appointed you as agent to collect the debt from him,' then this suspension necessitates that the debtor is not released from the debt before paying the stipulated amount at the specified time, and that the agency to collect the debt is not established before the judge's ruling to that effect; because the speaker who has the right to dispose has stipulated the payment or the ruling, and made it the criterion for achieving the release from the remainder of the debt or for the appointment. Also, the condition on which the suspension is made is a conventional condition (shart ja'li), as opposed to the 'legal condition' (shart shar'i) as previously mentioned in its appropriate context (ref: 6/28).
Second: Restriction by a Condition
7/3 - As for restriction by a condition (taqyid) or conjunction with it, it is a commitment in a verbal disposition that is not necessitated by that disposition when it is absolute.
For example, if a person sells goods on the condition that they be transported at his expense to the buyer's place, the seller here has committed himself to the condition within his contract, undertaking the transport of the sold item to the buyer's place. This commitment was not necessitated by the absolute sale, i.e., the sale free from condition, because the absolute sale only necessitates the mere transfer of ownership for a consideration; then the new owner - the buyer - is the one obliged to transport his purchase at his own expense.
Similarly, if someone appoints an agent to purchase something on the condition that the price be paid in installments, specifying their number and periods. The agent's agency is restricted by this condition, so he becomes obligated not to purchase for his principal except according to the installments specified. If he violates this, the purchase falls for himself and his principal is not bound by it. If the agency were absolute, the agent would have the authority to purchase for the principal in cash or installments.
And so on for every example of this kind.
Thus, the restricted thing here is the ruling of the concluded contract, and the restriction is the condition that the contracting party has committed beyond the original ruling of the contract.
It is noted here that the contract in the case of restriction is effective and concluded, not suspended in its existence on anything, because the meaning of restriction implies the existence of the thing restricted.
And restriction is usually formulated with the phrase 'on condition that' or 'on the condition that' or...
(1) The jurists define a restrictive condition as: undertaking a matter that does not exist in a matter that already exists (ref: al-Hashiya al-Hamawi on al-Ashbah, art 3, 2/224).
(2) In the mentioned example, the seller undertook a matter that did not occur, which is transporting the sold item at his expense, and this undertaking of his occurred within a matter that already existed and was concluded, which is the contract of sale. This definition corresponds to their definition of a suspensive condition as: arranging a matter that does not exist upon a matter that does not exist, as previously mentioned (ref: 3/43, footnote).
The definition we have placed here is more accurate in depicting the reality of the restrictive condition and clearer in form. Hence, we prefer to name this type 'taqyid' as is the Hanafi terminology, not 'iqtiran' as some name it (ref: 243), because 'iqtiran' includes suspension and addition as well, so it does not indicate the specific characteristic that distinguishes this type.
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses two fundamental types of conditions in contracts: ta'liq (suspensive condition) and taqyid (restrictive condition). The author begins by explaining ta'liq: if the condition is already present, the statement is considered immediate execution, not suspension. If the condition is impossible, the statement is void—a mere emphasis on refusal. The key maxim for ta'liq is that the conditioned matter becomes established only when the condition is fulfilled. Two examples illustrate this: a creditor forgiving debt upon partial payment, and appointing an agent upon a court judgment. In both, the effect does not occur before the condition. Ta'liq is a conventional (ja'li) condition, as opposed to a legal (shar'i) condition. The second type, taqyid, is a commitment added to a disposition that is not required by the absolute form of that disposition. For example, in a sale where the seller agrees to transport the goods, or in an agency where the agent is restricted to installment purchases. These conditions are additional obligations that do not suspend the contract but modify its content. The author emphasizes that in taqyid, the contract is immediately effective and concluded. He also prefers the term taqyid over iqtiran to distinguish it clearly from ta'liq. The passage provides a clear conceptual framework for understanding contractual conditions in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension: making the existence of a contractual effect contingent upon a future condition.
تقييد (taqyid): Restriction: an additional commitment in a verbal disposition that is not necessitated by its absolute form.
شرط جعلي (shart ja'li): Conventional condition: a condition created by the parties' agreement, as opposed to a legal condition imposed by shari'ah.
شرط شرعي (shart shar'i): Legal condition: a condition required by shari'ah for the validity of an act, e.g., capacity in contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The guarantee is valid upon resurrection.
B) The statement is void and considered an exaggeration of refusal.
C) The guarantee is suspended until the impossible occurs.
D) The statement is a valid ta'liq because it uses 'if'.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction: ta'liq suspends the contract's existence until a condition is met; taqyid adds an obligation to an immediately effective contract.

Hanafi Classification of Contracts by Condition Capacity
Introducing the eight-category Hanafi classification and the first two categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first two categories in the Hanafi eight-fold classification of contracts by their capacity for ta'liq, taqyid, and idafa.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school classifies contracts into eight categories based on their inherent capacity to accept conditions.

The Hanafi jurists do not treat all contracts equally when it comes to conditions. This lesson introduces their classification system, which serves as a key tool for a mufti in determining whether a condition in a contract is valid. The source passage presents the first two categories: financial commutative contracts and marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of conditions
Review ta'liq (suspension), taqyid (restriction), and idafa (annexation). These are the three contractual conditions discussed in the text.
2
Recognize the Hanafi classification framework
Understand that the Hanafi school divides all contracts and verbal dispositions into eight categories based on their essence and conditional capacity.
The Hanafi school takes a middle ground between broader (Hanbali) and narrower (other) schools.
3
Note the first category: financial commutative contracts
These include sale, partition, compromise for money, and lease. According to the text, they accept all three types of conditions.
This category is considered the most capable.
4
Note the second category: marriage
Marriage is described as a non-financial commutative contract, but the author recommends treating it as an independent type for classification purposes.
Its conditional capacity may differ from pure financial contracts.
5
Understand the significance for ifta
A mufti must first identify the contract category before ruling on the validity of a condition. The classification provides the default capacity.
The remaining six categories (not listed here) further guide the mufti.

المطلب الثالث: قابليات أنواع العقود لأنواع الشروط
١٢/٤٣ - ليست جميع أنواع العقود والتصرفات القولية في نظر جمهور الفقهاء قابلة لكل نوع من هذه الشروط العقدية الثلاثة : التعليق والتقييد، والإضافة. فمن العقود والتصرفات القولية ما يقبل هذه الشروط جميعاً، فيصح تعليقه وتقييده وإضافته ؛ ومنها ما يقبل بعضها دون بعض . والمذاهب الاجتهادية على اختلاف في هذه القابليات؛ وإنَّ أوسعها هو الاجتهاد الحنبلي كما تقدم.
وسنوجز فيما يلي قواعد الاجتهاد الحنفي في ذلك، وهو حد وسط بالنسبة إلى بقية الاجتهادات .
١٣/٤٣ - فنظر الاجتهاد الحنفي في قابلية العقود للشروط يمكن تلخيصه في القواعد التالية :
يقسم فقهاء الحنفية العقود وسائر التصرفات القولية الإنشائية في هذا المقام بحسب ماهيتها وقابلياتها الشرطية إلى الأقسام الثمانية التالية :
١ - المعاوضات المالية كالبيع والقسمة والصلح عن مال بمال، والإجارة .
٢ - النكاح ويصفونه بأنه : معاوضة غير مالية. والأفضل أن يعد نوعاً مستقلاً.
Subsection 3: Capacities of Types of Contracts for Types of Conditions. 12/43 - Not all types of contracts and verbal dispositions, in the view of the majority of jurists, are capable of each of these three contractual conditions: suspension (ta'liq), restriction (taqyid), and annexation (idafa). Some contracts and verbal dispositions accept all these conditions, so their suspension, restriction, and annexation are valid; and some accept some but not others. The jurisprudential schools differ regarding these capacities; and the broadest of them is the Hanbali jurisprudence, as previously mentioned. We will summarize below the rules of Hanafi jurisprudence in this regard, which is a middle ground relative to the other jurisprudences. 13/43 - So the view of Hanafi jurisprudence on the capacity of contracts for conditions can be summarized in the following rules: The Hanafi jurists divide contracts and other verbal constitutive dispositions in this context, according to their essence and their conditional capacities, into the following eight categories: 1. Financial commutative contracts, such as sale, partition, compromise for money, and lease. 2. Marriage, which they describe as a non-financial commutative contract. It is better to consider it an independent type.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage introduces the third subsection of the chapter, dealing with the capacity of different contracts to accept the three types of conditions: ta'liq (suspension), taqyid (restriction), and idafa (annexation). The author notes that not all contracts are equally capable; some accept all three, some only some. The schools differ, with the Hanbali school being the broadest. The Hanafi school takes a middle position. The author then states that the Hanafi jurists divide contracts into eight categories based on their essence and conditional capacity. Only the first two categories are listed here: (1) financial commutative contracts like sale, partition, money-based compromise, and lease; (2) marriage, which is a non-financial commutative contract but better treated as an independent type. The remaining six categories presumably appear on subsequent pages.

Key Terms
قابليات (qabiliyat): صلاحية العقد لأن يقبل نوعاً من الشروط العقدية
التعليق (al-ta'liq): تعليق العقد على حدوث أمر مستقبل غير محقق
التقييد (al-taqyid): تحديد العقد بشرط معين
الإضافة (al-idafa): إسناد العقد إلى زمن مستقبل

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of contracts by their capacity for conditions is an essential tool for a mufti, providing default rules for the validity of ta'liq, taqyid, and idafa.

Types of Conditions and Hanafi Contract Classification
Presenting the eight-fold classification of contracts and the three condition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of contractual conditions (ta'liq, taqyid, idafa) and list the eight categories of contracts according to the Hanafi school, explaining their relative capacity.

Core Idea
Contracts are classified into eight types based on their essence and capacity for conditions.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa introduces the Hanafi taxonomy of contracts in relation to conditions. As a mufti, understanding this classification is essential for determining the validity of conditional contracts. The Hanafi school takes a middle position among the schools, with the Hanbalis being the broadest. We will focus on the three condition types and the eight contract categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of conditions
Define each condition type: ta'liq (suspension on a future event), idafa (postponement to a future time), and taqyid (restriction/qualification). Understand their general acceptability across schools.
Ta'liq and idafa are more strictly regulated than taqyid.
2
List the eight Hanafi contract categories
Go through each of the eight categories listed in the passage: financial exchanges, marriage, gratuitous transfers, permissions, appointments, restrictions, commitments, and pure relinquishments. Note their key features.
These categories are based on the essence (mahiyya) of each contract.
3
Note which categories accept which condition types
From the passage, observe that some contracts (like permissions and commitments) accept all three conditions, while others (like financial exchanges) do not accept ta'liq or idafa. This will be detailed in the next lesson.
Taqyid (restriction) is generally accepted in all categories.
4
Reflect on the Hanafi middle position
Compare with the Hanbali school, which is more permissive. Understand that this classification is a key tool for muftis to evaluate conditional contracts.
The Hanafi position is considered moderate relative to other schools.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
استناد إلى تاريخ التعليق أي أثر رجعي للزم ذلك تقدم العقد المشروط على شرطه وهو خلاف المعقول.

بضاعتي
فلو علق إنسان الوكالة مثلاً بقوله لشخص آخر تسلمت متي الفلانية فقد وكلتك ببيعها فباعها المخاطب قبل تسليمها ثم تسلمها ربها لا ينفذ عليه البيع، لأنَّ وكالة ذلك الشخص إنما تثبت منذ تسلّم البضاعة، فقبل ذلك يكون فضولياً في بيعه .

المطلب الثالث: قابليات أنواع العقود لأنواع الشروط ٤٣ / ١٢ - ليست جميع أنواع العقود والتصرفات القولية في نظر جمهور الفقهاء قابلة لكل نوع من هذه الشروط العقدية الثلاثة : التعليق والتقييد، والإضافة. فمن العقود والتصرفات القولية ما يقبل هذه الشروط جميعاً، فيصح تعليقه وتقييده وإضافته ؛ ومنها ما يقبل بعضها دون بعض . والمذاهب الاجتهادية على اختلاف في هذه القابليات؛ وإنَّ أوسعها هو الاجتهاد الحنبلي كما تقدم.
وسنوجز فيما يلي قواعد الاجتهاد الحنفي في ذلك، وهو حد وسط بالنسبة إلى بقية الاجتهادات .
١٣/٤٣ - فنظر الاجتهاد الحنفي في قابلية العقود للشروط يمكن تلخيصه في القواعد التالية :
يقسم فقهاء الحنفية العقود وسائر التصرفات القولية الإنشائية في هذا المقام بحسب ماهيتها وقابلياتها الشرطية إلى الأقسام الثمانية التالية : ١ - المعاوضات المالية كالبيع والقسمة والصلح عن مال بمال، والإجارة .
٢ - النكاح ويصفونه بأنه : معاوضة غير مالية. والأفضل أن يعد نوعاً مستقلاً.
٣ - التبرعات: أي التمليك بلا عوض كالهبة والصدقة، والوصية والوقف ويدخل فيها الإبراء عن الدين لمعنى التبرع فيه، وإن كان في صورة إسقاط . فهو إسقاط غير محض، بل فيه معنى التمليك.
٤ - الإطلاقات كالوكالة، والإذن بالتجارة للصغير المميز (۱)، من قبل وليه فإن فيهما إطلاق سلطة تصرفية للوكيل والصغير كانا محجورين عنها.
٥ - الولايات: كتعيين الحكام والعمال وسائر الموظفين من فروع السلطة الإدارية .
٦ - التقييدات كعزل الوكيل والموظف، وحجر المأذون (٢) .
۷ - الالتزامات: كالكفالة بأنواعها .
- الإسقاطات المحضة كالطلاق ،والإعتاق وإسقاط الشفعة .
١٤/٤٣ - وبناءً على ذلك يقررون في قابلية هذه العقود والتصرفات للشروط التفصيل التالي :
١ - المعاوضات المالية ،والتبرعات ،والنكاح لا تقبل التعليق ولا الإضافة مطلقاً، بل يجب أن تعقد منجزة. فإذا علقت على شرط أو أضيفت إلى زمن مستقبل بطلت حتى لو وجد الشرط المعلق عليه، أو جاء الزمن المضاف إليه، لا ينعقد العقد.
فلو قال لآخر: إذا ولدت فرسي هذه فقد بعتكها بكذا» أو وهبتكها ، أو قال لامرأة بحضرة شهود تزوجتك اعتباراً من أول الشهر القادم، وتم القبول، لا تصبح الفرس مبيعة أو موهوبة إذا ولدت ولا المرأة زوجة إذا حلَّ الشهر المضاف إليه .
ولكن يستثنى من ذلك الوصية والإجارة، والإعارة

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh

Based on the date of suspension (ta'liq) i.e. retroactive effect, this would require the conditioned contract to precede its condition, which is contrary to reason.

My goods:
So if someone suspends agency, for example, by saying to another person: "You have taken delivery of such-and-such goods, so I have appointed you as agent to sell them," and the addressee sells them before delivery, then the owner later takes delivery, the sale is not binding on him, because the agency of that person is only established from the time of taking delivery of the goods; before that he is a meddler (fuduli) in his sale.

Third Demand: Capacities of Types of Contracts for Types of Conditions 43/12 - Not all types of contracts and verbal dispositions, in the view of the majority of jurists, are capable of each of these three contractual conditions: suspension (ta'liq), restriction (taqyid), and postponement (idafa). Some contracts and verbal dispositions accept all these conditions, so their suspension, restriction, and postponement are valid; and some accept some but not others. The schools of ijtihad differ in these capacities; and the broadest of them is the Hanbali ijtihad, as previously mentioned.
We will summarize below the rules of Hanafi ijtihad in this regard, and it is a middle ground relative to the other ijtihads.

43/13 - The view of Hanafi ijtihad regarding the capacity of contracts for conditions can be summarized in the following rules:
Hanafi jurists divide contracts and all other constructive verbal dispositions, in this context, according to their essence and conditional capacities into the following eight categories:
1. Financial exchanges (mu'awadat maliyya) such as sale, division, compromise for property, and lease.
2. Marriage (nikah), which they describe as a non-financial exchange. It is better to count it as an independent type.
3. Gratuitous transfers (tabarru'at): i.e., ownership without consideration, such as gift, charity, bequest, and endowment (waqf); included in this is remission of debt (ibra') due to the meaning of gratuitousness in it, even though it is in the form of a relinquishment (isqat). So it is a non-pure relinquishment, but contains the meaning of transfer of ownership.
4. Permissions (itlaqat) such as agency (wakala) and permission to trade for a discerning minor (1) from his guardian, for in both there is a release of dispositive authority for the agent and the minor, who were originally restricted from it.
5. Appointments (wilayat) such as appointing judges, officials, and all employees from the branches of administrative authority.
6. Restrictions (taqyidat) such as removing an agent or employee, and placing a ward under interdiction (hajr al-ma'dhun) (2).
7. Commitments (iltizamat) such as suretyship (kafala) of all types.
8. Pure relinquishments (isqatat mahda) such as divorce, manumission, and relinquishment of pre-emption (shuf'a).

43/14 - Based on this, they decide regarding the capacity of these contracts and dispositions for conditions the following detail:
1. Financial exchanges, gratuitous transfers, and marriage do not accept suspension or postponement at all; rather, they must be concluded immediately (munajjaza). If they are suspended on a condition or postponed to a future time, they become invalid; even if the condition occurs or the time arrives, the contract does not take effect.
So if someone says to another: "If this mare of mine gives birth, I have sold her to you for such-and-such" or "I have given her to you as a gift," or he says to a woman in the presence of witnesses: "I marry you effective from the beginning of next month," and acceptance is completed, the mare does not become sold or gifted when she gives birth, nor does the woman become a wife when the postponed month arrives.
But from this are excepted: bequest (wasiyya), lease (ijara), and loan for use (i'ara).

(1)(2) This minor is then called ma'dhun (permitted). It is permissible for his guardian who permitted him to place him under interdiction after the permission. The details of the rulings of the ma'dhun and his interdiction will come in the theory of legal capacity and guardianship (F 11/62-17).
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule from Hanafi jurisprudence regarding the capacity of different types of contracts and verbal dispositions to accept three kinds of conditions: ta'liq (suspension on a future event), taqyid (restriction/qualification), and idafa (postponement to a future time). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that the Hanafi school takes a middle position among the schools, with the Hanbalis being the most permissive. The Hanafis classify all contracts and constructive verbal acts into eight categories based on their essence. The key ruling is that financial exchanges, gratuitous transfers, and marriage do not accept suspension or postponement at all; they must be concluded immediately. If they are suspended or postponed, they are void, even if the condition later occurs. The exceptions are bequest (wasiyya), lease (ijara), and loan for use (i'ara), which are discussed in detail elsewhere. The passage also distinguishes between pure relinquishments (isqatat mahda) like divorce and manumission, which are treated differently. The examples given illustrate the invalidity of suspended sales and marriages. This is a fundamental rule for muftis determining the validity of conditional contracts.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): تعليق العقد على شرط مستقبل بحيث لا ينعقد إلا عند وجود ذلك الشرط
الإضافة (al-idafa): إضافة العقد إلى زمن مستقبل بحيث لا ينعقد إلا عند حلول ذلك الزمن
التقييد (al-taqyid): تقييد العقد بوصف أو شرط يصحبه
المعاوضات المالية (al-mu'awadat al-maliyya): العقود التي تقوم على مبادلة مال بمال كالبيع والإجارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the eight categories of contracts according to the Hanafi classification in the passage?
A) Financial exchanges (mu'awadat maliyya)
B) Permissions (itlaqat)
C) Mutual insurance (takaful)
D) Commitments (iltizamat)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies contracts into eight categories based on their essence, and this classification determines which contracts can accept conditions like suspension, postponement, or restriction.

The Dual Nature of Iqalah (Mutual Rescission) in Hanafi Fiqh
Exposition of iqālah's dual legal nature with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on iqalah as having two considerations (pure dissolution between parties and new contract for third parties) and apply it to cases involving pre-emption and hidden defects.

Core Idea
Iqalah dissolves the contract between the parties but creates a new purchase for third-party rights.

In Hanafi fiqh, mutual rescission (iqālah) is more than a simple cancellation. It protects third-party rights by treating the rescission as a new contract in their regard. This lesson explores the dual legal nature of iqālah and its practical consequences, such as re-triggering the right of pre-emption and barring claims for pre-existing defects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dual nature
Read the passage: iqālah is considered pure dissolution between the two contracting parties, restoring them to pre-contract status; but regarding third parties, it is treated as a new contract to protect their acquired rights.
The reason given is to prevent collusion between the parties that would harm third-party rights.
2
Apply to the pre-emption (shuf'a) case
If a pre-emptor's right attached to a sale, and then the seller and buyer rescind, the seller is considered a new buyer, so the pre-emptor gets a new opportunity to exercise his right.
This is the thamarah (practical consequence) of the dual nature.
3
Apply to the hidden defect case
If a buyer sells the item, then rescinds with the second buyer and recovers it, he cannot return the item to the original seller due to a pre-existing defect, because the rescission is a new purchase that cleanses previous rights.
The change of cause of ownership (sabab al-milk) is likened to a change in the thing itself.
4
Identify the exception
Every contract except marriage (nikah) can be rescinded by mutual consent (iqālah).
Marriage is excluded because it is not purely financial and has unique rules.

ويقابله العقد الفوري وهو الذي لا يحتاج تنفيذه إلى زمن ممتد يشغله باستمرار، بل يتم تنفيذه دفعة واحدة كالبيع والصلح.
٥۹۵
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and What Pertains to It in Fiqh. This rescission (iqālah) has two considerations in fiqhi view: - It is considered, with respect to the two contracting parties who mutually rescind, a pure dissolution by which the rescinded contract is dissolved, returning the two contracting parties to their original state before the contract. And it is considered, with respect to non-contracting parties (the third party), as a new contract, protecting their acquired rights from the collusion of the two contracting parties to demolish them through rescission. So real estate that was sold and in which the right of pre-emption (shuf‘a) was established for the pre-emptor, if the seller and buyer mutually rescind the sale and ownership is returned to the seller, that seller is considered a new purchaser by rescission, so a new right of pre-emption is established for the pre-emptor. Likewise, if the buyer sold the sold item and then mutually rescinded with the second buyer and recovered the sold item, and then discovered a pre-existing defect in it, he does not have the right to return it to the first seller due to the defect, because the rescission is like a new purchase with respect to the first seller. And it is established in the rulings of concealment of defect that the sold item, after leaving the ownership of its purchaser, if it returns to him by a new purchase, he does not return it due to the old defect to its seller, because the change of the cause of ownership is considered like the change of the very thing owned, thus necessitating the cleansing of the thing from previous rights. (See: Rad al-Muhtar 12/76 and al-Majalla 98). And this is a rule on which modern legal theory agreed with Islamic fiqh. Every contract accepts rescission except the marriage contract. This, and rescission has legal conditions detailed in its chapter in fiqh books (1) B – As for contracts non-binding with respect to both parties, like partnership and agency, they are dissolved by the will of each of them as long as a right of a third party is not attached to their continuance, as previously explained in section 6/40. (1) Jurists often mention rescission in a subsidiary chapter of the book of sales because most rescission occurs in sales, but it is appropriate to devote an independent book to it after contracts, because as we have shown it can occur in every contract except marriage. (Chapter 45: Dissolution of the Contract) C – And the contract binding with respect to one party without the other, like pledge, is dissolved by the will of the one for whom it is not binding, namely the creditor-payee. And the same is said regarding contracts originally binding with respect to both parties, if defects of consent occur that strip away the quality of bindingness with respect to one party, as in the option of defect and other options previously explained; they are dissolved by the will of the party whose will is affected and whose consent is defective (1). (See: section 40/6 – 40/9). D – And solitary will acts, if non-binding, are dissolved by the will of their originator, like a bequest for a charitable purpose; it is dissolved by the will of the testator himself. (3/45 – Secondly: Automatic Termination (Infisākh): (2) Every contract terminates automatically if its performance becomes impossible; and the continuing contract terminates if what its continuance depends upon is lost: (3) A – The sale terminates by the destruction of the sold item before delivery, due to the impossibility of performing the contract by delivery after the destruction of its subject matter. As for the death of the seller before delivery, the sale does not terminate thereby; rather, the heir becomes… (1) Legal scholars distinguish in naming the dissolution of the contract between two situations: A – A situation of defects of consent at the formation of the contract. Here they call removal of the contract by the will of the one whose consent is defective: annulment (ibṭāl), and they describe the contract in this situation as voidable. B – A situation of an emergent cause related to the performance of the contract, not its formation. Here they call the removal dissolution (faskh) or automatic termination (infisākh), according to circumstances. This is a good terminology. (2) As for binding ones like endowment and divorce, they are not dissolved by dissolution (faskh), but are subject to rules of nullity. (3) We will see in the discussion of classification of contracts type (11) that the continuing contract is one whose performance extends over a period of time, like lease and loan for use; the enjoyment of the contracted benefits in them is gradual, so the continuing contract is like a series of renewing contracts. Its opposite is the instantaneous contract, which does not require extended time for its performance, but is executed at once, like sale and compromise.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage is a systematic treatment of how contracts dissolve in Hanafi fiqh, drawn from al-Zarqā's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhī al-ʿĀmm. The author begins with the key mode of mutual rescission (iqālah). He explains that iqālah has a dual legal nature: between the original parties it is a pure dissolution, restoring them to pre-contract status; but regarding third parties, it is treated as a new contract. This protects third-party rights like pre-emption (shufʿa). For example, if a pre-emptor's right attached to a sale, and then the seller and buyer rescind, the seller is considered a new buyer, so the pre-emptor gets a new chance to exercise his right. Similarly, if a buyer sells the item, then rescinds with the sub-buyer and gets it back, he cannot claim a pre-existing defect against the first seller because the rescission is a new purchase. The reason given is that a change in the cause of ownership is like a change in the article itself—previous rights are cleansed. The author notes that modern legal theory agrees with this principle. The rule is that every contract can be rescinded except marriage. The conditions for iqālah are detailed in fiqh works. Next, the author categorizes contracts by bindingness: (A) Non-binding contracts like partnership and agency can be dissolved by either party, unless a third party's right is involved. (B) Contracts binding on one side only, like a pledge (rahn), can be dissolved by the party not bound (the creditor). (C) Contracts originally binding on both sides can become non-binding for one party due to defects of consent (e.g., option of defect), allowing that party alone to dissolve. (D) Non-binding solitary acts of will, like a charitable bequest, can be revoked by the testator. Finally, the author discusses automatic termination (infisākh) due to impossibility of performance. A sale ends if the goods are destroyed before delivery; but the seller's death does not terminate the sale—the heir steps in. The author also clarifies terminology: Western jurists distinguish between voidable (for defects at formation) and dissolution or automatic termination (for subsequent causes). In Hanafi fiqh, binding dispositions like waqf or irrevocable divorce are not simply dissolved but follow rules of nullity. This passage is foundational for understanding how contracts end in Hanafi law, and it reflects the school's careful balancing of party autonomy, third-party rights, and legal certainty.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): فسخ العقد بالتراض بين الطرفين، ويعتبر فسخاً بينهما وعقداً جديداً بالنسبة للغير
الشفعة (al-shufʿah): حق الأولوية للشريك أو الجار في تملك العقار المبيع بدفع الثمن
تبدل سبب الملك (tabaddul sabab al-milk): تغير الأساس القانوني الذي صار به الشيء ملكاً للشخص، ويترتب عليه اعتبار الشيء كالجديد في بعض الأحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of iqālah on a pre-emptor's right?
A) It extinguishes the pre-emptor's right because the original sale is cancelled.
B) It creates a new right of pre-emption because the seller is considered a new buyer.
C) It has no effect; pre-emption rights are only created at the original sale.
D) It depends on whether the pre-emptor consents to the rescission.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, iqālah has a dual legal nature: it dissolves the contract between the parties but acts as a new contract vis-à-vis third parties, protecting their acquired rights.

Dissolution of Non-Binding Contracts and Automatic Termination in Hanafi Law
Categorization of contracts by bindingness and rules of dissolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize contracts by bindingness and determine which party can dissolve each type, and identify when a contract terminates automatically (infisākh).

Core Idea
A contract's bindingness determines who can dissolve it; impossibility of performance causes automatic termination.

Contracts in Hanafi law vary in bindingness. Some are non-binding for both parties (like partnership), others binding on one side only (like pledge), and contracts originally binding can become non-binding due to defects of consent. This lesson also covers automatic termination (infisākh) when performance becomes impossible, and distinguishes it from dissolution by will.

Guided Path
1
Identify categories of bindingness
Read the passage: contracts non-binding on both sides (partnership, agency), contracts binding on one side (pledge), and contracts originally binding on both that become non-binding for one party due to defects of consent.
Also solitary acts of will (e.g., bequest) can be revoked if non-binding.
2
Apply dissolution rules to each category
Non-binding contracts are dissolved unilaterally by either party. Contracts binding on one side are dissolved by the party not bound (e.g., creditor in rahn). Contracts with defects of consent can be dissolved by the party whose consent was defective.
Third-party rights may prevent dissolution when attached to the contract's continuance.
3
Understand automatic termination (infisākh)
Infisākh occurs automatically without need for will if performance becomes impossible (e.g., destruction of subject matter). Continuing contracts terminate if the basis of their continuance is lost.
Distinguish from dissolution by will: infisākh is not a party's act.
4
Differentiate from related concepts
The passage notes that legal scholars distinguish between voidability (for defects at formation) and dissolution/automatic termination (for subsequent events). In Hanafi fiqh, binding dispositions like waqf or irrevocable divorce are not dissolved but governed by rules of nullity.
This aligns with modern legal terminology but is rooted in classical fiqh.

٥۹۵
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and What Pertains to It in Fiqh. This rescission (iqālah) has two considerations in fiqhi view: - It is considered, with respect to the two contracting parties who mutually rescind, a pure dissolution by which the rescinded contract is dissolved, returning the two contracting parties to their original state before the contract. And it is considered, with respect to non-contracting parties (the third party), as a new contract, protecting their acquired rights from the collusion of the two contracting parties to demolish them through rescission. So real estate that was sold and in which the right of pre-emption (shuf‘a) was established for the pre-emptor, if the seller and buyer mutually rescind the sale and ownership is returned to the seller, that seller is considered a new purchaser by rescission, so a new right of pre-emption is established for the pre-emptor. Likewise, if the buyer sold the sold item and then mutually rescinded with the second buyer and recovered the sold item, and then discovered a pre-existing defect in it, he does not have the right to return it to the first seller due to the defect, because the rescission is like a new purchase with respect to the first seller. And it is established in the rulings of concealment of defect that the sold item, after leaving the ownership of its purchaser, if it returns to him by a new purchase, he does not return it due to the old defect to its seller, because the change of the cause of ownership is considered like the change of the very thing owned, thus necessitating the cleansing of the thing from previous rights. (See: Rad al-Muhtar 12/76 and al-Majalla 98). And this is a rule on which modern legal theory agreed with Islamic fiqh. Every contract accepts rescission except the marriage contract. This, and rescission has legal conditions detailed in its chapter in fiqh books (1) B – As for contracts non-binding with respect to both parties, like partnership and agency, they are dissolved by the will of each of them as long as a right of a third party is not attached to their continuance, as previously explained in section 6/40. (1) Jurists often mention rescission in a subsidiary chapter of the book of sales because most rescission occurs in sales, but it is appropriate to devote an independent book to it after contracts, because as we have shown it can occur in every contract except marriage. (Chapter 45: Dissolution of the Contract) C – And the contract binding with respect to one party without the other, like pledge, is dissolved by the will of the one for whom it is not binding, namely the creditor-payee. And the same is said regarding contracts originally binding with respect to both parties, if defects of consent occur that strip away the quality of bindingness with respect to one party, as in the option of defect and other options previously explained; they are dissolved by the will of the party whose will is affected and whose consent is defective (1). (See: section 40/6 – 40/9). D – And solitary will acts, if non-binding, are dissolved by the will of their originator, like a bequest for a charitable purpose; it is dissolved by the will of the testator himself. (3/45 – Secondly: Automatic Termination (Infisākh): (2) Every contract terminates automatically if its performance becomes impossible; and the continuing contract terminates if what its continuance depends upon is lost: (3) A – The sale terminates by the destruction of the sold item before delivery, due to the impossibility of performing the contract by delivery after the destruction of its subject matter. As for the death of the seller before delivery, the sale does not terminate thereby; rather, the heir becomes… (1) Legal scholars distinguish in naming the dissolution of the contract between two situations: A – A situation of defects of consent at the formation of the contract. Here they call removal of the contract by the will of the one whose consent is defective: annulment (ibṭāl), and they describe the contract in this situation as voidable. B – A situation of an emergent cause related to the performance of the contract, not its formation. Here they call the removal dissolution (faskh) or automatic termination (infisākh), according to circumstances. This is a good terminology. (2) As for binding ones like endowment and divorce, they are not dissolved by dissolution (faskh), but are subject to rules of nullity. (3) We will see in the discussion of classification of contracts type (11) that the continuing contract is one whose performance extends over a period of time, like lease and loan for use; the enjoyment of the contracted benefits in them is gradual, so the continuing contract is like a series of renewing contracts. Its opposite is the instantaneous contract, which does not require extended time for its performance, but is executed at once, like sale and compromise.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage is a systematic treatment of how contracts dissolve in Hanafi fiqh, drawn from al-Zarqā's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhī al-ʿĀmm. The author begins with the key mode of mutual rescission (iqālah). He explains that iqālah has a dual legal nature: between the original parties it is a pure dissolution, restoring them to pre-contract status; but regarding third parties, it is treated as a new contract. This protects third-party rights like pre-emption (shufʿa). For example, if a pre-emptor's right attached to a sale, and then the seller and buyer rescind, the seller is considered a new buyer, so the pre-emptor gets a new chance to exercise his right. Similarly, if a buyer sells the item, then rescinds with the sub-buyer and gets it back, he cannot claim a pre-existing defect against the first seller because the rescission is a new purchase. The reason given is that a change in the cause of ownership is like a change in the article itself—previous rights are cleansed. The author notes that modern legal theory agrees with this principle. The rule is that every contract can be rescinded except marriage. The conditions for iqālah are detailed in fiqh works. Next, the author categorizes contracts by bindingness: (A) Non-binding contracts like partnership and agency can be dissolved by either party, unless a third party's right is involved. (B) Contracts binding on one side only, like a pledge (rahn), can be dissolved by the party not bound (the creditor). (C) Contracts originally binding on both sides can become non-binding for one party due to defects of consent (e.g., option of defect), allowing that party alone to dissolve. (D) Non-binding solitary acts of will, like a charitable bequest, can be revoked by the testator. Finally, the author discusses automatic termination (infisākh) due to impossibility of performance. A sale ends if the goods are destroyed before delivery; but the seller's death does not terminate the sale—the heir steps in. The author also clarifies terminology: Western jurists distinguish between voidable (for defects at formation) and dissolution or automatic termination (for subsequent causes). In Hanafi fiqh, binding dispositions like waqf or irrevocable divorce are not simply dissolved but follow rules of nullity. This passage is foundational for understanding how contracts end in Hanafi law, and it reflects the school's careful balancing of party autonomy, third-party rights, and legal certainty.

Key Terms
العقد غير اللازم (al-ʿaqd ghayr al-lāzim): العقد الذي يحق لكل من الطرفين فسخه بإرادته المنفردة دون حاجة لرضا الآخر أو سبب
الانفساخ (al-infisākh): زوال العقد بنفسه دون إرادة أحد الطرفين بسبب استحالة التنفيذ أو فقدان ما يعتمد عليه بقاء العقد المستمر
خيار العيب (khiyār al-ʿayb): حق المشتري في فسخ البيع إذا وجد في المبيع عيباً قديماً لم يعلم به

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, contracts are categorized by bindingness; non-binding contracts can be dissolved unilaterally, while binding contracts require mutual consent or a legal right. Automatic termination (infisākh) occurs without any party's act when performance becomes impossible.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications

The Maxim: Rescission Makes the Contract as if It Never Existed
Explains the general principle of rescission and its limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that rescission makes the contract null ab initio to determine whether rescission is possible in spot and continuous contracts, identifying when restitution is possible and when it is barred.

Core Idea
Rescission requires restitution; if the subject matter is destroyed or time has passed, rescission is impossible.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational Hanafi maxim on contract rescission: 'Rescission makes the contract as if it had never been.' This principle, stated by Ibn Nujaym, governs the effect of dissolution. However, its application depends on whether restitution (tarad) is possible. We will examine how this rule applies to spot contracts (like sale) and continuous contracts (like lease), and when rescission is limited or prohibited due to impossibility of restitution.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its source
The maxim is from Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir: 'Rescission makes the contract as if it had never been.' This implies full annulment ab initio (istinad).
2
Identify the requirement for restitution
When a contract is rescinded, the parties must return to their pre-contract positions. This is obligatory (tarad). If restitution is impossible, rescission cannot be carried out.
Restitution is the key condition for applying the maxim.
3
Apply to spot contracts
For spot contracts like sale, the subject matter must exist for rescission. If it is destroyed, rescission is impossible. If only part is destroyed, rescission is allowed for the remaining part.
Example: If a sold item is consumed, sale cannot be rescinded.
4
Apply to continuous contracts
For continuous contracts like lease, if fully executed, rescission is impossible because time cannot be returned. If partially executed, rescission is allowed only for the future portion while the past stands.
Time is an irreplaceable element.
