الفصل (٤٥) انحلال العقد
ويقول ابن نجيم في أحكام الفسوخ من كتابه (الأشباه والنظائر): «أن الفسخ يجعل العقد كأنه لم يكن».
فإذا كان العقد من عقود المعاوضة المالية كبيع أو صلح أو قسمة مثلاً وتم تنفيذه، ثم انحل العقد بأحد الأسباب الآتي بيانها، وجب التراد أي نقض التنفيذ الواقع فعلى البائع إعادة الثمن إلى المشتري، وعلى المشتري إعادة المبيع إلى البائع ومثل ذلك يقال في بقية العقود.
فإذا لم يمكن نقض التنفيذ مطلقاً، لا كلياً ولا جزئياً، فيما تم تنفيذه من آثار العقد امتنع الفسخ وإن أمكن النقض والتراد عيناً في جزء من محل العقد دون آخر جاز الفسخ فيما يمكن فيه النقض والتراد دون ما لا يمكن.
وعلى هذا الأساس تتفرّع حالات امتناع الفسخ وجوازه، وحالات الاستناد والاقتصار فيه :
أ - ففي العقود الفورية يشترط الفقهاء قيام محل العقد إذا كان عيناً لتمكن إقالته فإذا كان المحل - كالمبيع مثلاً في عقد البيع - هالكاً أو مستهلكاً بعد التنفيذ لا تمكن إقالة البيع لعدم إمكان التراد.
وإذا كان بعضه هالكاً جازت الإقالة في الباقي فقط
ب - وفي العقود الزمنية المستمرة إذا تم تنفيذها تماماً لا يمكن فسخها كالإجارة بعد استيفاء كامل المنافع وانقضاء مدة الإيجار، فإنها حينئذ لا تقبل الإقالة لأن الترادَّ فيها غير ممكن ما دام الزمن عنصراً فيها، والزمن لا يمكن رده .
ج - وأما العقود المستمرة قبل تمام تنفيذها كالإجارة التي مضى من مدتها قسم وبقي قسم، فإنها تقبل الفسخ بالنسبة إلى ما بقي من مدتها دون ما مضى. فإنّ ما مضى يجري على حكم العقد؛ ويكون للمؤجر حصة الزمن الماضي من الأجرة المتفق عليها.
٥٩٧
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence
Liable for delivery as long as the sold item remains in the estate, because the spot contract like sale and its like, all its effects are established collectively upon its conclusion, so the survival of the contracting party after that and his death are equal.
B - Partnership (shirkah), mudarabah, muzara'ah, and musaqah are dissolved by the death of one of the contracting parties, because these contracts create practical obligations with renewed effects that involve extension and continuity; their continuation depends on the survival of the contracting party in addition to the survival of the subject matter.
Ijarah (lease) – according to the preponderant view in Hanafi ijtihad – likewise dissolves upon the death of one of the contracting parties.
Our laws today hold that ijarah does not dissolve upon death, which is the Shafi'i ijtihad; and public interest (maslahah) today requires it.
4/45 - Result of Dissolution of Contract: Istinad (Retroactivity) and Iqtisar (Non-retroactivity) Therein
The general principle concerning the results of dissolution of a contract, whether by rescission (faskh) or automatic dissolution (infisakh), is that it returns the contracting parties to their respective positions prior to the contract.
(1) See on the meaning of mudarabah (F 14/46).
(2) In the common legal terminology of our era, the extension of rulings to the past is called 'retroactive effect'. This expression is used for the retroactive effect of laws themselves as well as for the effects of contracts alike. It is said: this law has retroactive effect, that one does not; likewise, it is said: if the owner of property without permission, upon the owner's ratification, has a retroactive effect, then the contract's ruling is considered effective from its conclusion, not from its ratification. There is no term in legal language for the absence of retroactive effect. As for Islamic jurisprudence, rich in its language and terminology that manifest the genius of its jurists over the ages, it calls the non-retroactivity of effects 'iqtisar', meaning that the ruling is established limited to the present state without extending to the past. And it calls the retroactivity of effect 'istinad', which is the terminology of the Hanafi school. The Malikis call it 'in‘itaf'. Our new civil code, the drafters of its Egyptian origin, borrowed this terminology from Islamic jurisprudence, using the word 'istinad' in the sense of retroactive effect. 596
[Page 565]
Chapter (45) Dissolution of Contract
Ibn Nujaym says in the rulings on rescissions from his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir: 'That rescission (faskh) makes the contract as if it had never been.'
If the contract is one of financial transactional contracts like sale, sulh, or division, for example, and it has been executed, then the contract is dissolved by one of the reasons to be mentioned, restitution (tarad) becomes obligatory, i.e., reversal of the executed performance: the seller must return the price to the buyer, and the buyer must return the sold item to the seller; the same applies to the rest of the contracts.
If reversal of performance is absolutely impossible, neither wholly nor partially, regarding what has been executed of the contract's effects, rescission is prohibited. But if reversal and restitution in kind are possible in part of the contract's subject matter but not in another part, rescission is permissible in what can be reversed and restituted, not in what cannot.
On this basis are derived the cases where rescission is prohibited or permissible, and the cases of istinad (retroactivity) and iqtisar (non-retroactivity) therein:
A - In spot contracts (fawriyyah), the jurists require the existence of the subject matter if it is a specific object to enable its rescission (iqalah). If the subject matter – like the sold item in a sale contract – is destroyed or consumed after execution, rescission of the sale is not possible due to impossibility of restitution. If part of it is destroyed, rescission is permissible only for the remaining part.
B - In continuous time-based contracts (zamanistamirrah) after full execution, they cannot be rescinded, such as a lease after full utilization of benefits and expiry of the lease term; then it does not accept rescission because restitution is impossible as long as time is an element in it, and time cannot be returned.
C - As for continuous contracts before complete execution, such as a lease where part of the term has passed and part remains, it accepts rescission with respect to the remaining part of the term, not the past. For the past runs on the ruling of the contract; and the lessor is entitled to the share of the past period from the agreed rent. 597
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage is a discussion from the general introduction to Islamic jurisprudence (al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm) on the theory of contracts, specifically focusing on the dissolution of contracts and its effects. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains the general principle that dissolution of a contract – whether by rescission (faskh) or automatic dissolution (infisakh) – aims to return the parties to their pre-contract positions. He highlights the terminology used in Hanafi fiqh: 'istinad' for retroactive effect and 'iqtisar' for non-retroactive effect. He notes that the modern Egyptian civil code adopted the term 'istinad' from Islamic jurisprudence.

The passage then applies this principle to various contract types. For spot contracts (like sale), all effects are established immediately upon formation, so death of a party does not affect it. For partnership, mudarabah, muzara‘ah, musaqah, and according to the preponderant Hanafi view, ijarah, these dissolve upon death because they involve ongoing obligations. However, contemporary positive law follows the Shafi‘i view that ijarah does not dissolve upon death, citing public interest.

Ibn Nujaym’s maxim from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza’ir is cited: ‘Rescission makes the contract as if it had never been.’ This is the foundational rule. The author then elaborates on the conditions for restitution (tarad). Restitution is obligatory when possible; if impossible wholly or partially, rescission is limited accordingly. Specific cases are given: in spot contracts, the subject matter must exist for rescission to be possible; in continuous contracts fully executed (e.g., a completed lease), rescission is impossible because time cannot be returned; in partially executed continuous contracts (e.g., a lease partially performed), rescission is allowed only for future performance while past performance stands.

This lesson provides a clear framework for understanding how the Hanafi school handles contract dissolution, balancing the ideal of restoring parties to pre-contract status with practical limitations.

Key Terms
استناد (istinād): Retroactive effect of dissolution, treating the contract as if it never existed from inception.
اقتصار (iqtisār): Non-retroactive effect, where the dissolution only applies from the time of dissolution forward.
انحلال العقد (inhilāl al-‘aqd): Dissolution of contract, whether by rescission (faskh) or automatic dissolution (infisakh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the condition for rescission in a spot contract like sale?
A) The subject matter must be in existence
B) The price must be returned
C) Both parties must agree
D) The contract must be in writing

Key Takeaway
Rescission seeks to undo the contract, but practical limitations—like destruction of subject matter or passage of time—may bar full annulment.

Dissolution of Continuous Contracts upon Death and the Ijarah Dispute
Explains the effect of death on contracts and the ijarah dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between spot contracts (which survive the death of a party) and continuous contracts (which generally dissolve upon death), and explain the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute regarding ijarah.

Core Idea
Continuous contracts dissolve upon death; spot contracts do not. Ijarah is debated.

This lesson examines the effect of death on different contract types according to the Hanafi school. While spot contracts like sale are unaffected by the death of a party because all effects are established immediately, continuous contracts like partnership, mudarabah, muzara'ah, musaqah, and (according to the preponderant Hanafi view) ijarah dissolve upon death. We also explore the Shafi'i counterview on ijarah and the modern legal adoption of that view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for spot contracts
Sale and similar spot contracts are not dissolved by death of a party because all effects are established at conclusion.
The text says: 'the survival of the contracting party after that and his death are equal.'
2
Identify the ruling for continuous contracts
Partnership, mudarabah, muzara'ah, musaqah dissolve upon death because they involve ongoing obligations depending on the person.
These contracts create obligations with renewed effects that depend on the contracting party's survival.
3
Note the Hanafi view on ijarah
The preponderant Hanafi view is that ijarah also dissolves upon death.
The text says: 'Ijarah – according to the preponderant view in Hanafi ijtihad – likewise dissolves upon the death of one of the contracting parties.'
4
Recognize the Shafi'i opposition
The Shafi'i school holds that ijarah does not dissolve upon death; it continues with the heirs.
The opposing school's argument: ijarah is a contract of exchange similar to sale.
5
Understand the modern position
Modern positive laws adopt the Shafi'i view due to public interest (maslahah).
The text states: 'Our laws today hold that ijarah does not dissolve upon death, which is the Shafi'i ijtihad; and public interest today requires it.'

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
مسؤولاً بالتسليم ما دام المبيع قائماً في التركة، لأن العقد الفوري كالبيع ونحوه، تثبت جميع آثاره جملة واحدة بمجرد انعقاده، فبقاء العاقد بعد ذلك وموته سواء.

الفصل (٤٥) انحلال العقد
ويقول ابن نجيم في أحكام الفسوخ من كتابه (الأشباه والنظائر): «أن الفسخ يجعل العقد كأنه لم يكن».
فإذا كان العقد من عقود المعاوضة المالية كبيع أو صلح أو قسمة مثلاً وتم تنفيذه، ثم انحل العقد بأحد الأسباب الآتي بيانها، وجب التراد أي نقض التنفيذ الواقع فعلى البائع إعادة الثمن إلى المشتري، وعلى المشتري إعادة المبيع إلى البائع ومثل ذلك يقال في بقية العقود.
فإذا لم يمكن نقض التنفيذ مطلقاً، لا كلياً ولا جزئياً، فيما تم تنفيذه من آثار العقد امتنع الفسخ وإن أمكن النقض والتراد عيناً في جزء من محل العقد دون آخر جاز الفسخ فيما يمكن فيه النقض والتراد دون ما لا يمكن.
وعلى هذا الأساس تتفرّع حالات امتناع الفسخ وجوازه، وحالات الاستناد والاقتصار فيه :
أ - ففي العقود الفورية يشترط الفقهاء قيام محل العقد إذا كان عيناً لتمكن إقالته فإذا كان المحل - كالمبيع مثلاً في عقد البيع - هالكاً أو مستهلكاً بعد التنفيذ لا تمكن إقالة البيع لعدم إمكان التراد.
وإذا كان بعضه هالكاً جازت الإقالة في الباقي فقط
ب - وفي العقود الزمنية المستمرة إذا تم تنفيذها تماماً لا يمكن فسخها كالإجارة بعد استيفاء كامل المنافع وانقضاء مدة الإيجار، فإنها حينئذ لا تقبل الإقالة لأن الترادَّ فيها غير ممكن ما دام الزمن عنصراً فيها، والزمن لا يمكن رده .
ج - وأما العقود المستمرة قبل تمام تنفيذها كالإجارة التي مضى من مدتها قسم وبقي قسم، فإنها تقبل الفسخ بالنسبة إلى ما بقي من مدتها دون ما مضى. فإنّ ما مضى يجري على حكم العقد؛ ويكون للمؤجر حصة الزمن الماضي من الأجرة المتفق عليها.

Commentary
This passage is a discussion from the general introduction to Islamic jurisprudence (al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm) on the theory of contracts, specifically focusing on the dissolution of contracts and its effects. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains the general principle that dissolution of a contract – whether by rescission (faskh) or automatic dissolution (infisakh) – aims to return the parties to their pre-contract positions. He highlights the terminology used in Hanafi fiqh: 'istinad' for retroactive effect and 'iqtisar' for non-retroactive effect. The passage then applies this principle to various contract types. For spot contracts (like sale), all effects are established immediately upon formation, so death of a party does not affect it. This lesson provides a clear framework for understanding how the Hanafi school handles contract dissolution, balancing the ideal of restoring parties to pre-contract status with practical limitations.

Key Terms
انحلال العقد (inhilāl al-‘aqd): Dissolution of contract, whether by rescission (faskh) or automatic dissolution (infisakh).
عقد فوري (‘aqd fawrī): Spot contract where all effects are established immediately upon conclusion, such as sale.
عقد مستمر (‘aqd mustamir): Continuous contract where effects are realized over time, such as ijarah, partnership.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate

Retroactive vs. Prospective Rescission: Spot and Continuing Contracts
Explains two types of dissolution effects with contract examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'Rescission makes the contract as if it never existed' to distinguish between retroactive (mustanid) and prospective (muqtasir) rescission in spot and continuing contracts, and identify when rescission is impossible.

Core Idea
Rescission does not always unwind the past — its effect depends on contract type.

This lesson unpacks Ibn Nujaym's qawida that rescission 'makes the contract as if it never existed.' However, as al-Zarqa shows, this is not a blanket rule. The type of contract — spot or continuing — determines whether the rescission retroactively nullifies past performance or merely cuts off future obligations. Understanding this distinction is essential for fatwa on contract disputes, especially when partial performance or destruction of subject matter occurs.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida
Identify Ibn Nujaym's maxim from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir: 'Rescission makes the contract as if it never existed.' This is the starting point for analysis.
The qawida is sughra and governs contract dissolution.
2
Classify the contract type
Determine if the contract is spot (fawri) like sale, or continuing (zamani) like lease. Spot contracts involve immediate exchange; continuing contracts involve time as a component.
This classification is the key to applying the qawida correctly.
3
Apply the qawida with furuat
For each furu' case, check the condition of subject matter existence. In spot contracts, if the subject matter exists, rescission is retroactive (mustanid) requiring mutual return. If destroyed, rescission is impossible. In continuing contracts, if partially performed, rescission is prospective (muqtasir) for the future portion only.
Use the examples from the text: sale with intact goods, sale with destroyed goods, lease partially performed.
4
Identify the istithna'
Note where the qawida is blocked: when subject matter is destroyed (spot) or contract fully performed (continuing). The principle of impossibility of return prevents retroactive effect.
This exception avoids logical absurdities and aligns with maslaha.
5
Derive the mufta bihi ruling
Conclude the applicable effect: mustanid for spot contracts with intact subject matter; muqtasir for continuing contracts except when fully performed (no rescission).
The mufta bihi is the operative ruling for fatwa based on contract type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٥) انحلال العقد
ويقول ابن نجيم في أحكام الفسوخ من كتابه (الأشباه والنظائر): «أن الفسخ يجعل العقد كأنه لم يكن».
فإذا كان العقد من عقود المعاوضة المالية كبيع أو صلح أو قسمة مثلاً وتم تنفيذه، ثم انحل العقد بأحد الأسباب الآتي بيانها، وجب التراد أي نقض التنفيذ الواقع فعلى البائع إعادة الثمن إلى المشتري، وعلى المشتري إعادة المبيع إلى البائع ومثل ذلك يقال في بقية العقود.
فإذا لم يمكن نقض التنفيذ مطلقاً، لا كلياً ولا جزئياً، فيما تم تنفيذه من آثار العقد امتنع الفسخ وإن أمكن النقض والتراد عيناً في جزء من محل العقد دون آخر جاز الفسخ فيما يمكن فيه النقض والتراد دون ما لا يمكن.
وعلى هذا الأساس تتفرّع حالات امتناع الفسخ وجوازه، وحالات الاستناد والاقتصار فيه :
أ - ففي العقود الفورية يشترط الفقهاء قيام محل العقد إذا كان عيناً لتمكن إقالته فإذا كان المحل - كالمبيع مثلاً في عقد البيع - هالكاً أو مستهلكاً بعد التنفيذ لا تمكن إقالة البيع لعدم إمكان التراد.
وإذا كان بعضه هالكاً جازت الإقالة في الباقي فقط
ب - وفي العقود الزمنية المستمرة إذا تم تنفيذها تماماً لا يمكن فسخها كالإجارة بعد استيفاء كامل المنافع وانقضاء مدة الإيجار، فإنها حينئذ لا تقبل الإقالة لأن الترادَّ فيها غير ممكن ما دام الزمن عنصراً فيها، والزمن لا يمكن رده .
ج - وأما العقود المستمرة قبل تمام تنفيذها كالإجارة التي مضى من مدتها قسم وبقي قسم، فإنها تقبل الفسخ بالنسبة إلى ما بقي من مدتها دون ما مضى. فإنّ ما مضى يجري على حكم العقد؛ ويكون للمؤجر حصة الزمن الماضي من الأجرة المتفق عليها.

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
يتلخص مما تقدم أن انحلال العقد تكون نتيجته على أحد شكلين :
فتارة يكون مستنداً، أي ذا أثر رجعي منسحب على الماضي، فيوجب التراجع فيما نفذ من التزامات العقد.
ففسخ البيع بعد تنفيذه يوجب التراد في المبيع والثمن. وكذا انفساخه بهلاك المبيع قبل التسليم يوجب رد الثمن المقبوض لأن الثمن فيه يصبح بلا مقابل.
- وتارة يكون الانحلال مقتصراً ليس له انعطاف وأثر رجعي، وإنما يسري حكمه على المستقبل فقط من تاريخ وقوعه. وذلك في العقود الاستمرارية كالشركة والإجارة. فالفسخ أو الانفساخ يقطعان تأثير هذه العقود بالنسبة إلى المستقبل. أما ما مضى فيكون على حكم العقد.
وكذا انحلال الوكالة بالعزل لا ينقض تصرفات الوكيل السابقة

(۱) هذا التفصيل الذي رسمناه في استناد أثر الفسخ واقتصاره هو الذي يعلم بالرجوع إلى أحكام كل عقد، إذ يتجلى منها : أنَّ بعض العقود يكون لفسخه استناد أو انعطاف، أي أثر رجعي منسحب على الماضي ينقض ما تم من أحكامه؛ وبعضها يكون فسخه مقتصراً .
وقد نقلنا آنفاً عن ابن نجيم في أحكام الفسوخ من كتابه الأشباه والنظائر) أن الفسخ يجعل العقد كأنه لم يكن. ونقل تفسير ذلك عن فريق من العلماء بأنه يجعل العقد لم يكن بالنسبة إلى المستقبل دون الماضي، فإنه لو اعتبر العقد بالفسخ معدوماً في الماضي لوجب أن يعتبر فسخه أيضاً معدوماً، إذ لا يتصور فسخ إلا لعقد موجود، فاعتبار عدم العقد في الماضي يستلزم عدم الفسخ الذي لحقه فيعود العقد !! ر: الأشباه وحاشية الحموي ، ج ۲، الفن ٣، ص/ ١٩٥ - ١٩٦). ولكن لا يخفى أن الفقه إنما يبنى على المصالح وما تقتضيه دواعيها من الاعتبارات لا على مثل هذه التوليدات النظرية وكثيراً ما تفك في بناء أحكامه اللوازم النظرية المجردة استناداً إلى ما توجبه مصلحة التطبيق. وإن هذا الإشكال النظري على كل حال لا ينافي ما رسمناه من انسحاب حكم الفسخ في بعض العقود على الماضي من جهة متفق عليها وهي وجوب التراد فيما تم تنفيذه من التزامات العقد كرد المبيع واسترداد الثمن.
Chapter 45: Dissolution of a Contract. Ibn Nujaym says in his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir under 'Rulings of Rescissions': 'That rescission makes the contract as if it never existed.' If the contract is a financial commutative contract like sale, compromise, or partition, and it has been executed, then the contract dissolves due to one of the reasons to be mentioned, mutual return (taradd) becomes obligatory, i.e., reversing the execution that occurred. So the seller must return the price to the buyer, and the buyer must return the sold item to the seller. The same applies to other contracts. If reversing execution is absolutely impossible, neither wholly nor partially, for the executed effects of the contract, then rescission is prevented. But if reversal and return are possible in kind for part of the subject matter without the other, then rescission is permitted for that which can be reversed and returned, but not for what cannot. On this basis, the cases of prohibition and permissibility of rescission, and the cases of retroactive and prospective effect, branch out: A - In spot contracts, jurists require the existence of the subject matter if it is a specific thing to enable rescission (iqala). If the subject matter (e.g., the sold item in a sale contract) is destroyed or consumed after execution, rescission of the sale is not possible due to impossibility of mutual return. If part of it is destroyed, rescission is allowed only for the remaining part. B - In continuing time-based contracts, if they have been fully executed, they cannot be rescinded, like a lease after complete utilization of benefits and expiry of the lease term. At that point, rescission is not accepted because mutual return is impossible as long as time is a component in it, and time cannot be returned. C - As for continuing contracts before their complete execution, like a lease where part of its term has passed and part remains, it accepts rescission with respect to the remaining part of its term but not for the past. For the past, it is governed by the contract ruling, and the lessor is entitled to the portion of the agreed rent for the past time. Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. It is summarized from the foregoing that the dissolution of a contract results in one of two forms: Sometimes it is 'mustanid' (retroactive), i.e., having a retroactive effect applied to the past, necessitating reversal of what was executed from the contract's obligations. For example, rescission of a sale after its execution necessitates mutual return of the sold item and price. Likewise, its dissolution due to destruction of the sold item before delivery necessitates return of the received price because the price then becomes without consideration. Sometimes dissolution is 'muqtasir' (prospective), having no retroactive effect, but its ruling applies only to the future from the date of its occurrence. This applies to continuing contracts like partnership and lease. Rescission or dissolution cuts off the effect of these contracts with respect to the future. As for the past, it remains under the ruling of the contract. Similarly, dissolution of agency by dismissal does not nullify the agent's previous actions. (1) This detail we have set out regarding the retroactive and prospective effect of rescission is learned by referring to the rulings of each contract. It becomes clear from them that some contracts have rescission that is retroactive, i.e., having a retroactive effect applied to the past, nullifying what was executed of its rulings; and some have rescission that is prospective. We have quoted earlier from Ibn Nujaym in his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir under 'Rulings of Rescissions' that rescission makes the contract as if it never existed. And we quoted the interpretation of that by a group of scholars: that it makes the contract non-existent with respect to the future, not the past. For if the contract were considered non-existent in the past by rescission, then its rescission would also have to be considered non-existent, since rescission is only conceivable for an existing contract. Considering the contract non-existent in the past entails the non-existence of the rescission that followed it, so the contract would return!! (See: al-Ashbah and Hashiyat al-Hamawi, Vol. 2, Art. 3, pp. 195-196). However, it is not hidden that fiqh is built on interests (masalih) and the considerations they necessitate, not on such theoretical derivations. Often in constructing its rulings, fiqh breaks purely logical implications based on what the interest of application requires. And this theoretical problem, in any case, does not contradict what we have set out regarding the application of the ruling of rescission to the past in some contracts from an agreed-upon aspect: the obligation of mutual return regarding what was executed from the contract's obligations, such as returning the sold item and reclaiming the price.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work discusses the effects of contract dissolution (inhilal al-'aqd) and presents a key qawida from Ibn Nujaym: 'Rescission makes the contract as if it never existed.' The author then clarifies that this qawida is not absolute; it applies differently depending on the contract type. He divides contracts into spot contracts (fawriyya) and continuing contracts (zamaniyya). For spot contracts like sale, if the subject matter still exists, rescission retroactively undoes the transaction (mustanid), requiring mutual return. If destroyed, rescission is impossible. For continuing contracts like lease, if fully performed, rescission is impossible because time cannot be returned. If partially performed, rescission only applies to the future portion (muqtasir). The author also addresses a theoretical objection by some scholars that retroactive rescission is logically impossible, but he dismisses this by noting that fiqh prioritizes practical interests (masalih) over pure logical consistency. The final point is that retroactive effect is agreed upon only insofar as it entails the obligation of mutual return for executed obligations.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Dissolution or rescission of a contract, making it as if it never existed (in whole or in part) depending on contract type.
مستند (mustanid): Having retroactive effect; applied to past obligations, e.g., in spot contracts.
مقتصر (muqtasir): Having only prospective effect; does not nullify past performance.
إقالة (iqala): Mutual rescission by the parties, a form of faskh.
تراد (taradd): Mutual return of what was exchanged under the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A lease contract was made for one year. After six months, both parties agree to rescind the lease. What is the Hanafi ruling on the rescission of the remaining six months?
A) The rescission is void because lease cannot be rescinded once started.
B) Rescission is allowed only for the remaining six months, and the lessor keeps the rent for the past six months.
C) Rescission retroactively cancels the entire lease, and the lessor must return all rent.
D) Rescission is allowed only if the lessee returns the keys to the property.

Key Takeaway
The effect of rescission depends on the contract type: spot contracts are retroactive (mustanid), continuing contracts are prospective (muqtasir).

Definition and Types of Partnership (Sharika)
Defining partnership and its types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the contract of partnership (sharikat al-aqd) and distinguish it from partnership in ownership (sharikat al-milk), and apply the distinction in legal analysis.

Core Idea
Partnership by contract is a named contract for profit; joint ownership is not a contract.

This passage from al-Zarqa provides a clear definition of partnership (sharika) and its two fundamental types: partnership in ownership and partnership by contract. Understanding this distinction is essential because the legal rules governing each are entirely different—only partnership by contract is considered a named contract in Hanafi fiqh, with its own conditions and effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Extract the precise definition of partnership by contract from the passage: an agreement between two or more persons to cooperate in a profit-making activity and share profits.
Note the key elements: multiple parties, cooperation, profit-seeking, and profit-sharing.
2
Distinguish the two types
Contrast partnership in ownership (sharikat al-milk) and partnership by contract (sharikat al-aqd) as described. Sharikat al-milk arises from natural causes like inheritance; it is not a contract. Sharikat al-aqd is intentional and contractual.
Remember: the same property can be owned jointly without a partnership contract.
3
Recognize legal significance
Understand that only sharikat al-aqd is counted among the named contracts in fiqh. This means specific rules apply (e.g., mutual agency, profit-sharing obligations). Sharikat al-milk lacks these unless a separate contract exists.
This distinction affects fatwa: a mufti must first classify the arrangement.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a case of joint property or a business venture, determine which type applies based on whether there is an agreement to invest and share profits.
Practice with examples: inheritance vs. joint purchase with profit intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٣/٤٦ ـ (١١) عقد الشركة : (المجلة / ١٣٢٩ - ١٤٠٣) .. وهو عقد بين شخصين فأكثر على التعاون في عمل اكتسابي، واقتسام أرباحه.

والشركة في ذاتها قد تكون شركة في ملك مشترك بين عدة أشخاص ناشئة عن سبب طبيعي كالإرث مثلاً. وقد تكون شركة عقد بأن يتعاقد جماعة على القيام بعمل استثماري يتساعدون فيه بالمال أو بالعمل، ويشتركون في نتائجه.

فشركة الملك هي من قبيل الملك الشائع وليست من العقود، وإن كان سببها قد يكون عقداً، كما لو اشترى شخصان شيئاً، فإنه يكون مشتركاً بينهما شركة ملك، ولكن ليس بينهما عقد على استغلاله واستثماره بتجارة أو إجارة ونحو ذلك من وسائل الاسترباح كما تقدم بيانه في نظرية المكية (ر ف ٨/٢٤)

وأما شركة العقد التي غايتها الاستثمار والاسترباح فهي المقصودة هنا والمعدودة من أصناف العقود المسماة
Section 11: The Contract of Partnership (al-Sharika) (al-Majalla, articles 1329-1403). It is a contract between two or more persons to cooperate in a profit-making activity and to share its profits. Partnership in itself may be a partnership in common ownership between several persons arising from a natural cause such as inheritance, for example. Or it may be a partnership by contract (sharikat aqd) where a group contracts to undertake an investment activity, assisting each other with capital or labor, and sharing in its results. Partnership in ownership (sharikat milk) is a type of common ownership and is not one of the contracts, although its cause may be a contract, as when two persons buy something; it becomes jointly owned between them as ownership partnership, but there is no contract between them to exploit and invest it through trade, lease, or other means of profit-making, as previously explained in the theory of ownership. As for the partnership by contract, whose purpose is investment and profit-making, it is what is intended here and counted among the types of named contracts.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage defines the partnership contract (sharikat al-aqd). It first gives the definition: an agreement between two or more persons to cooperate in a profit-generating activity and share profits. Then it distinguishes between two types: partnership in ownership (sharikat milk) and partnership by contract (sharikat aqd). Partnership in ownership is not a contract itself but a result of joint ownership (e.g., inheritance or joint purchase). There is no agreement to exploit the property for profit. In contrast, partnership by contract is a true contract where parties agree to invest capital or labor and share profits. The author notes that the latter is the focus here and is considered one of the named contracts in Islamic fiqh. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi jurisprudence because the legal effects differ: sharikat milk does not create agency or profit-sharing obligations, whereas sharikat aqd does. The reference to al-Majalla indicates that the rules align with the Ottoman codification of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
عقد الشركة (aqd al-sharika): A contract between two or more persons to cooperate in profit-making and share profits.
شركة الملك (sharikat al-milk): Joint ownership of property arising from a cause like inheritance or purchase, not from a profit-sharing contract.
شركة العقد (sharikat al-aqd): A contractual partnership aimed at investment and profit-making.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of partnership is considered a named contract in this passage?
A) Partnership in ownership (sharikat al-milk)
B) Partnership by contract (sharikat al-aqd)
C) Both are named contracts
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Partnership by contract is a distinct named contract requiring an agreement to profit; joint ownership alone does not create a partnership.

Mudaraba (Qirad): The Profit-Sharing Partnership
Defines mudaraba and notes terminological differences between schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mudaraba, distinguish the roles of rabb al-mal and mudarib, and explain the terminological difference between Hanafis and Shafi‘is/Malikis.

Core Idea
Mudaraba: one provides capital, the other labor, profit shared – not fixed return.

Mudaraba, also known as qirad, is one of the fundamental partnership contracts in Islamic finance. It embodies the principle of profit-sharing with capital and labor. This lesson will anchor the definition, the parties, and the key terminological distinctions between the madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
Define mudaraba
State the core elements: capital from one side, labor from the other, profit shared as agreed.
2
Identify the parties
Name the capital provider (rabb al-mal) and the worker/manager (mudarib).
3
Note the synonym qirad
Recognize that both 'mudaraba' and 'qirad' refer to the same contract.
4
Recognize terminological differences
Hanafis prefer 'mudaraba'; Shafi‘is and Malikis prefer 'qirad'. This difference is purely linguistic, not substantive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٦) عرض لأهم العقود المسماة

١٤/٤٦ - (١٢) المضاربة : (المجلة / ١٤٠٤ - ١٤٣٠). وهي نوع من شركة العقد يتفق فيها على أن يكون رأس المال من جانب، والعمل على استثماره من الجانب الآخر، والربح مشترك.

فصاحب رأس المال يسمى ربّ المال والعامل فيه : مضارب .

عقد المضاربة أيضاً قراضاً (بكسر القاف)، يقال: قارضه، ويسمى وضاربه، إذا دفع إليه المال ليعمل فيه، والربح مشترك بنسبة معينة يتفقان عليها.

لكن شاع لفظ المضاربة في اصطلاح فقهاء الحنفية، ولفظ القراض في اصطلاح فقهاء الشافعية والمالكية .
Chapter (46) Presentation of the Most Important Named Contracts. Section 12: Mudaraba (al-Majalla, articles 1404-1430). It is a type of partnership by contract in which it is agreed that the capital is from one side and the labor for its investment is from the other side, and the profit is shared. The owner of the capital is called rabb al-mal, and the worker in it is called mudarib. The mudaraba contract is also called qirad (with a kasra on the qaf); it is said qaradahu, and it is called qirad and mudaraba, when one gives money to another to work with it, and the profit is shared according to a specified proportion they agree upon. However, the term mudaraba became prevalent in the terminology of Hanafi jurists, and the term qirad in the terminology of Shafi‘i and Maliki jurists.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage covers mudaraba, a specific type of partnership contract (sharikat al-aqd). The essential feature is that one party (rabb al-mal) provides capital and the other (mudarib) provides labor and expertise in investment. Profits are shared according to a pre-agreed ratio; losses are borne solely by the capital owner unless due to negligence. The text also mentions that mudaraba is also known as qirad, and notes a difference in terminology between madhhabs: Hanafis predominantly use 'mudaraba', while Shafi‘is and Malikis prefer 'qirad'. This terminological difference does not affect the legal essence. The reference to al-Majalla articles shows that the Hanafi codification recognizes mudaraba as a distinct contract. The contract is considered a form of 'sharecropping' on capital, and it is permissible (ja‘iz) in Hanafi law, with conditions such as specifying the profit share and the capital being in the form of currency.

Key Terms
المضاربة (al-mudaraba): A partnership contract where capital is provided by one party (rabb al-mal) and labor by another (mudarib), with profit sharing as agreed.
القراض (al-qirad): Synonym for mudaraba, especially in Shafi‘i and Maliki fiqh.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The capital provider in a mudaraba contract.
المضارب (al-mudarib): The worker/manager in a mudaraba contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What term is used by Hanafi jurists for the contract where one provides capital and the other provides labor for profit-sharing?
A) Qirad
B) Mudaraba
C) Both are used equally
D) Muzara‘a

Key Takeaway
Mudaraba is a profit-sharing partnership where one party provides capital (rabb al-mal) and the other provides labor (mudarib), with profit divided by agreement and loss borne by the capital owner.

Muzara'a: The Agricultural Partnership in Hanafi Law
A foundational definition of muzara'a from the Majalla.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the essential elements of a muzara'a contract, state its legal classification (jâ'iz), and apply the Hanafi definition to distinguish it from other contracts.

Core Idea
Muzara'a is a permissible partnership where land and labor share in the harvest proportionally.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational definition of muzara'a as codified in the Majalla. Understanding the precise roles of the landowner (rabb al-ard) and the farmer (muzari') is essential for correct application in fatwa. This definition also reveals the conditions that prevent gharar and ensure fairness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their contributions
Note that one party provides land (rabb al-ard) and the other provides labor (muzari'). This is the core exchange.
No other contributions are required in the basic definition.
2
Identify the profit-sharing mechanism
The crop is shared according to an agreed proportion. The proportion must be a fraction, not a fixed amount.
If a fixed quantity is specified, it becomes a lease (ijarah) not muzara'a.
3
Classify the contract legally
From the definition, the contract is jâ'iz (permissible) as long as conditions are met. It is a type of partnership, separate from lease.
Additional conditions are detailed in the Majalla articles.
4
Note the conditions implied
The definition implies conditions: land must be arable, the share must be known, and the parties must be capable of contracting.
These prevent gharar and ensure mutual consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٥/٤٦ - (۱۳) المزارعة : (المجلة / ١٤٣١ - ١٤٤٠). -

وهي نوع شركة زراعية على استثمار الأرض، يتعاقد فيها الطرفان على أن تكون الأرض من أحدهما والعمل من الآخر، والمحصول الزراعي مشترك بينهما بنسبة يتفق عليها.

والعامل في الأرض بالمزارعة يسمى مزارعاً. والطرف الآخر : رب الأرض.
Section 13: Muzara‘a (al-Majalla, articles 1431-1440). It is a type of agricultural partnership for the exploitation of land, in which the two parties contract that the land is from one of them and the labor is from the other, and the agricultural produce is shared between them according to an agreed proportion. The worker on the land in muzara‘a is called muzari‘, and the other party is called rabb al-ard (owner of the land).
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage defines muzara‘a, a specific agricultural partnership. The key elements: one party provides land (rabb al-ard), the other provides labor (muzari‘), and the crop is shared according to an agreed ratio. This contract is permissible in Hanafi law under certain conditions, such as the land being arable and the profit share being a fraction (e.g., half or one-third). Muzara‘a is distinguished from ijarah (lease) where a fixed rent is paid. In Hanafi fiqh, muzara‘a is part of the named contracts and is governed by detailed rules to prevent gharar and ensure fairness. The reference to al-Majalla codifies the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
المزارعة (al-muzara‘a): Contract where land is provided by one party and labor by another in exchange for a share of produce.
مزارع (muzari‘): The worker/farmer in a muzara‘a contract.
رب الأرض (rabb al-ard): The landowner in a muzara‘a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a muzara'a contract, what does the muzari' provide?
A) Land
B) Seeds
C) Labor
D) Irrigation

Key Takeaway
Muzara'a is a permissible agricultural partnership defined by land from one party, labor from the other, and proportional sharing of the crop.

Definition and Basic Elements of Musaqat
Defines musaqat and identifies the two parties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define musaqat, identify the two parties, and state the essential condition for its validity.

Core Idea
Musaqat: trees from one party, labor from another, fruit shared by agreed proportion.

Musaqat is a specific type of agricultural partnership unique to trees with edible fruit. It resembles muzara‘a (crop-sharing) but is tailored for perennial plants like date palms and grapevines. The mufti must understand its basic structure to apply its rules correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the contract
Recognize that musaqat is a partnership (shirkah) where capital (trees) and labor are combined for a joint purpose: producing fruit.
Not a sale or lease—it's a type of sharikah 'aqd.
2
Distinguish the two parties
One party (rabb al-shajar) provides the trees; the other (al-musaqi) provides the work (tending, watering, care).
The trees must already be planted and fruit-bearing or capable of bearing.
3
Specify the share of fruit
The fruit is shared according to a pre-agreed proportion (e.g., half, one-third). This ratio must be fixed and known to both parties.
If the share is unknown, the contract is fasid (void) per Hanafi rules.
4
Understand the legal effect
The resulting fruit becomes jointly owned by the two parties in the agreed proportion.
The contract does not transfer ownership of the trees themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٦/٤٦ - (١٤) المساقاة : (المجلة/ ١٤٤١ - ١٤٤٨).

وهي أيضاً نوع شركة زراعية على استثمار الشجر، يكون فيها الشجر من طرف والعمل في الشجر بتربيته وخدمته وسقيه من الطرف الآخر، والثمرة الحاصلة مشتركة بنسبة متفق عليها .

فالعامل هو : المساقي والطرف الآخر : رب الشجر.
Section 14: Musaqat (al-Majalla, articles 1441-1448). It is also a type of agricultural partnership for the exploitation of trees, in which the trees are from one party and the work on the trees, including their cultivation, care, and watering, is from the other party, and the resulting fruit is shared according to an agreed proportion. So the worker is called al-musaqi, and the other party is called rabb al-shajar (owner of the trees).
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage defines musaqat, a partnership specifically for trees (e.g., orchards). Similar to muzara‘a but for perennial plants. The tree owner (rabb al-shajar) provides the trees and land, while the worker (musaqi) provides labor for tending and watering. The fruit is shared according to a pre-agreed ratio. In Hanafi fiqh, musaqat is permissible and is considered a type of partnership. It is often applied to date palms, grapevines, etc. The contract must specify the share of fruit. Al-Majalla articles codify the rules, including that the trees must already be planted and bearing fruit or capable of bearing.

Key Terms
المساقاة (al-musaqat): Contract where tree-owner provides trees and the worker provides labor, sharing the fruit.
المساقي (al-musaqi): The worker in a musaqat contract.
رب الشجر (rabb al-shajar): The owner of the trees in a musaqat contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Trees
B) Land
C) Labor and care
D) Fruit

Key Takeaway
Musaqat is a valid partnership for existing fruit trees with labor from one party and a fixed share of the fruit.

Hanafi Definition of Agency (Wakala) and Its Core Elements
Defines wakala as a delegation contract with three parties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of wakala, identify its three essential parties (muwakkil, wakil, muwakkal bih), and apply the definition to determine the scope of an agent's authority.

Core Idea
Wakala is a delegation of authority, not a transfer of ownership: the agent acts on behalf of the principal.

This lesson unpacks the foundational definition of wakala (agency) as given in Hanafi fiqh. Agency is a critical contract in Islamic law, enabling representation in transactions, litigation, and other legal acts. Understanding the precise terminology and the contractual nature of wakala is essential before studying its detailed rulings and exceptions. The passage from al-Majalla provides a clear, concise definition that serves as a memorization anchor for the entire chapter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contract Type
Recognize that wakala is classified as a 'contract of delegation' (عقد تفويض). This distinguishes it from sale, hire, or gift – it is a permissive (ja'iz) contract that authorizes representation.
In Hanafi usul, wakala is considered a type of niyaba (representation).
2
Parse the Three Parties
The definition involves three essential elements: the principal (muwakkil), the agent (wakil), and the subject matter (muwakkal bih). Identify each and their roles.
The principal retains ownership; the agent merely exercises authority within limits.
3
Understand the Legal Effect
The effect of wakala is that the agent's actions within the scope of authorization are binding on the principal. This is not transfer of ownership but a conferral of authority to act.
Acts beyond the scope are not binding unless ratified.
4
Apply to a Simple Scenario
Test the definition: A (muwakkil) appoints B (wakil) to sell A's car. The car is the muwakkal bih. B's act of selling binds A as long as B follows A's instructions.
This distinguishes wakala from sale where the agent would own the car.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٧/٤٦ - (١٥) الوكالة : (المجلة ١٤٤٩ - ١٥٣٠).

وهي عقد تفويض ينيب فيه شخص شخصاً آخر عن نفسه في التصرُّف. فالمستنيب : موكل (بصيغة الفاعل والمستناب وكيل ومحل الوكالة، وهو الأمر المستناب فيه : موكل به
Section 15: Wakala (al-Majalla, articles 1449-1530). It is a delegation contract in which a person appoints another person to act on his behalf in disposition. So the person appointing is called muwakkil (in the form of the subject), and the appointee is called wakil, and the subject matter of the agency, which is the matter for which he is appointed, is called muwakkal bih.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage defines wakala, the contract of agency. It is a delegation of authority: the principal (muwakkil) appoints an agent (wakil) to perform legal acts on his behalf. The text details the terminology: muwakkil (the one who appoints), wakil (the appointee), and muwakkal bih (the matter for which agency is given). In Hanafi fiqh, wakala is a permissible contract (ja‘iz) and can be general or specific. The agent acts within the scope of authority given. It is widely used in trade, litigation, and other transactions. The reference to al-Majalla indicates that the rules follow the Hanafi codification. The contract is considered a form of representation (niyaba) and requires mutual consent.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakala): Contract where a person appoints another to act on his behalf in legal dispositions.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent.
وكيل (wakil): The agent appointed to act on behalf of the principal.
موكل به (muwakkal bih): The matter for which the agency is given.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wakil
B) Muwwakkil
C) Muwwakkal bih
D) Muwakkal

Key Takeaway
Wakala is a delegation contract where the principal retains ownership and the agent acts within a defined scope – understanding this is essential for all furu' applications.

Sulh (Compromise): Legal Nature and the Maxim of Closest Contract
Defines sulh and presents the qawida with furuat and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim "al-sulh yu'tabar bi-aqrab al-uqud ilayhi" to classify sulh agreements and identify the exceptions for guardians.

Core Idea
Sulh takes the ruling of the closest contract it resembles; guardians may not concede rights unless it is a profit.

Sulh (compromise) is a unique contract because its subject matter is not fixed—it can resolve any dispute. This flexibility raised a fundamental question for the Hanafi jurists: what rules govern sulh? Their answer is a powerful legal maxim: sulh is treated as the contract it most closely resembles. This lesson unpacks that maxim, its applications, and the crucial exception protecting minors and waqf properties.

Guided Path
1
Define sulh and its terminology
Identify the parties (musalih), the disputed right (musalah 'anhu), and the consideration (musalah 'alayh / badal al-sulh).
These terms are essential for precise legal analysis.
2
Understand why sulh has no fixed nature
Sulh can cover any dispute. The text says it 'does not have a specific subject distinguishing it by nature.' This is why a maxim is needed.
3
Identify the exception for guardians
Guardians (wali, wasi, mutawalli) cannot compromise on behalf of minors or waqf for less than full value unless the opponent is a denier and no evidence exists.
This is an istithna' from the general permissibility of sulh.
4
State the qawida and its three applications
The qawida: 'Sulh is considered according to the closest contract based on the form in which it occurs.' Then apply to sale, lease, and ibra cases.
Memorize the three furuat: property-property → sale; property-usufruct → lease; debt-partial payment → ibra.
5
Recognize the overriding hadith limitation
The footnote cites 'Umar's letter: 'Sulh is permissible except a sulh that makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful.' This sets a boundary for all sulh.
This hadith is a key text (nas) that limits the scope of valid sulh.

Source Text

(۱) انظر ما تقدم في كتاب عمر بن الخطاب إلى أبي موسى الأشعري رضي الله عنهما في القضاء من قوله : الصلح جائز إلا صلحاً أحلّ حراماً أو حرم حلالاً (ر: ف ٦/٣) .
46/18 - (16) Compromise (Sulh): (Majalla/ 1531-1560). It is a contract in which the two disputants agree on a right regarding what lifts the dispute between them. So each of the two parties to the compromise is a musalih. The right under dispute is the musalah 'anhu. What one of them gives or undertakes to his opponent to end the dispute is the musalah 'alayh; it is also called badal al-sulh. Chapter (46) Presentation of the Most Important Nominate Contracts. And the contract of sulh does not have a specific subject distinguishing it by nature and special suitability, because it occurs in every dispute, except that it usually involves the plaintiff relinquishing some of his claim. Therefore, it is not accepted from a guardian, executor, or administrator to compromise on a right belonging to a minor, incapacitated person, or endowment for a substitute less than the value of these rights, because those managing the funds of minors and endowments are not allowed to donate anything from their assets or relinquish anything from their rights, unless the opponent is a denier and ready to take an oath and the guardian, executor, or administrator has no evidence to prove the disputed right. Then it is permissible for them to compromise with the opponent, because sulh is profit. (cf. Majalla/ 1539-1540)(1) At that time. And since the contract of sulh has no specific subject, the jurists decided that sulh is considered according to the closest contract to it based on the form in which it occurs. So sulh for property in exchange for property is considered in the ruling of sale, and sulh for property in exchange for a usufruct is considered in the ruling of lease, such as if the subject of dispute is a specific property, and they compromise that one leaves it for the other in exchange for living in his house for a month, for example. And sulh in a debt claim by the plaintiff taking only part of it and dropping his claim is considered a taking of some of the right and a release (ibra) of the remaining right. So sulh is like a clear white glass vessel, it takes the color of what is in it. And the fruit of that is that the rulings of the contract by which it is considered, whether sale, lease, or other, apply to sulh (cf. Majalla/ 1548-1560). (1) See what preceded in the letter of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with them, regarding judicial rulings: 'Sulh is permissible except a sulh that makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful' (cf: F 6/3).
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of sulh (compromise/settlement) and explains its legal nature, particularly the principle that sulh is considered the closest contract to it in form, with detailed furuat (applications). The opening defines sulh as an agreement between disputing parties to resolve their conflict, with specific terminology: the parties are musalih, the disputed right is musalah 'anhu, and the consideration given to end the dispute is musalah 'alayh or badal al-sulh. The text then emphasizes that sulh does not have a fixed subject; it can apply to any dispute. However, there are restrictions for guardians: they cannot compromise on behalf of minors or waqf for less than the full value unless the opponent is a denier ready to take an oath and there is no evidence. In such a case, sulh is considered a profit (sulh ribh). The core legal principle is stated: since sulh has no specific subject, jurists treat it as the closest contract based on its form. Three furuat are provided: sulh on property for property is treated as a sale; sulh on property for usufruct is treated as a lease (ijarah); sulh on a debt claim where the plaintiff takes part and drops the rest is treated as a partial receipt and release (ibra). The metaphor of a clear glass vessel taking the color of its contents illustrates that sulh adapts to the contract it resembles. The footnote cites the famous hadith from Caliph Umar: 'Sulh is permissible except a sulh that makes lawful what is unlawful or makes unlawful what is lawful,' which limits the scope of valid sulh.

Key Terms
مُصَالَح عنه (muṣālaḥ ʿanhu): The right under dispute in a sulh contract.
مُصَالَح عليه (muṣālaḥ ʿalayh): The consideration given or undertaken to end the dispute; also called badal al-sulh.
بدل الصلح (badal al-ṣulḥ): Same as musalah 'alayh; the compensatory payment or benefit in a sulh agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal classification of a sulh agreement where one party agrees to drop a debt claim in exchange for receiving a portion of the debt?
A) Sale
B) Lease
C) Partial receipt and release (ibra)
D) Gift

Key Takeaway
Sulh is a flexible contract that takes the ruling of its closest analogous contract; guardians may only compromise when it is a profit.

Mukhārajah: Definition and Basic Rules
Definition and rules of mukhārajah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the contract of mukhārajah, identify its key terms (mukhārij, mukhāraj), and apply the rule for distributing the outgoing heir's share based on the source of compensation.

Core Idea
Mukhārajah is a sale of an inheritance share, with distribution rules depending on whether compensation comes from the estate or personal assets.

In inheritance practice, heirs sometimes wish to buy out another heir's share. This transaction, called mukhārajah, is governed by specific rules. In this lesson, we unpack the definition, the roles of the parties, and the two scenarios of compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and its parties
Recognize the contract as mukhārajah (or takhāruj). The buyer is called mukhārij (active), the seller is mukhāraj (passive).
2
Understand the two compensation scenarios
Distinguish whether the compensation (badal) comes from the estate itself or from the personal assets of the remaining heirs.
This distinction determines how the outgoing heir's share is distributed.
3
Apply the distribution rule
If from estate: remaining heirs get the share in proportion to their original shares. If from personal assets: they get it in proportion to the amounts they paid.
The share is always distributed according to the source of payment.
4
Note the possibility of an outsider buyer
Mukhārajah can also occur between an heir and a non-relative (ajnabī), in which case it is a straightforward sale of the inheritance share.
This reinforces the sale-like nature of the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٠/٤٦ - (۱۸) المخارجة أو التخارج : عقد وهي يبيع فيه أحد الورثة حِصَّته من تركة مورثه المتوفى فيخرج منها ويحل محله مشتريها . يقال: تخارج الورثة إذا خارج بعضهم بعضاً. فدافِعُ المال الذي سيحل في المخارجة محل الوارث في حقه الإرثي هو مُخارج : (بصيغة الفاعل). والوارث الذي أخذ البدل وخرج من التركة : مُخارَج (بصيغة المفعول، أو خارج فإذا كان بدل المخارجة مدفوعاً من التركة نفسها استحق الورثة المخارجون نصيب المخارج بنسبة سهامهم في التركة . وإذا كان البدل مدفوعاً من أموالهم الأخرى الخاصة يكون نصيب الخارج الإرثي بينهم بنسبة ما دفع كل منهم. وقد تجري المخارجة بين وارث وأجنبي عن التركة، فتكون بمنزلة بيع الوارث حصته الإرثية لذلك الأجنبي، فيحل هذا الأخير محله في نصيبه الإرثي بين بقية الورثة. والمجلة لم تبحث عن المخارجة مع أنها عقد مدني محض، وإن كان متعلقاً بحق إرثي من حقوق الأحوال الشخصية الخارجة عن موضوع المجلة . والفقهاء يفردونها بفصل خاص من كتاب الصلح، لأنها في الغالب بين ورثة متنازعين ولكن الأليق بها كتاب البيع، لأن حقيقتها بيع وكثيراً ما تجري بين الورثة دون أن يسبقها نزاع بينهم. (ر: رد المحتار ٤٨١/٤ - ٤٨٣) .
20/46 – (18) Al-Mukhārajah or al-Takhāruj: A contract in which one of the heirs sells his share from the estate of his deceased predecessor, thereby leaving it, and the buyer takes his place. It is said: 'the heirs made mukhārajah' when they make such an arrangement among themselves. The payer of the money that will take the place of the heir in his inheritance right is called 'mukhārij' (in active form). The heir who took the compensation and left the estate is called 'mukhāraj' (in passive form, or 'khārij'). If the compensation of the mukhārajah is paid from the estate itself, then the other heirs are entitled to the share of the outgoing heir in proportion to their shares in the estate. If the compensation is paid from their own separate assets, then the inheritance share of the outgoing heir is divided among them in proportion to what each paid. And mukhārajah may occur between an heir and a non-relative of the estate, then it is like the sale of the heir's inheritance share to that outsider, so the latter takes his place in his inheritance share among the remaining heirs. The Majalla did not discuss mukhārajah even though it is a purely civil contract, although it relates to an inheritance right among personal status matters outside the scope of the Majalla. The jurists devote a special section to it in the Book of Sulh (compromise), because it is mostly among disputing heirs, but the more appropriate place is the Book of Sale, because its reality is a sale and often occurs among heirs without prior dispute. (See: Radd al-Muhtār 4/481-483).
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of mukhārajah (also called takhāruj), a mechanism by which one heir sells his share of the inheritance to another heir or an outsider. The author explains the terminology: the heir paying the compensation is the mukhārij (active), and the heir leaving is the mukhāraj (passive). Two scenarios are distinguished: if the compensation comes from the estate itself, the remaining heirs receive the outgoing heir's share proportionally to their original shares; if it comes from their personal assets, they receive it in proportion to the amounts they paid. The contract can also involve an outsider, in which case it is treated as a straightforward sale of the inheritance share. The passage notes that the Ottoman Majalla did not cover mukhārajah because it concerns inheritance (personal status), though it is a civil contract. Hanafi jurists typically place it under the Book of Sulh (compromise) because it often settles disputes, but the author suggests the Book of Sale is more suitable given its true nature as a sale.

Key Terms
مخارجة (mukhārajah): A contract in which an heir sells his share of the inheritance to another heir or an outsider.
مخارج (mukhārij): The payer in a mukhārajah contract; he takes the place of the outgoing heir in inheritance rights.
مخارج (mukhāraj): The heir who receives compensation and leaves the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the compensation in a mukhārajah is paid from the estate itself, how is the outgoing heir's share distributed among the remaining heirs?
A) Equally among them
B) In proportion to their original shares in the estate
C) According to the rule of 'awal
D) In proportion to the amounts they paid

Key Takeaway
Mukhārajah is a permissible contract for buying out an heir, with distribution rules depending on the source of compensation.

The Fiqh Classification of Mukhārajah: Between Sulh and Bay'
Debate on fiqh classification of mukhārajah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi jurists' debate over whether mukhārajah belongs to the Book of Compromise (Sulh) or Sale (Bay'), and evaluate the preferred view.

Core Idea
Though classified under Sulh due to its dispute-resolution context, mukhārajah's essence is a sale, making Bay' the more appropriate fiqh chapter.

The placement of legal contracts in fiqh books reflects their underlying nature. Mukhārajah is typically found under Kitab al-Sulh, but some argue it is really a sale. This lesson explores the reasoning behind these views and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposed classifications
The passage notes that jurists place mukhārajah in Kitab al-Sulh (compromise) but the author suggests Kitab al-Bay' (sale) is more fitting.
Each classification carries implications for the rules that apply.
2
Understand the basis for Sulh classification
Mukhārajah often occurs among disputing heirs, so it resembles a compromise (sulh) that ends a conflict.
The context of dispute is the main reason for this placement.
3
Understand the basis for Bay' classification
The essence (haqiqah) of mukhārajah is a sale: the heir transfers ownership of his share for compensation. It often occurs without prior dispute.
The author argues that essence overrides context.
4
Evaluate the author's preference
The author explicitly says 'al-alyaq biha kitab al-bay'' (the more appropriate is the Book of Sale). He supports this with the nature of the contract and the fact that disputes are not inherent.
This is a minority view; most Hanafi manuals keep it under Sulh.

Source Text

٢٠/٤٦ - (۱۸) المخارجة أو التخارج : عقد وهي يبيع فيه أحد الورثة حِصَّته من تركة مورثه المتوفى فيخرج منها ويحل محله مشتريها . يقال: تخارج الورثة إذا خارج بعضهم بعضاً. فدافِعُ المال الذي سيحل في المخارجة محل الوارث في حقه الإرثي هو مُخارج : (بصيغة الفاعل). والوارث الذي أخذ البدل وخرج من التركة : مُخارَج (بصيغة المفعول، أو خارج فإذا كان بدل المخارجة مدفوعاً من التركة نفسها استحق الورثة المخارجون نصيب المخارج بنسبة سهامهم في التركة . وإذا كان البدل مدفوعاً من أموالهم الأخرى الخاصة يكون نصيب الخارج الإرثي بينهم بنسبة ما دفع كل منهم. وقد تجري المخارجة بين وارث وأجنبي عن التركة، فتكون بمنزلة بيع الوارث حصته الإرثية لذلك الأجنبي، فيحل هذا الأخير محله في نصيبه الإرثي بين بقية الورثة. والمجلة لم تبحث عن المخارجة مع أنها عقد مدني محض، وإن كان متعلقاً بحق إرثي من حقوق الأحوال الشخصية الخارجة عن موضوع المجلة . والفقهاء يفردونها بفصل خاص من كتاب الصلح، لأنها في الغالب بين ورثة متنازعين ولكن الأليق بها كتاب البيع، لأن حقيقتها بيع وكثيراً ما تجري بين الورثة دون أن يسبقها نزاع بينهم. (ر: رد المحتار ٤٨١/٤ - ٤٨٣) .
20/46 – (18) Al-Mukhārajah or al-Takhāruj: A contract in which one of the heirs sells his share from the estate of his deceased predecessor, thereby leaving it, and the buyer takes his place. It is said: 'the heirs made mukhārajah' when they make such an arrangement among themselves. The payer of the money that will take the place of the heir in his inheritance right is called 'mukhārij' (in active form). The heir who took the compensation and left the estate is called 'mukhāraj' (in passive form, or 'khārij'). If the compensation of the mukhārajah is paid from the estate itself, then the other heirs are entitled to the share of the outgoing heir in proportion to their shares in the estate. If the compensation is paid from their own separate assets, then the inheritance share of the outgoing heir is divided among them in proportion to what each paid. And mukhārajah may occur between an heir and a non-relative of the estate, then it is like the sale of the heir's inheritance share to that outsider, so the latter takes his place in his inheritance share among the remaining heirs. The Majalla did not discuss mukhārajah even though it is a purely civil contract, although it relates to an inheritance right among personal status matters outside the scope of the Majalla. The jurists devote a special section to it in the Book of Sulh (compromise), because it is mostly among disputing heirs, but the more appropriate place is the Book of Sale, because its reality is a sale and often occurs among heirs without prior dispute. (See: Radd al-Muhtār 4/481-483).
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of mukhārajah (also called takhāruj), a mechanism by which one heir sells his share of the inheritance to another heir or an outsider. The author explains the terminology: the heir paying the compensation is the mukhārij (active), and the heir leaving is the mukhāraj (passive). Two scenarios are distinguished: if the compensation comes from the estate itself, the remaining heirs receive the outgoing heir's share proportionally to their original shares; if it comes from their personal assets, they receive it in proportion to the amounts they paid. The contract can also involve an outsider, in which case it is treated as a straightforward sale of the inheritance share. The passage notes that the Ottoman Majalla did not cover mukhārajah because it concerns inheritance (personal status), though it is a civil contract. Hanafi jurists typically place it under the Book of Sulh (compromise) because it often settles disputes, but the author suggests the Book of Sale is more suitable given its true nature as a sale.

Key Terms
مخارجة (mukhārajah): A contract in which an heir sells his share of the inheritance to another heir or an outsider.
كتاب الصلح (Kitab al-Sulh): The Book of Compromise in fiqh, covering contracts that resolve disputes.
كتاب البيع (Kitab al-Bay'): The Book of Sale in fiqh, covering all exchange contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because mukhārajah is always used to end disputes
B) Because the essence (haqiqah) of mukhārajah is a sale of an inheritance share
C) Because the Majalla did not cover it
D) Because it is a civil contract

Key Takeaway
Mukhārajah is canonically classified under Kitab al-Sulh, but its essence as a sale makes Kitab al-Bay' a contender; this debate illustrates how context and essence influence fiqh categorization.

The Loan Contract (Qard) and Its Dual Nature
Defining the loan contract and analyzing its two components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the loan contract in Hanafi fiqh, identify its dual components (i'arah and sale), and apply the legal effects (ownership and debt) to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Qard blends donation and exchange: the borrower owns the item and owes its like.

A loan (qard) may seem straightforward, but Islamic law recognizes it as a unique hybrid. This lesson unpacks its definition, the two contractual elements it contains, and its legal effect. Understanding this dual nature is essential for correctly applying qard in fatwa and distinguishing it from similar contracts like 'ariyah and sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and its subject matter
Read the definition: qard involves taking fungible, consumable property (mithli istihlaki) such as money, oil, or wheat. Identify this as the core condition for the contract's validity.
Fungible means replaceable by weight, measure, or number.
2
Name the parties and the property
Learn the technical terms: the lender is muqriḍ, the borrower is mustaqriḍ or muqtariḍ, and the loaned item is qarḍ or muqraḍ. Recognize the linguistic usage: aqrada (with hamzah) is the correct verb for lending.
This precision avoids confusion with qaraḍa (to cut).
3
Analyze the dual nature: i'arah and sale
Note the author's insight: qard initially resembles a loan for use (i'arah) because it starts as a donation (tabarru'). However, because the borrower consumes the item and returns only its equivalent, it also contains the meaning of a sale (mu'awada). This dual nature sets qard apart from both pure donation and pure exchange.
4
Determine the legal effect
Upon possession, the borrower becomes owner of the specific item, and a debt of its like is established in his liability. This contrasts with 'ariyah, where ownership remains with the lender and the same item must be returned.
5
Note the linguistic precision
The footnote clarifies that the verb for lending is aqrada (form IV), not qarada (form I, which means to cut). This is important for reading classical fiqh texts accurately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢١/٤٦ - (۱۹) القرض : وهو عقد موضوعه أن يأخذ أحد المتعاقدين من الآخر مالاً مثلياً استهلاكياً كالنقود والزيت والقمح، على أن يرد إليه مثله فيما بعد (۱). فدافع المال : مُقْرِض بضم فسكون والآخذ: مُسْتَقْرض ومُقْتَرض؛ والمال المدفوع : قَرْضٌ بفتح فسكون»، ومُقرَضٌ بصيغة المفعول». والقرض فيه معنى عقدين اثنين : معطيه . - معنى الإعارة ابتداء، لأنَّ فيه تبرعاً بإعطاء مال على أساس رده إلى - ومعنى المعاوضة والبيع ؛ لأن المال لا يُؤخذ فيه على أساس استعماله والاحتفاظ بعينه وإعادته بذاته كما في العارية، وإنما يُؤخذ على سبيل استهلاكه في حاجة المقترض، ثم تأدية عِوَض عنه من مثله إلى المقرض. فمتى قبضه يملكه ويصبح العوض المقابل ديناً عليه ثابتاً ذمته . (۱) يقال في اللغة: استقرض فلان من فلان إذا طلب قرضاً ؛ واقترض إذا أخذ قرضاً؛ وأقرض فلان فلاناً إذا أعطاه قرضاً ولا يقال بهذا المعنى قَرَضه قرضاً من الفعل الثلاثي المجرّد بل: أقرضه إقراضاً بزيادة الهمزة وإنما يستعمل الفعل الثلاثي المجرد بمعنى القطع، فيقال : قرضت الثوب ،ونحوه، إذا قطعته بالمقراضين، وهما المقص (ر ): المصباح المنير، والقاموس المحيط).
21/46 – (19) Al-Qard (Loan): It is a contract whose subject is that one of the two contracting parties takes from the other fungible, consumable property such as money, oil, and wheat, on the condition that he return its like to him later (1). The giver of the property is called 'muqriḍ' (with ḍamm on the first letter and sukūn on the second), and the taker is called 'mustaqriḍ' and 'muqtariḍ'; the property given is called 'qarḍ' (with fatḥ on the first and sukūn on the second) and 'muqraḍ' (passive participle). The loan (qard) contains the meaning of two contracts: the meaning of loan for use (i'ārah) initially, because it involves a gift of property on the basis of returning it to the giver; and the meaning of exchange and sale, because the property is not taken on the basis of using it and retaining its corpus and returning it itself as in 'ariyah, but rather it is taken for the purpose of consuming it for the borrower's need, then paying a substitute of its like to the lender. So when he takes possession of it, he owns it, and the corresponding substitute becomes a debt firmly established in his liability. (1) It is said in the language: 'istaqrada fulān min fulān' if he requested a loan; 'iqtaraḍa' if he took a loan; 'aqraḍa fulān fulānan' if he gave him a loan. The simple triple verb 'qaraḍahu qarḍan' is not used in this meaning; rather, 'aqraḍahu iqrāḍan' with the addition of the hamzah. The simple triple verb is only used in the sense of cutting: 'qaraḍtu al-thawba' meaning I cut it with scissors (the two blades). (See: al-Miṣbāḥ al-Munīr, al-Qāmūs al-Muḥīṭ).)
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage defines the loan (qard) contract in Hanafi fiqh. It specifies that the subject matter must be fungible (mithlī) and consumable (istihlākī), like money or staple goods. The parties are named: the lender is muqriḍ, the borrower mustaqriḍ or muqtariḍ, and the loaned item is qarḍ or muqraḍ. The author then gives a deep legal analysis: qard comprises two contractual aspects. Initially it resembles a loan for use (i'ārah) because the lender donates the property for return. However, it also contains the meaning of a sale because the lender does not expect the exact item back; rather, the borrower consumes it and must return its equivalent (mithl). Upon receipt, the borrower becomes owner of the item, and a debt of its like is created in his liability. This dual nature explains why qard is not a pure donation nor a pure exchange, but a unique contract. The footnote clarifies proper Arabic usage: the verb for 'to lend' is aqrada (with hamzah), not qarada (which means 'to cut'). This linguistic precision is important for understanding legal texts.

Key Terms
قرض (qarḍ): A contract whereby one party gives fungible, consumable property to another for consumption, with the obligation to return its like.
مقرض (muqriḍ): The lender who gives the property.
مقترض (muqtariḍ): The borrower who takes the property.
مال مثلي استهلاكي (māl mithlī istihlākī): Fungible, consumable property that can be replaced by weight, measure, or number.
إقراض (iqrāḍ): The act of lending; derived from aqrada (form IV), not qarada.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He becomes a trustee and must return the identical item
B) He becomes owner and a debt of the like is established in his liability
C) He may use it but the ownership remains with the lender
D) The contract becomes void if the property is consumed

Key Takeaway
Qard is a unique contract that transfers ownership of fungible, consumable property to the borrower, who must repay its like, blending elements of donation and exchange.

Definition, Scope, and Conditions of Iqalah
Legal exposition on iqalah: definition, scope, conditions, and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of iqalah, identify its scope (binding contracts excluding marriage), and apply its conditions to determine validity in given scenarios.

Core Idea
Iqalah is a contract to lift a previous contract, valid only for binding contracts (excluding marriage) and requires mutual agreement.

This passage clarifies the concept of iqalah (rescission) in Hanafi fiqh. It distinguishes between binding and non-binding contracts, outlines the conditions for a valid iqalah, and provides examples where it is invalid, such as after the subject matter perishes. Understanding the scope of iqalah is crucial for muftis when handling contract rescission questions.

Guided Path
1
Define iqalah
Read the author's definition and note that it is a contract that lifts a previous contract. Identify the key components: it is a mutual agreement between parties.
The definition is general, not limited to sale.
2
Identify the scope of iqalah
Determine which contracts can be rescinded by iqalah. The author specifies 'binding contracts (al-ʿuqūd al-lāzima) except marriage.' Non-binding contracts (e.g., wakalah, i'arah) are terminated unilaterally.
Marriage is excluded; it can only be ended by divorce or other means.
3
List the conditions of iqalah
Extract the required conditions: (1) agreement of both wills with all contractual components, (2) verbal expression or its equivalent, (3) unity of session, (4) existence of the legal subject matter (al-maḥall al-sharʿī).
These conditions mirror those of any contract.
4
Apply conditions to invalid cases
Analyze the two examples where iqalah is invalid: (a) after the sold item perishes in the buyer's hand – subject matter gone; (b) iqalah of a discharge of debt (ibrā’) – debt is extinguished and cannot return. This illustrates the principle 'as-sāqiṭ lā yaʿūd' (what has fallen does not return).
The maxim is a qaʿida fiqhiyya.
5
Note the dual nature
Understand that iqalah is a rescission between the original parties but is considered a new sale with respect to third parties. This protects third-party rights arising from the original contract.
This is not a contradiction but a legal construction.
