الدر المختار وحاشيته ١٥٠/٤).
Chapter (46): Overview of the Most Important Named Contracts. The scope of iqalah (rescission) is only binding contracts, excluding the marriage contract. A non-binding contract, like agency and loan of a commodity, is terminated by the unilateral will of one of the contracting parties without need for agreement, as previously mentioned (F 2/45). The marriage contract, after being validly concluded, does not admit of iqalah or annulment, but it can be terminated by other means as we will see in the classification of contracts (R: F 9/47). As for those binding contracts, if nothing occurs that divests them of the attribute of bindingness and gives one of the parties an option therein, they cannot be lifted and annulled after their conclusion except by the method by which they were concluded, namely the agreement of the two wills, because this annulment will cause the ownerships or rights established by the previous contract that is intended to be rescinded to be unsettled after their stability. Thus, agreement is necessary with all its contractual components and conditions—such as verbal expression or what stands in its place, unity of session, and existence of the legal subject matter. For example, iqalah of a sale is not valid after the sold item has perished in the buyer's hand, since there is no room for retransfer of ownership after the loss of the subject matter. Likewise, iqalah of a discharge of debt is not valid, because discharge drops the debt from the liability, and it is a rule that: that which has fallen does not return because it is non-existent (R: Majalla 51). So with the extinction of the debt, there remains no subject matter for the iqalah contract (R: Radd al-Muhtar 4/146 and 151). Jurists mention iqalah in the book of sales as a contract derived from it, because it most often occurs in sales, and because when a previous sale is rescinded through it, it is then considered, with respect to a third party, as a new sale, as previously discussed in the section on dissolution of contracts (R: F 2/45). (1) It was stated there that this dual consideration in iqalah (i.e., it being a rescission for the contracting parties and a new sale for a third party) is only to safeguard the rights of others that arose from the previous contract that was rescinded. Similarly, the Majalla also did this, making iqalah a branch of the book of sales and defined it—following most jurists—as the lifting of a sale contract (Majalla 163). This, as you see, is a definition of iqalah of sale, not of iqalah in general. However, it is appropriate to consider it an independent contract with which contracts of transactions are concluded, because iqalah is not specific to sales but applies to all binding contracts except marriage. The verified definition in general is that it is 'a contract by which a previous contract is lifted' (R: Chapter of Iqalah from the Book of Sales in al-Jawhara and in Radd al-Muhtar). Just as a sale contract is lifted by iqalah, so also are contracts of hire, guarantee, compromise, and partnership in cultivation, etc., lifted; thus the two contracting parties return to their state and rights before the rescinded contract. Even iqalah itself admits of iqalah; by the iqalah of the iqalah, the consideration of the contract between the two parties is restored, and their connection returns with its rulings and effects as if it had not been rescinded (R: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its gloss 4/150).
Source pages p. 592

Commentary
This passage is a legal exposition on iqalah (rescission of contracts) from a general introduction to fiqh. The author clarifies that iqalah applies only to binding contracts (uqud lazimah) and specifically excludes the marriage contract. Non-binding contracts like agency (wakalah) and loan for use (i'arah) can be terminated unilaterally without need for mutual rescission. For binding contracts, if they have not become non-binding due to an option, they can only be lifted by the same method they were concluded: mutual agreement. The author gives two examples where iqalah is invalid: (1) when the sold item has perished in the buyer's possession because the subject matter (mahall) no longer exists, and (2) iqalah of a discharge of debt (ibra'), because the debt once extinguished cannot be revived—the principle being that 'that which has fallen does not return.' The author notes that jurists usually discuss iqalah in the context of sales, but in reality it is a general contract applicable to all binding transactions except marriage. Even iqalah itself can be rescinded, effectively reinstating the original contract.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): A contract by which a previous contract is lifted, applicable to all binding contracts except marriage.
العقد اللازم (al-ʿaqd al-lāzim): A binding contract that cannot be terminated unilaterally unless an option arises.
الساقط لا يعود (as-sāqiṭ lā yaʿūd): A legal maxim meaning 'that which has fallen does not return'; used to prohibit reviving extinguished rights or properties.
المحل الشرعي (al-maḥall al-sharʿī): The legally valid subject matter of a contract, which must be existent, owned, and deliverable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a condition for a valid iqalah?
A) Written documentation
B) Presence of witnesses
C) Unity of session (waḥdat al-majlis)
D) Payment of a fee

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is a mutual contract to rescind a previous binding contract (excluding marriage) and requires the existence of the subject matter and all contractual conditions.

Trust vs. Guarantee Contracts: The Foundational Classification
Introduction to contract classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given contract as trust or guarantee and apply the Hanafi maxim to determine liability for loss.

Core Idea
Trust contracts shield the holder from loss unless negligence; guarantee contracts transfer full risk even from acts of God.

In Hanafi fiqh, contracts are divided into two fundamental types based on liability for loss. This classification governs many practical rulings in mu'amalat. The passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam lays out the distinction and provides key examples. We will study the maxim that determines liability and apply it to various contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
First, determine whether the contract falls under trust (amanah) or guarantee (daman) based on the nature of the transaction. Trust contracts include deposit, loan for use, lease, partnership. Guarantee contracts include sale, partition, settlement, loan.
The distinction hinges on who bears the risk of accidental loss.
2
State the relevant maxim
For trust contracts, the maxim is: 'Trusts are not guaranteed except in case of transgression or negligence.' For guarantee contracts, the opposite maxim applies: 'Guarantees are guaranteed against every loss, even by a heavenly calamity.'
Memorize both maxims; they are complementary.
3
Apply the maxim to the scenario
Identify any transgression (ta'addi) or negligence (taqsir) in trust contracts. If absent, the holder is not liable. In guarantee contracts, liability is automatic upon delivery.
Transgression means exceeding the permitted limits; negligence means failure to take proper care.
4
Derive the ruling
Based on the maxim and facts, state the legal consequence: compensation or no compensation, who bears the loss, etc.
This step is the mufti's conclusion.

Source Text

تصنيف العقود
- جرت عادة المؤلفين القانونيين في نظرية العقد أن يبحثوا في تصنيف العقود قبل البحث في الأحكام العامة. وقد رأينا نحن أن نؤخر بحثنا في تقسيم العقود وتصنيفها إلى ختام مباحث الأحكام العامة للعقد، لكي يكون الطالب قد تزود بالمعلومات الكافية التي تساعده على فهم التقسيم والتصنيف .

Chapter Forty-Seven: Classification of Contracts. – It is the custom of legal authors in the theory of contract to discuss the classification of contracts before researching the general rules. And we have decided to delay our research on the division and classification of contracts until the conclusion of the discussions on the general rules of contract, so that the student may have acquired sufficient information that helps him understand the division and classification. 47/2 – Indeed, contracts in general differ from each other in the basis upon which they rest, the subject they aim for, the characteristics that distinguish them, the qualities and rulings that affect them, and other legal considerations. And each group and category of them may share a common aspect from some perspectives and considerations, although there are differences and distinctions between them from other aspects and perspectives. For example, deposit (wadi‘ah), loan for use (‘ariyah), lease (ijarah), and partnership (shirkah) share that they are contracts of property that is received by the contract and implementation; in the hand of the depositee, borrower, lessee, or partner, it is considered a trust (amanah), and he is not liable for any loss or damage that befalls it except by his transgression or negligence in its preservation. As for sale (bay‘), partition (qismah), settlement (sulh), and loan (qard), for example, they are considered contracts of guarantee (damanat); as soon as they are implemented by delivery, the property that was subject to implementation becomes guaranteed upon the contracting party from any loss or damage, even if by a heavenly calamity, and the recipient bears it. So whatever befalls that property is on the responsibility and account of the contracting recipient.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Aam presents the Hanafi classification of contracts into two fundamental types: ‘trust contracts’ (uqud al-amanat) and ‘guarantee contracts’ (uquq al-damanat). The author delays this classification until after the general rules for pedagogical benefit. The key legal distinction is liability: in trust contracts (e.g., deposit, loan for use, lease, partnership), the property is held as an amanah; the holder is not liable for accidental loss unless he oversteps bounds or is negligent. In guarantee contracts (e.g., sale, partition, settlement, loan), the property immediately becomes a guarantee (daman) on the recipient, who bears full liability even for acts of God. This classification has wide application in Hanafi fiqh, affecting rulings on risk, compensation, and rescission. The examples given are all taken from the core areas of mu‘amalat.

Key Terms
أمانة (amānah): A property held by another without liability for loss except in case of transgression or negligence.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability for property such that the holder bears the risk of loss even from heavenly calamities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Deposit (wadi‘ah)
B) Loan for use (‘ariyah)
C) Sale (bay‘)
D) Partnership (shirkah)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of contracts into trust and guarantee determines who bears the risk of accidental loss: trust contracts protect the holder except for misconduct, while guarantee contracts transfer full liability upon delivery.

Classification of Contracts by Shari'ah Legality
Classifies contracts by shari'ah legality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the classification of contracts by shari'ah legality to identify prohibited contracts and state that their legal consequence is void (bāṭil) due to missing a condition of formation.

Core Idea
A contract prohibited by shari'ah is void for lacking a condition of formation.

In Islamic law, contracts are not all equal; some are lawful (mashrū') and others are prohibited (mamnū'). This classification helps a mufti determine validity at a glance. The key is understanding what makes a contract prohibited and what legal effect that prohibition has. In this passage, we learn that prohibited contracts are void from the start, not merely sinfu l but legally ineffective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that contracts are divided into lawful (mashrū') and prohibited (mamnū'). Lawful contracts are those permitted by sharī'ah; prohibited contracts are those forbidden.
Note the examples: sale, pledge, gift are lawful; sale of unborn animals, temporary marriage are prohibited.
2
Understand the basis of prohibition
Prohibition arises from a clear textual ruling (Qur'an or Sunnah) or from a principle like gharar (excessive uncertainty). The Prophet's prohibition of al-malaqih and al-madamin is cited as an example.
The hadith chain is weak but the ruling is established by other evidence; the author presents it as an example.
3
Recognize other prohibited contracts
Besides gharar, contracts are prohibited if they involve a minor's property (without guardian), public obscenity, crime, or temporary marriage (mut'ah).
These all violate a condition of formation or subject matter legality.
4
State the legal consequence
The consequence of a prohibited contract is that it is void (bāṭil) and not formed. This is because it lacks a condition of formation (shart al-in'iqād).
In Hanafi fiqh, a void contract is null ab initio, unlike a voidable (fāsid) contract which may be rectified.

(۳) بيع الملاقيح: هو بيع ما ستحمل به إناث الحيوان. وبيع المضامين هو بيع ما سيتولد من فحول الحيوان (رد المحتار، ١٠٢/٤). وقد كان ذلك معهوداً في الجاهلية تسليفاً على نتاج الأنساب المستجادة من الإبل والخيل، فنهى عنه النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام فيما نهى لما فيه من غَرَر ففي الحديث عن ابن عباس أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن بيع المضامين والملاقيح .... (ر: ف ۱/۳۱ و ۸/۳۱).
4/47 - (Secondly) With regard to legality and its absence: Under this view, contracts are classified into two types: 1- Lawful contracts: those permitted by the shari'ah and authorized, such as the sale of valuable property, pledge, and gift. 2- Prohibited contracts: those forbidden by the shari'ah and prohibited, such as the sale of fetuses in their mothers' wombs and the sale of al-malaqih and al-madamin (3), which were known in pre-Islamic Arab society and were prohibited by Islamic law. (3) Sale of al-malaqih: the sale of what female animals will conceive. Sale of al-madamin: the sale of what will be produced from male animals (Radd al-Muhtar, 4/102). This was customary in pre-Islamic times as a loan on the offspring of prized breeds of camels and horses, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade it as part of what he forbade due to the gharar (uncertainty) involved. In a hadith from Ibn Abbas: the Prophet forbade the sale of al-madamin and al-malaqih... (narrated by al-Tabarani, and al-Haythami said that Ismail ibn Abi Habiba was authenticated by Ahmad but weakened by the majority of imams; al-Zawa'id 4/104). So one must wait for the birth to occur before selling the offspring. Likewise, a gratuitous contract from a minor's property, and a contract involving what is contrary to public morals like obscenities, or contrary to public order like hiring for a crime, and temporary marriage. All these are prohibited, not lawful contracts. The consequence of a contract being unlawful is that it is considered void and not formed, due to the absence of one of the conditions of formation as previously stated. (See F 1/31 and 8/31).
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage presents the second classification of contracts: by their permissibility in shari'ah. The two categories are: lawful contracts (e.g., sale of valuable property, pledge, gift) and prohibited contracts. Prohibited contracts include those with excessive uncertainty (gharar), such as selling unborn animals (al-malaqih and al-madamin), which were pre-Islamic practices forbidden by the Prophet. The hadith evidence is cited, though its chain is weak. Also prohibited are contracts involving a minor's wealth (without guardian), contracts involving obscenity or crime, and temporary marriage (mut'ah). The legal effect of a prohibited contract is that it is void (bāṭil) and not considered formed because it lacks a condition of formation (shart al-in'iqād). This aligns with the Hanafi position that a contract lacking a condition of validity (e.g., legality of subject matter) is null ab initio.

Key Terms
عقد مشروع (ʻaqd mashrūʻ): A contract permitted by shari'ah, fulfilling all requirements for validity.
عقد ممنوع (ʻaqd mamnūʻ): A contract prohibited by shari'ah; it is void and not formed.
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract that leads to prohibition.
شروط الانعقاد (shurūṭ al-inʻiqād): Conditions of formation: prerequisites for a contract to be legally formed and considered existent.

Key Takeaway
A prohibited contract (mamnū') is void (bāṭil) ab initio because it lacks a condition of formation.

Valid and Defective Contracts: The Hanafi Distinction
Classifies contracts by validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid (ṣaḥīḥ) and defective (fāsid) contract in Hanafi law, state the impact of missing subsidiary conditions, and explain why a void (bāṭil) contract is not deemed a separate type in this classification.

Core Idea
A valid contract meets all conditions; a defective one lacks a subsidiary condition but can be rectified.

Contracts are classified in various ways in Islamic law. One pivotal classification, employed by Hanafi jurists, evaluates contracts based on the fulfillment of all required conditions. This lesson examines the two categories: valid (ṣaḥīḥ) contracts, which have all conditions in place and produce full legal effects, and defective (fāsid) contracts, which are valid in essence but lack a subsidiary condition. The passage also explains why void (bāṭil) contracts are not considered a third type in this scheme: they are legally non-existent. For a mufti, recognizing a fāsid contract as curable is crucial for providing practical guidance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and note that contracts are divided into ṣaḥīḥ (valid) and fāsid (defective). Observe that bāṭil (void) is intentionally excluded.
The footnote is key to understanding why bāṭil is not listed.
2
Define each category
From the definitions: a ṣaḥīḥ contract has all shari'ah conditions, both general and specific, in its essence and subsidiary aspects. A fāsid contract misses some subsidiary conditions.
The conditions include those related to price, term, subject matter, etc.
3
Examine the examples
The passage gives examples: a valid sale is one with a known price and term; a defective sale is one with an unknown price or an unknown deferment term. Similarly for lease: a valid lease specifies the object, wage, and duration; a defective lease lacks clarity in wage or period.
These examples illustrate subsidiary conditions.
4
Understand the exclusion of bāṭil
The author notes they did not include bāṭil because it is non-existent in Shari'ah. In Hanafi law, bāṭil contracts fail at the essential level (missing a rukn or general condition), so they are not considered contracts at all. This classification considers only contracts that have formed (exist in essence).
This distinction prevents confusion: a fāsid contract is still a contract but defective; a bāṭil contract is not a contract.

5/47 - (Thirdly) With regard to validity and invalidity: Under this view, contracts are also classified into two types: 1- Valid contracts: those in which all their general and specific shari'ah conditions are present in their essence and in their subsidiary aspects, such as the sale of valuable property for a known price, whether cash or on credit for a known term, and the lease of a specified thing for a specified wage and a specified period. 2- Defective (fāsid) contracts: those that are impaired by the absence of some of their conditions related to some subsidiary aspects, such as the sale of something for an unknown price, i.e., unspecified; or a known price but deferred to an unknown term. Likewise, a lease with ignorance of the wage or the period (1). We will see the clarification of the meaning of fasad and its ruling in the theory of legislative validators. (1) We did not classify contracts into formed and void (bāṭil) because the void is non-existent in shar'iah, so it is not considered a type.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage presents the third classification of contracts: by validity. Valid contracts (ṣaḥīḥ) fulfill all conditions, both general and specific. Examples include a sale with a known price and term, or a lease with specified subject, rent, and duration. Defective contracts (fāsid) lack some subsidiary condition, like an unknown price or an ambiguous time period. In Hanafi law, a fāsid contract is voidable, not void ab initio; it can be corrected by removing the defect. The footnote explains why the author did not include 'void' (bāṭil) as a separate category here: because a void contract is legally non-existent and thus not a real contract type. The distinction between 'bāṭil' (missing an essential pillar or condition of formation) and 'fāsid' (missing a subsidiary condition) is fundamental in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
عقد صحيح (ʿaqd ṣaḥīḥ): A contract that meets all shari'ah conditions, both essential and subsidiary, and produces legal effects.
عقد فاسد (ʿaqd fāsid): A contract that is valid in essence but defective in a subsidiary condition; it is voidable and can be rectified.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a contract that lacks a subsidiary condition is classified as fāsid, not bāṭil, and can be corrected by removing the defect.

Real vs. Consensual Contracts in Hanafi Law
Classifies contracts as real or non-real based on need for delivery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five real contracts in Hanafi law and explain why delivery is a condition of completion (tamām) rather than formation (inʿiqād).

Core Idea
Delivery perfects real contracts but does not form them; without it, the contract is valid but incomplete.

In Hanafi fiqh, contracts are classified not only by subject matter or consideration but also by how they reach their full legal effect. This fourth classification separates contracts that require physical delivery of the item for their completion (real contracts) from those that are fully binding upon offer and acceptance alone (consensual contracts). Understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti to determine when ownership transfers, when risk shifts, and what remedies are available in case of breach.

Guided Path
1
Define the real contract
A real contract (ʿaqd ʿaynī) requires delivery (qabḍ) of the subject matter for its completion in the eyes of the Shariʿah. Without delivery, the contract exists but produces no binding effect.
Delivery is a condition of perfection (sharṭ tamām), not of formation (sharṭ inʿiqād).
2
Memorize the five real contracts
The Hanafi school enumerates exactly five real contracts: gift (hibah), loan for use (ʿāriyah), loan (qarḍ), pledge (rahn), and deposit (wadīʿah). These are the only contracts to which the rule applies.
Other schools may add more; for a Hanafi mufti, this list is authoritative.
3
Contrast with consensual contracts
All other contracts – such as sale, lease, partnership, and agency – are completed and binding at the moment of offer and acceptance. Delivery may be an obligation but is not a condition for legal completion.
Thus, in a sale, ownership transfers immediately; in a gift, it transfers only upon delivery.
4
Apply the effect
If a real contract is formed but delivery has not occurred, the parties cannot enforce transfer of ownership or claim damages for non-performance; the contract is in a state of inchoate obligation.
This affects fatwa on liability for loss before delivery: the subject matter remains at the donor's risk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦/٤٧ - (رابعاً) بالنظر إلى الصفة العينية : تصنف العقود بهذا النظر صنفين أيضاً: ۱ - عقود عينية وهي التي يشترط لتمامها شرعاً تسليم الشيء المعقود عليه فلا يتم عقدها ولا يأخذ حكمه إلا بتنفيذها. وهذه هي العقود الخمسة المتقدمة ف ۱۳/۳۰). ۲ - وعقود غير عينية وهي التي تتم وتأخذ حكمها بمجرد عقدها كسائر العقود الأخرى.
6/47 - (Fourthly) With regard to the real nature: Under this view, contracts are also classified into two types: 1- Real contracts ('uqūd 'ayniyyah): those for which shari'ah requires delivery of the subject matter for their completion; they are not completed nor take their ruling except by execution. These are the five contracts previously mentioned in F 13/30. 2- Non-real contracts ('uqūd ghayr 'ayniyyah): those that are completed and take their ruling merely by their contract, like all other contracts.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth classification of contracts: by whether they are 'real' (calling for delivery). Real contracts require physical delivery (qabḍ) of the subject matter for the contract to be complete and produce legal effects. The author references 'the five contracts' mentioned earlier, which in Hanafi fiqh are typically: gift (hibah), loan for use (ʿāriyah), loan (qarḍ), pledge (rahn), and deposit (wadīʿah). These are incomplete without delivery. Non-real contracts, such as sale and lease, are formed and binding upon offer and acceptance alone, without delivery. This classification affects timing of ownership transfer and risk.

Key Terms
عقد عيني (ʻaqd ʻaynī): A contract that requires delivery of the subject matter for its completion.
تسليم (taslīm): Physical or constructive delivery of the subject matter.
تمام العقد (tamām al-ʿaqd): Legal completion of the contract, after which all effects apply.

Key Takeaway
Delivery is a condition of perfection for only five real contracts; all other contracts are completed by offer and acceptance alone.

Non-Binding Contracts for Both Parties
Describing a category of contracts non-binding for both parties with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contracts that are fundamentally non-binding for both parties and apply the ruling that each party has the right to unilaterally revoke such contracts.

Core Idea
In non-binding contracts, both parties retain the right to rescind — the default is revocability.

This lesson examines a specific category of contracts that are not binding from inception for either party. Understanding this classification is crucial for a mufti when advising clients about their rights to cancel certain agreements. We will explore the definition, examples, and the legal effect that each party can withdraw at any time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Recognize that certain contracts are classified as ghayr lazim (non-binding) from the outset, meaning they do not obligate either party to continue.
2
Understand the legal effect
The hukm is that both parties have the right to revoke (الرجوع) and cancel (الإلغاء) the contract unilaterally.
3
Apply to examples
The author gives three examples: al-īdāʻ (deposit), al-iʻārah (loan for use), and al-wakālah (agency). Verify that each fits the definition of a gratuitous contract where no binding obligation is presumed.
4
Note exceptions
Remember that this default non-binding nature can change if a condition of bindingness is stipulated or if the contract has progressed to a stage where revocation would cause harm.

٤ - وعقود غير لأزمة أصلاً بحق كلا الطرفين؛ وهي التي يملك كل منهما فيها حق الرجوع والإلغاء؛ كالإيداع والإعارة والوكالة، مما تقدم بيانه في بحث اللزوم من الآثار العامة للعقود (ف ٣/٤٠ - ٥/٤٠).

Commentary
This passage identifies a specific category of contracts within the classification based on bindingness (لزوم). These contracts are not binding from the outset for either party, meaning both the party giving and the party receiving have the right to cancel the contract unilaterally. Examples include al-īdāʻ (deposit for safekeeping), al-iʻārah (loan of a benefit for free), and al-wakālah (agency). The author refers to earlier discussion (in the section on bindingness, which is part of the general effects of contracts) for more details. This is a practical distinction: in such contracts, either party can terminate at any time unless conditions make it binding.

Key Terms
عقد غير لازم (ʻaqd ghayr lāzim): A contract that is not binding from inception for either party, allowing each to rescind.

Key Takeaway
Contracts of deposit, loan for use, and agency are fundamentally non-binding for both parties, meaning each may revoke at will.

Contracts: Exchange, Donation, and Mixed Types
Classifying contracts based on rights exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify contracts into three types based on exchange of rights (commutative, gratuitous, mixed) and apply the Hanafi ruling that mixed contracts require donation capacity initially and exchange rulings upon compensation.

Core Idea
Contracts that start as gifts can end as exchanges, changing their legal rulings mid-stream.

Contracts are not all the same. Some are pure exchanges (sale), others pure gifts. A third category begins as a donation but ends as an exchange when compensation is received. The Hanafi school treats these mixed contracts with a dual regime: capacity for donation is needed at the start, but later the rulings of sale (defect, preemption) apply. This classification is essential for muftis determining validity and effects of contracts.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three categories
Understand the definitions: commutative (each gives and receives), gratuitous (pure grant), mixed (initially grant, later exchange).
The mixed category includes loan (qard), suretyship at debtor's request, and gift with condition of compensation.
2
Identify examples of pure exchange
Recognize sale, lease, and settlement for property as commutative contracts where each party gives and receives a countervalue.
These are subject to all conditions of sale, including preemption and defect options.
3
Identify pure gratuitous contracts
Recognize gift and loan for use (ʻāriyah) as contracts where one party gives without expecting a return.
Only donation capacity is required; no exchange rulings apply.
4
Analyze mixed contracts
For loan, suretyship at request, and gift with condition of compensation: note that initially they are donations, so capacity for donation is required (not from minors). When compensation is received, exchange rulings apply: the consideration is treated like a sale price—defects allow return, preemption applies if real estate.
The nature of the contract shifts upon completion of the exchange.
5
Recognize the exception
Suretyship without the debtor's request remains purely gratuitous; the surety has no right to reimbursement.
This exception is a key distinction when applying the mixed contract ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثامناً بالنظر إلى تبادل الحقوق: تصنف العقود بهذا النظر ثلاثة أصناف:
1 - عقود المعاوضات؛ وهي التي تقوم على أساس إنشاء وجانب متقابلة بين العاقدين يأخذ فيها كل من الطرفين شيئاً ويعطي في مقابله شيئاً. وذلك كالبيع والإجارة والصلح عن مال بمال.
۲ - وعقود التبرعات؛ وهي التي تقوم على أساس المنحة أو المعونة من أحد الطرفين للآخر، كالهبة والإعارة.
- وعقود تحوي معنى التبرع ابتداء والمعاوضة انتهاء؛ كالقرض والكفالة بأمر المدين والهبة بشرط العوض:
فالمقرض متبرع بإسعاف المقترض بالمال الذي يعوزه.
والكفيل بالأمر متبرع بالتزام أداء الدين عن المدين.
والواهب الذي يهب على شرط أخذ عوض عن هبته متبرع أيضاً بما يعطي.
ولكن عندما يسترد المقرض نظير ما أقرض، ويرجع الكفيل على المدين بنظير ما دفع عنه ويأخذ الواهب من الموهوب له شيئاً مقابلاً لهبته، يؤول العقد وينتهي إلى معاوضة.
ونتيجة ذلك أن تطبق على العقد في ابتدائه شرائط التبرع، وفي انتهائه أحكام المعاوضة فتشترط في هذه العقود أهلية العاقد للتبرع: فلا تقبل من القاصر مثلاً، ويجري حكم البيع في عوض الهبة فيرد بالعيب مثلاً، ويؤخذ بالشفعة إن كان عقاراً. (المجلة ٦٢٨ و ١٠٢٢). فلو كانت الكفالة بدون أمر المدين بأن تطوع شخص بالكفالة عنه بلا تكليف منه، تكون تبرعاً من كل وجه، إذ ليس للكفيل عندئذ حق الرجوع على المدين بما يدفع عن ذمته من دينه (ر الدر المختار ٤/٢٧١-٢٧٢).
Eighth: According to the exchange of rights: Contracts are classified from this perspective into three types:
1 - Contracts of exchange (commutative contracts); they are based on creating reciprocal obligations between the contracting parties, where each takes something and gives something in return. That is like sale, lease, and settlement for property in exchange for property.
2 - Gratuitous contracts; they are based on a grant or assistance from one party to the other, like gift and loan for use.
3 - Contracts that contain the meaning of a gift initially and exchange at the end; like loan (qard), suretyship at the request of the debtor, and gift conditional on compensation:
The lender is a donor by helping the borrower with money he needs.
The surety (at the request) is a donor by undertaking payment of the debt on behalf of the debtor.
The donor who gives a gift on condition of receiving compensation is also a donor with what he gives.
But when the lender recovers the equivalent of what he lent, the surety recovers from the debtor the equivalent of what he paid, and the donor takes something from the donee in return for his gift, the contract culminates and ends as an exchange.
Consequently, at its inception, the conditions of a gift apply, and at its end, the rulings of exchange apply. Thus, in these contracts, the capacity of the contracting party to make a gift is required: it is not accepted from a minor, for example, and the ruling of sale applies to the consideration of the gift, so it may be returned due to defect, for instance, and preemption applies if it is real estate. (Majalla 628 and 1022). If the suretyship was without the order of the debtor, i.e., a person voluntarily becomes surety without being requested, it is a gift from every aspect, since the surety then has no right to recourse against the debtor for what he paid from his debt. (Radd al-Muhtar 4/271-272).
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of contracts based on the nature of the rights exchanged between the parties. There are three categories: (1) Commutative contracts (عقود المعاوضات) where each party gives and receives something of value, like sale and lease. (2) Gratuitous contracts (عقود التبرعات) where one party gives without return, like gift and loan for use. (3) Contracts that start as gratuitous but end as commutative, such as loan (qard), suretyship at the debtor's request, and gift with condition of compensation. The author explains that initially, these contracts are considered donations, so the capacity for donation is required (e.g., a minor cannot enter such contracts). However, when the donor receives compensation (e.g., the lender gets repaid, the surety is reimbursed, the donor gets a return gift), the contract takes on the rulings of exchange: the consideration is treated like a sale price, so it can be returned for defects and preemption applies. An exception is suretyship without the debtor's request: it remains purely gratuitous, and the surety has no right to reimbursement. This classification affects many practical rulings, like eligibility, liability for defects, and preemption.

Key Terms
عقد المعاوضة (ʻaqd al-muʻāwaḍah): A contract where each party gives and receives a countervalue, like sale.
الهبة بشرط العوض (al-hibah bi-sharṭ al-ʻiwaḍ): A gift conditional on receiving compensation; treated as a mixed contract.
كفالة بأمر المدين (kafālah bi-amr al-madīn): Suretyship at the request of the debtor; a mixed contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a contract that starts as a donation but ends as an exchange?
A) Sale
B) Gift without condition
C) Loan (qard)
D) Rental

Key Takeaway
Contracts that begin as donations but involve a return become exchanges upon receipt of consideration, requiring capacity at inception and sale-like rulings at completion.

Classification of Contracts by Guarantee and Trust in Hanafi Fiqh
Classifying contracts by liability regime

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of contracts based on liability (guarantee/trust) and apply the Hanafi rule to determine the holder's liability in a given contract.

Core Idea
Contractual possession is either guaranteed, trusted, or mixed, determining who bears risk of loss.

In Hanafi fiqh, contracts are not just about transfer of ownership or permissibility; they also determine who is liable for property in hand. This classification is crucial for muftis when determining compensation for lost or damaged property. This passage from Zarqa's introduction presents the threefold classification with examples, forming a foundational qawida for muamalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the three categories: guarantee contracts, trust contracts, and dual-effect contracts. Understand the basis of each.
Guarantee = holder bears all risk; Trust = holder only liable for negligence; Dual = mixed.
2
Memorize the examples under each category
Learn the contracts listed under each type: guarantee (sale, partition, etc.), trust (deposit, partnership, etc.), dual-effect (lease, pledge, etc.).
These examples are from the Hanafi school; other schools may differ in classification.
3
Apply the classification to a new contract
Given a contract not listed (e.g., contract of sale with a condition), determine which category it falls into based on whether the property is guaranteed or held as trust.
Focus on the nature of possession and transfer of risk.
4
Explain the dual-effect contracts
For lease, pledge, and sulh, explain how both guarantee and trust coexist: the asset is trust, but the benefits (or the right to sell) are guaranteed.
Use the lease example: the leased item is trust, but the lessee guarantees the rent even if not used.
5
Connect to fatwa practice
As a mufti, when asked about liability for lost property, first classify the contract. Then apply the appropriate liability rule.
This classification is a dabt (sub-maxim) that constrains the broader principle of liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تاسعاً) بالنظر إلى الضمان وعدمه: تصنف العقود بهذا النظر ثلاثة أصناف:
۱ - عقود ضمان؛ وهي التي يعتبر المال المنتقل، بناء على تنفيذها، من يد إلى يد مضموناً على الطرف القابض له فمهما يصيبه من تلف فما دونه ولو بآفة سماوية، يكون على مسؤوليته وحسابه. وهذه العقود هي: البيع والقسمة والصلح عن مال بمال والمخارجة، والقرض وإقالة هذه العقود.
۲ - وعقود أمانة؛ وهي التي يكون المال المقبوض في تنفيذها أمانة في يد قابضه لحساب صاحبه فلا يكون القابض مسؤولاً عما يصيبه من تلف فما دون إلا إذا تعدى عليه أو قصر في حفظه. وهذه العقود هي الإيداع والإعارة والشركة بأنواعها، والوكالة والوصاية.
- وعقود مزدوجة الأثر؛ فتنشىء الضمان من وجه، والأمانة من وجه. وهذه العقود هي الإجارة والرهن والصلح عن مال بمنفعة. فالإجارة يعتبر فيها المال المأجور أمانة في يد المستأجر، لكن منافعه المعقود على استيفائها مضمونة على المستأجر بمجرد تمكنه من استيفائها. فلو ترك المأجور دون أن ينتفع به حتى مضت مدة الإجارة يكون ما فات من المنافع فائتاً على حسابه وتلزمه الأجرة التي هي قيمة تلك المنافع (المجلة ٤٧٠).
Ninth: According to guarantee and its absence: Contracts are classified from this perspective into three types:
1 - Guarantee contracts; they are those in which the property transferred, based on their execution, from hand to hand is guaranteed upon the receiving party, so whatever befalls it of loss or damage, even by a heavenly calamity, is on his responsibility and account. These contracts are: sale, partition, settlement for property in exchange for property, mukhārajah (a settlement to avoid interest), loan, and rescission (iqālah) of these contracts.
2 - Trust contracts; they are those in which the property received in their execution is a trust in the hand of the receiver on behalf of its owner, so the receiver is not responsible for whatever befalls it of loss or damage unless he commits transgression or negligence in its preservation. These contracts are: deposit, loan for use, partnership of all types, agency, and guardianship.
3 - Contracts with dual effect; they create guarantee from one aspect and trust from another. These contracts are: lease, pledge (rahn), and settlement for property in exchange for a benefit (sulh ʻan māl bi manfaʻah). In lease, the leased property is considered a trust in the hand of the lessee, but its benefits contracted for enjoyment are guaranteed upon the lessee merely by his ability to enjoy them. So if the lessee leaves the leased property without using it until the lease period expires, then the benefits that have passed are lost on his account and he is obliged to pay the rent, which is the value of those benefits (Majalla 470).
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage classifies contracts based on the liability (ضمان) regime for property held by one party. There are three categories. (1) Guarantee contracts (عقود ضمان): The property is guaranteed, meaning the holder bears the risk of loss or destruction for any reason, even acts of God. Examples include sale (the buyer bears risk after taking possession), partition, settlement for property, mukhārajah, loan (the borrower guarantees the loaned money), and rescission of these contracts. (2) Trust contracts (عقود أمانة): The property is held as a trust; the holder is not liable unless he is negligent or exceeds bounds. Examples: deposit, loan for use, partnership, agency, guardianship. (3) Dual-effect contracts: They combine both aspects. For example, in a lease, the physical asset (عين) is a trust in the lessee's hands, but the benefits (منافع) are guaranteed; if the lessee fails to use the property, he still owes the rent because the ability to use was his. Similarly, in pledge, the pledged item is a trust for the pledgor, but the creditor can sell it in default; in sulh for a benefit, the property is a trust but the benefit is guaranteed. This classification affects legal rules regarding liability for loss, insurance, and compensation.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability for loss or damage of property in one's possession
أمانة (amānah): Trust; property held without liability unless negligence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which type of contract is the property held as a trust, so the holder is not liable for loss unless negligent?
A) Sale
B) Loan (qard)
C) Deposit (wadi'ah)
D) Lease

Key Takeaway
The liability for property in contractual relationships depends on the contract type: guarantee (full liability), trust (liability only for negligence), or dual-effect (mixed).

Classification of Contracts by Guarantee and Trust: The Principle of Exchange
Classification of contracts by guarantee and trust with a unifying principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim that guarantee in contracts traces exchange to classify any given contract as guarantee, trust, or mixed.

Core Idea
Guarantee follows exchange: where there is exchange, there is guarantee, even if only ultimately.

Contracts in Hanafi fiqh are not all treated alike regarding liability. Some make the recipient a guarantor (damin), others a trustee (amin). How do we know which is which? Mustafa al-Zarqa, in his Madkhal, distills a powerful principle: guarantee orbits around exchange. Once you see the exchange, you identify the guarantee. This lesson unpacks that classification and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The text classifies contracts into guarantee contracts, trust contracts, and mixed contracts. Make sure you know the characteristic of each: in guarantee, the recipient bears all loss; in trust, only negligence; mixed combines both.
Examples are given for each type.
2
Understand the distinguishing principle
The principle states that guarantee in a contract revolves with exchange (المعاوضة), even if ultimately. Exchange means each party gives something to receive something else. Where exchange is present, the recipient guarantees what he receives.
This is the core maxim (قاعدة فقهية).
3
Apply to pure guarantee contracts
Contracts like sale (بيع), compromise for property (صلح عن مال بمال), and loan (قرض) involve direct exchange. Therefore, the received property is guaranteed. For example, in sale, the buyer guarantees the price and the seller guarantees the goods.
4
Apply to mixed contracts
In lease (إجارة), the exchange is between rent and usufruct, not the asset itself. Thus the asset is trust, but the usufruct is guaranteed: the lessee must pay rent even if not used. Similarly in pledge (رهن), the part equal to the debt is guaranteed, the excess is trust.
The principle is applied to each side of the contract.
5
Recognize the method of derivation
The principle is derived by induction (استقراء) of rulings and extraction of their causes (علل). The text invites you to see how different contracts are coherently explained by a single principle.
This is a classic usul al-fiqh approach applied to qawaid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٧) تصنيف العقود

٦٤١

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها الفقه
والصلح عن مال بمنفعة يعتبر في حكم الإجارة كما تقدم في تقسيم العقود (ر: ف (١٨/٤٦)

وعقود
١٢/٤٧ - ضابط التمييز بين عقود الضمان وعقود الأمانة :
والمبدأ الشرعي الذي يقوم على أساسه التمييز بين عقود الضمانات والأمانات، بحسب ما يوحي به استقراء الأحكام واستنطاق عللها الفقهية في شتى المناسبات، هو أنَّ فكرة الضمان في العقد تدور مع المعاوضة فيه ولو نهاية ومآلاً. وحينئذ يكون العقد عقد ضمان في الناحية التي تتعلق بها المعاوضة. ويكون القابض ضامناً في هذه الناحية. أما فيما سوى ذلك فيعتبر العقد عقد أمانة ؛ وتنفيذه بالتسليم والتسلم يجعل القابض أميناً على المال المقبوض .

أ - أن عقد البيع يعتبر عقد ضمان مطلقاً. وكذا عقد الصلح عن مال بمال. ذلك لأن قبض المال المعقود عليه فيهما إنما هو قبض على أساس الاستيفاء لما استحقه القابض في مقابل عوض.
وكذلك عقد القرض، لأن المال إنما يقبض فيه امتلاكاً على أساس الإيفاء برد مثله عوضاً عنه فهو معاوضة انتهاء ومالاً كما تقدم.
ب - أما عقد الإجارة فإنّ المعاوضة فيه إنما هي بين الأجرة والمنفعة دون عين المأجور وليس تسليم العين إلا وسيلة ضرورية لاستيفاء منفعتها .
Chapter (47): Classification of Contracts

Guarantee without the debtor's order: that a person voluntarily becomes a guarantor for him without being asked, is a donation from every aspect, because the guarantor then has no right of recourse against the debtor for what he pays from his debt. (See al-Durr al-Mukhtar 4/271-272).

11/47 - Ninth: With respect to guarantee and its absence:
Contracts are classified from this perspective into three types:
1 - Guarantee contracts: those in which the transferred property, upon execution, is considered guaranteed in the hand of the recipient, so whatever loss or damage befalls it, even from a heavenly affliction, is on his responsibility and account.
These contracts are: sale, division, compromise for property in exchange for property, sharing of debts, loan, and rescission of these contracts.
2 - Trust contracts: those in which the property received in execution is a trust in the hand of the recipient for the account of its owner, so the recipient is not liable for any loss or damage unless he transgresses or is negligent in its preservation.
These contracts are: deposit, loan for use, partnerships of all types, agency, and guardianship.
3 - Contracts with dual effect: they create guarantee from one aspect and trust from another.
These contracts are: lease, pledge, and compromise for property in exchange for a usufruct. In lease, the leased property is considered a trust in the hand of the lessee, but its usufructs contracted for are guaranteed upon the lessee merely by his ability to take them. So if he leaves the leased property without using it until the lease period passes, the lost usufructs are lost on his account and he is obligated to pay the rent which is the value of those usufructs (Majalla 470).

[PAGE_609]
Book Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Fiqh
And compromise for property in exchange for a usufruct is considered like lease as previously mentioned in the classification of contracts (see: para 46/18).
The property that one of the parties to the compromise gives so that its usufruct during a specified period becomes the subject of the compromise is considered like the leased property in the hand of the lessee. Its usufructs pass as guaranteed on the account of the recipient, so with their passing he is considered to have received the consideration of the compromise. As for the property itself, it is a trust in his hand.
And the pledged property: an amount equivalent to the debt is guaranteed from it upon the pledgee, and the excess is a trust in his hand as previously mentioned (see: para 46/21).

Contracts
12/47 - The principle of distinction between guarantee contracts and trust contracts:
The legal principle upon which the distinction between guarantee contracts and trust contracts is based, according to what is suggested by the induction of rulings and the extraction of their juristic causes on various occasions, is that the idea of guarantee in a contract revolves with the exchange in it, even if ultimately and as a final outcome. Then the contract is a guarantee contract in the aspect that relates to exchange, and the recipient is a guarantor in that aspect. As for other than that, the contract is considered a trust contract; and its execution by delivery and receipt makes the recipient a trustee over the received property.
Deriving from this principle, we see:
A - The contract of sale is considered an absolute guarantee contract, and likewise the contract of compromise for property in exchange for property. That is because the receipt of the contracted property in both is receipt on the basis of taking what the recipient is entitled to in exchange for a countervalue.
Likewise the contract of loan, because the property is received therein for ownership on the basis of returning its equivalent as compensation, so it is an exchange ultimately and in terms of property, as previously mentioned.
B - As for the contract of lease, the exchange therein is between the rent and the usufruct, not the leased asset itself, and delivery of the asset is only a necessary means to take the usufruct.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage is from Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm, a modern introduction to Islamic jurisprudence. It classifies contracts from the perspective of guarantee (ضمان) and trust (أمانة). The author first introduces the classification in three categories: guarantee contracts, trust contracts, and mixed contracts. He then provides a detailed principle (ضابط) for distinguishing between them: the idea of guarantee in a contract revolves around the element of exchange (المعاوضة), even if only ultimately. That is, wherever there is a reciprocal exchange of values, the recipient bears the risk of loss for what he receives in exchange. In sale, compromise for property, and loan, the exchange is inherent, so they are pure guarantee contracts. In lease, the exchange is between rent and usufruct, not the asset itself, so the asset is a trust, but the usufruct is guaranteed. Similarly, in pledge and compromise for usufruct, the asset is a trust for any excess beyond the debt or consideration. The principle is derived by induction of rulings and extracting their 'illah (cause). The author applies it to various contracts, showing how the classification follows logically. This principle is important in Hanafi fiqh for determining liability in contractual relationships.

Key Terms
عقد ضمان ('aqd daman): A contract where the property transferred is guaranteed upon the recipient, who bears loss even from acts of God
عقد أمانة ('aqd amanah): A contract where property is held as a trust, and the holder is only liable for negligence or transgression
عقد مزدوج الأثر ('aqd muzdawij al-athar): A contract that combines both guarantee and trust aspects in different respects
معاوضة (mu'awadah): Exchange of reciprocal values between parties

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle stated in the text, how is the contract of lease (إجارة) classified?
A) It is a pure guarantee contract because the lessee pays rent.
B) It is a pure trust contract because the leased asset is returned.
C) It is a mixed contract: the asset is a trust, but the usufruct is guaranteed.
D) It is a guarantee contract only for the rent, not for the asset.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that guarantee in a contract follows the existence of exchange allows jurists to classify contracts and determine liability in a systematic way.

The Guarantee-Trust Distinction: The Principle of Compensation
Defines the legal maxim distinguishing guarantee from trust contracts with applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi qawida distinguishing guarantee and trust contracts, apply it to various contracts, and identify when an exception occurs.

Core Idea
Guarantee arises from compensation; where there is no compensation, there is trust.

This lesson presents a foundational Hanafi legal principle: whether a contract is one of guarantee (daman) or trust (amanah) depends on the presence of compensation (mu'awada). Mustafa al-Zarqa, the author, derives this maxim from an inductive study of rulings and illustrates it through several contract types. Understanding this principle is essential for a mufti to correctly determine liability for property loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
Read the Arabic statement: 'The idea of guarantee in a contract revolves around compensation in it, even if ultimately and eventually.' Understand that compensation is the sole criterion.
2
Understand the criterion (mu'awada)
Mu'awada means an exchange of countervalues. Where the receipt of property is in exchange for something else (directly or indirectly), the receiver is a guarantor. Otherwise, the receiver is a trustee.
3
Apply to pure guarantee contracts
Contracts where compensation is inherent: sale (bay'), settlement for property (sulh), and loan (qard). In each, the receiver gets the property in exchange for a countervalue, so it is guarantee absolutely.
In qard, the borrower must return an equivalent, making it compensation ultimately.
4
Apply to hybrid contracts (ijara, rahn)
In lease (ijara), compensation exists between rent and benefit, but not the leased object itself. Thus, ijara is guarantee regarding rent/benefit but trust regarding the object. In mortgage (rahn), the mortgaged property is guarantee up to the debt (compensation ultimately) but trust for any excess.
The author reduces the security element in rahn to ultimate compensation.
5
Apply to pure trust contracts
Contracts like loan for use (i'ara), agency (wakala), and partnership (sharika) have no compensation. The receiver holds property as a trustee, not liable without negligence.
This is supported by the text from al-Hidayah stating the partner's hand is a trust hand.

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Fiqh. And sulh (compromise) for a benefit in exchange for property is considered like ijarah (lease) as previously discussed in the division of contracts (see: vol. 18 p. 46). So the property that one of the two contracting parties gives so that its benefit during a specific period becomes a substitute (badal) in the sulh contract is considered like the leased object in the hand of the lessee. Thus its benefits pass as guaranteed (madhmun) against the receiver, so he is considered by the passage of time as having received the substitute of the sulh. As for the property itself, it is a trust (amanah) in his hand. And the mortgaged property is guaranteed from the mortgagee to the extent of the debt, and the excess is a trust in his hand as previously discussed (see: vol. 21 p. 46). And contracts. 12/47 - The discerning principle between guarantee contracts and trust contracts: Trusts, according to meaning. And the legal principle upon which the distinction between guarantee contracts and trust contracts is based, as suggested by the induction of rulings and the elicitation of their juristic causes (ilal) on various occasions, is that the idea of guarantee (daman) in a contract revolves around compensation (mu'awada) in it, even if ultimately and eventually. Then the contract is a guarantee contract in the aspect related to compensation, and the receiver is a guarantor in this aspect. As for anything else, the contract is considered a trust contract; and its execution by delivery and receipt makes the receiver a trustee (amin) over the received property. Deriving from this principle, we see: (a) The contract of sale is considered a guarantee contract absolutely. Likewise, the contract of sulh for property in exchange for property. That is because the receipt of the contracted property in both is a receipt on the basis of receiving what the receiver is entitled to in exchange for a countervalue. Likewise, the contract of loan (qard), because the property is received for ownership on the basis of returning its equivalent in exchange for it; it is compensation ultimately and in terms of property, as previously discussed. (b) As for the contract of ijarah, compensation therein is between the rent and the benefit, not the leased object itself, and delivery of the object is only a necessary means to receive its benefit. So ijarah is considered a guarantee contract with respect to the rent and the benefit because they are the subject and object of compensation; the lessor is a guarantor for the rent he receives, and the lessee is a guarantor for the benefits that come under his control. As for the leased object in the hand of the lessee, the contract regarding it is a trust contract. Likewise, the contract of mortgage (rahn): receipt of the mortgaged property is a receipt on the basis of receiving (istifa') when necessary, alongside the meaning of securing (istithaq) as previously discussed (see: vol. 3 p. 19). And receipt here is compensation. But the receipt from the mortgaged property is limited to the amount of the debt, so the excess of the mortgaged property is a trust, since there is no compensation regarding it. (c) As for contracts of i'ara (loan for use), wakala (agency), sharika (partnership) and the like, they are completely devoid of the meaning of compensation. Thus they are considered trust contracts. The property of the principal in the hand of his agent, the property of the minor in the hand of his guardian, the property of the partner in the hand of his co-partner, and the loaned object in the hand of the borrower—all of that is a pure trust, not guaranteed by the contract, like wadi'a (deposit). (1) In the last discussion of sharikat al-'inan from al-Hidayah, it is stated: "The hand of the partner over the property is a trust hand because he receives the property with the permission of the owner, not on the basis of substitution or security, so it is like a deposit." (See: al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir, vol. 5 p. 404). So by its implication, it indicates that the guaranteed receipt (qabd madmun) is that which occurs over the property of another without his permission, like the receipt of a usurped object, or occurs as execution of a contract of exchange (mu'awada) like receipt of a sold item, or for the purpose of security like receipt of a mortgaged item. We have found in this text specifically and in the established rulings for each contract in general, the basis for this principle we mentioned for distinguishing between guarantee contracts and trust contracts. Since our discussion is about guarantee contracts, not about guaranteed receipt, there is no need to mention permission and its absence. And we have seen the possibility of doing without the cause of security (al-tawthiq) by relying on the cause of compensation (al-mu'awada), because the purpose of mentioning security is to explain the guarantee of mortgage and what is like it, and that can be reduced to what is in mortgage of the meaning of compensation ultimately, as we have clarified.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim (qawida) that distinguishes guarantee contracts (عقود الضمان) from trust contracts (عقود الأمانة). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, derives the maxim from inductive study of fiqh rulings: the idea of guarantee in a contract revolves around compensation (mu'awada), even if only ultimately. Where compensation exists, the contract is one of guarantee; otherwise, it is one of trust. The author then applies the maxim to several contracts: (a) Sale (bay') and settlement (sulh) for property are pure guarantee because receipt is for compensation. Loan (qard) is also guarantee because repayment of the equivalent is compensation. (b) Lease (ijara) is guarantee regarding rent and benefit, but the leased object itself is trust. Mortgage (rahn) is guarantee to the extent of the debt, and the excess is trust. (c) Contracts like loan for use (i'ara), agency (wakala), and partnership (sharika) are pure trust because they lack compensation. The author also references al-Hidayah to support the principle, noting that the hand of a partner is a trust hand. He argues that the cause of compensation is sufficient to explain all cases, including mortgage, where security can be reduced to ultimate compensation.

Key Terms
عقد الضمان ('aqd al-daman): A contract where the receiver is liable for the property because the contract involves compensation (mu'awada).
عقد الأمانة ('aqd al-amanah): A contract where the receiver is a trustee (amin) and not liable, because the contract lacks compensation.
المعاوضة (al-mu'awada): Exchange of countervalues; compensation is the criterion for distinguishing guarantee from trust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sale (bay')
B) Lease (ijara)
C) Mortgage (rahn)
D) Loan (qard)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'Guarantee revolves around compensation' is the decisive tool for classifying any contract as guarantee or trust, regardless of possession.

The Distinction Between Contracts of Guarantee and Trust
Classifying contracts by the presence of consideration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given contract as a contract of guarantee (daman) or trust (amanah) using the Hanafi criterion of mu'awadah (consideration).

Core Idea
Mu'awadah (consideration) determines whether a contract creates liability (daman) or trust (amanah).

This lesson introduces a fundamental Hanafi framework for categorizing contracts. Rather than memorizing each contract individually, the jurists derived a unifying principle: the presence or absence of consideration (mu'awadah) dictates whether possession under the contract is guaranteed or trusted. We will trace this principle across several contracts including ijarah, rahn, and wakalah, drawing on the seminal text of Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and highlight the two overarching categories: 'aqd damān (guarantee contract) and 'aqd amānah (trust contract). Understand that these categories describe the legal nature of possession under the contract.
The passage uses the terms 'عقد ضمان' and 'عقد أمانة'.
2
Understand the criterion: mu'awadah
The author repeatedly ties the classification to the existence of mu'awadah (consideration). For example, in ijarah, the rent and benefit are subject to consideration, making them daman; the leased object itself is not, so it is amanah. Extract this causal link.
The illah (effective cause) is the presence of mu'awadah.
3
Apply the criterion to each contract
The passage explicitly applies the rule to ijarah, rahn, ariyah, wakalah, and shirkah. For each, state whether it is daman or amanah with respect to specific elements, and justify using the criterion.
In rahn, the debt portion is daman due to eventual consideration; the excess is amanah.

وقد وجدنا في هذا النص خاصة وفي الأحكام المقررة لكل عقد بوجه عام، مستند هذا الضابط الذي ذكرناه للتمييز بين عقود الضمان وعقود الأمانة. وبما أن بحثنا عن عقد الضمان لا عن قبض الضمان فلا حاجة إلى ذكر الإذن وعدمه. وقد رأينا إمكان الاستغناء بعلة المعاوضة» عن علة التوثيق»، لأن المقصود بذكر التوثيق تعليل ضمان الرهن وما في حكمه، وهذا يمكن رده إلى ما في الرهن من معنى المعاوضة مآلاً كما أوضحناه .
Chapter (47): Classification of Contracts. Ijarah is considered a contract of guarantee with respect to the rent and the benefit because they are the subject and object of consideration, and the lessor is guarantor for the rent he receives, and the lessee is guarantor for the benefits that come under his control. As for the leased object itself in the hands of the lessee, the contract with respect to it is a contract of trust. Similarly, the contract of rahn: the seizure of the mortgaged item is a seizure on the basis of fulfillment when required, alongside the meaning of securing, as previously mentioned (f 3/19). Fulfillment here is consideration. But fulfillment from the mortgaged item is limited to the amount of the debt, so the excess of the mortgaged item is trust, since there is no consideration with respect to it. C - As for contracts of ariyah, wakalah, shirkah, and the like, they are completely free of the meaning of consideration. Thus they are considered contracts of trust, and the property of the principal in the hands of his agent, the property of the minor in the hands of his guardian, the property of the partner in the hands of his partner, and the borrowed item in the hands of the borrower, all of that is pure trust, not guaranteed by virtue of the contract, like wadi'ah (1). (1) It came at the end of the discussion of shirkat al-'inan in al-Hidayah: 'The partner's hand over the property is a trust hand, because he took possession of the property with the owner's permission, not on the basis of exchange or security, so it becomes like wadi'ah.' (R) al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir 5/404). Thus, by its implication, it indicates that guaranteed possession is that which occurs over the property of another without his permission, like possession of a usurped item, or occurs in execution of a contract of exchange, i.e., consideration, like possession of a sold item, or by way of security, like possession of a mortgaged item. We have found in this text specifically and in the established rulings for each contract in general, the basis for this principle we have mentioned for distinguishing between contracts of guarantee and contracts of trust. Since our discussion is about the contract of guarantee, not about guaranteed possession, there is no need to mention permission or its absence. We have seen the possibility of dispensing with the 'cause of consideration' over the 'cause of security', because the purpose of mentioning security is to explain the guarantee of rahn and similar cases, and this can be traced back to the meaning of consideration in rahn eventually, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage establishes a key rasm rule for classifying contracts as either daman (guarantee/liability) or amanah (trust). The author uses the presence or absence of mu'awadah (consideration) as the decisive criterion. In ijarah, the rental payment and the benefit are subject to consideration, so the contract is daman for those elements; however, the leased object itself is amanah because it is not exchanged. Similarly, in rahn, the debt is secured by the mortgaged item, but any excess value beyond the debt is amanah. Contracts like ariyah, wakalah, and shirkah lack consideration entirely, so they are pure amanah. The author cites a passage from al-Hidayah to reinforce that possession with permission and without exchange or security is a trust hand. He refines the rule by noting that the underlying cause is mu'awadah alone, and the concept of security (tawthiq) can be subsumed under it when tracing the ultimate outcome.

Key Terms
عقد ضمان (‘aqd damān): A contract where the subject matter is subject to consideration (mu'awadah), making the party liable for loss or compensation.
عقد أمانة (‘aqd amānah): A contract where there is no consideration, and the property in hand is not guaranteed; it is held as a trust.
معاوضة (mu‘āwaḍah): The exchange of something for something else; consideration.
قبض مضمون (qabḍ maḍmūn): Possession that creates liability for the possessor, either due to lack of permission (ghasb) or due to a contract of exchange or security.
قبض أمانة (qabḍ amānah): Possession that is trusted, without liability for loss except due to negligence, as in wakalah or ariyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is an ijarah contract considered a contract of guarantee (daman) with respect to the rental payment and the benefit?
A) Because the lessor and lessee are both liable for each other's actions.
B) Because the rental payment and benefit are the subject of consideration (mu'awadah).
C) Because ijarah is a continuous contract requiring time.
D) Because the leased object itself is guaranteed.

Key Takeaway
Contracts are classified as daman or amanah based on the presence of mu'awadah; this principle unifies the rulings of ijarah, rahn, wakalah, and other contracts.

Continuing Contracts as Renewed Contracts
Defines the rule for continuing contracts and its application to defects in ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that a continuing exchange contract is treated as a series of renewed contracts, and derive the rule for defects in ijarah vs. spot sales.

Core Idea
A continuing contract is treated as instant-by-instant renewals, so a later defect becomes an old defect for future periods.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi legal maxim: a continuing exchange contract (aqd al-mu'awadah al-mustamir) is considered in fiqh as a series of renewed contracts over its duration. This principle is essential for understanding the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) in contracts like ijarah, where a defect that arises during the tenancy is treated as a pre-existing defect for the remaining term, giving the lessee the right to rescind. In contrast, in a spot sale, only defects present at the time of contract give the buyer a right to rescind.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Identify the Arabic maxim: عقد المعاوضة المستمر يعتبر في النظر الفقهي بمثابة عقود متجددة في المدة التي يستغرقها تنفيذه. Understand its meaning: a continuous exchange contract is seen as a series of momentary contracts.
This is a wusta (intermediate) maxim, applicable to contracts like ijarah.
2
Identify the scope
Determine which contracts fall under this maxim. Primarily ijarah (leasing/hiring) and other continuous exchange contracts where performance spans time.
Spot sales (bay' al-fawri) are not included.
3
Apply to ijarah defect case
In a lease, if a defect arises in the leased object during the tenancy, treat the contract as renewed each moment. Thus, the defect exists before the 'new' contract period, so it is considered a pre-existing defect for the future.
Result: the lessee has the right to rescind for a defect that arises after the start of the lease.
4
Contrast with spot sale
In a spot sale, the contract is not treated as renewed over time. Therefore, a defect arising after delivery is not grounds for rescission; only defects existing before delivery matter.
This highlights the practical difference driven by the maxim.
5
Derive the practical ruling
For fatwa: in ijarah, a lessee may rescind due to a defect that arises during the tenancy, just as for an old defect. In a sale, the buyer's right to rescind is limited to pre-existing defects.
This ruling is based on the maxim and supported by Hanafi sources (Majalla, Hidayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
فباعتبار التجدد في عقد الإجارة مع آناء الزمن يصبح العيب الحادث في المأجور عيباً قديماً بالنسبة إلى المستقبل.
- ان عقد المعاوضة المستمر يعتبر في النظر الفقهي بمثابة عقود متجددة في المدة التي يستغرقها تنفيذه.
وعلى هذا كان عقد الإجارة يحق للمستأجر فسخه بطروء العيب الحادث على المأجور وهو في يده، كما يحق له فسخه بظهور العيب القديم فيه. وهذا بخلاف عقد البيع الفوري، فإنه لا يحق للمشتري فسخه بالعيب الحادث في المبيع بعد قبضه إياه بل بالعيب القديم الموجود فيه قبل التسليم. (ر: المجلة/ 337 و الهداية وشرحها 16/8-17 ورد المحتار 9/0).
Sixth Chapter: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence
Thus, considering the renewal in the contract of ijarah with each moment of time, a defect that occurs in the leased object becomes an old defect with respect to the future.
- A continuing exchange contract is considered in fiqh as a series of renewed contracts over the period of its execution.
On this basis, in a contract of ijarah, the lessee has the right to rescind due to a defect that arises in the leased object while it is in his possession, just as he has the right to rescind upon discovery of an existing old defect. This is contrary to a spot sale contract, where the buyer cannot rescind due to a defect that arises in the sold item after taking possession, but only due to an old defect that existed before delivery.
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage presents a key fiqhi principle: a continuing exchange contract (like ijarah) is juristically treated as a series of instantaneous contracts renewed with each time unit. The author explains the practical consequence: in ijarah, if a defect arises in the leased property during the tenancy, it is considered an 'old defect' with respect to future periods, giving the lessee the right to rescind—just as he could rescind for a pre-existing defect. In contrast, in a spot sale (bay' al-fawri), the buyer can only rescind for defects existing at the time of sale, not for subsequent ones. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for understanding the rules of khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) in different contract types. The reference to Majalla and Hidayah indicates this is a well-established principle.

Key Terms
عقد المعاوضة المستمر (aqd al-mu'awadah al-mustamir): A contract of exchange that is executed over a period of time, such as ijarah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the defect is considered part of the contract at formation
B) Because the contract is treated as renewed with each moment, so the defect becomes an old defect for future
C) Because the lessee owns the leased object
D) Because the lessor is always responsible for any defect

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a continuing exchange contract (like ijarah) is treated as a series of renewed contracts, which gives the lessee the right to rescind for defects that arise during the term.

Classification of Contracts: Original vs Subsidiary
Classification and critique of contract definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi classification of contracts as original or subsidiary and apply the correct definition of subsidiary contracts to cases involving rahn and kafalah.

Core Idea
A subsidiary contract depends on a right, not necessarily a prior contract.

Contracts in Islamic law are classified in multiple ways. One key distinction is between contracts that stand independently and those that exist only to secure a right. This lesson explores that classification, with a focus on the Hanafi definition of subsidiary contracts and a critical examination of a narrower definition proposed by some modern scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that contracts are divided into original (independent) and subsidiary (dependent). List examples of each from the passage.
Original contracts: sale, ijarah, deposit, loan. Subsidiary: rahn, kafalah.
2
Define the subsidiary contract
Extract the author's definition: a subsidiary contract is one that is subsidiary to a 'right' (ḥaqq), not necessarily to another contract. Note the key term: 'تبعي لحق آخر'.
Contrast with Sanhuri's definition: 'تابع لعقد أصلي'.
3
Examine the examples
Analyze how rahn and kafalah function. They require an underlying right, which may be present, expected, or future. See the cases of rahn against a promised debt and kafalah for a future sale.
These examples demonstrate the flexibility of the Hanafi view.
4
Analyse the critique
Identify the three counterexamples presented against Sanhuri's definition. Understand why each shows that subsidiary contracts can exist without a prior original contract.
1) Rights from torts, 2) rights from contracts but separate from the contract itself, 3) rights that arise later.
5
Synthesize the correct definition
Consolidate the Hanafi position: a subsidiary contract is any contract that is accessory to a right, regardless of the source of that right or whether the right is present or expected.
This definition is broader and more accurate for fatwa.

Source Text
