(ثاني عشر) بالنظر إلى الأصلية والتبعية:
تصنف العقود بهذا النظر صنفين أيضاً :
أ - عقود أصلية؛ وهي كل عقد يكون مستقلاً في وجوده غير مرتبط بأمر آخر على سبيل التبعية له في الوجود والزوال. وذلك كالبيع، والإجارة والإيداع والإعارة إلخ...
ب - وعقود تبعية؛ وهي كل عقد يكون تابعاً لحق آخر ومرتبطاً في وجوده وزواله كاتصال الفرع بأصله وذلك كالرهن، والكفالة.
فالرهن والكفالة كلاهما توثيق لغيره، فلا ينعقدان ابتداءً إذا لم يكن في مقابلهما حق آخر ثابت أو متوقع.
وينعقد الرهن في مقابل دين موعود كما لو وعد إنسان آخر بأن يقرضه مبلغاً، وأخذ منه رهناً سَلَفاً في مقابله.
وتنعقد الكفالة في مقابل ما سيثبت من دين؛ كما لو قال لآخر: بايع فلاناً، وما ثبت لك عليه فأنا كفيل به (ر) الدر 252/2 و 298).
وكما لا ينعقدان ابتداءً إلا في مقابل حق آخر، يزولان بزوال ذلك الحق المقابل كما يسقط كل فرع بسقوط أصله. فلو أبرأ الدائن المدين الأصيل سَقَطت الكفالة أيضاً عن الكفيل تبعاً لسقوط الدين. ومثل ذلك يقال في الرهن فإنه يبطل بإبراء الدائن المدين (1).
(1) جاء في كتاب نظرية العقد للأستاذ العلامة السنهوري (ف/ 150) تعريف العقد التبعي بأنه هو العقد الذي يكون تابعاً لعقد أصلي وجد قبله». ويتضح مما بيناه أن هذا التعريف لا يستقيم للملاحظات التالية :
1 - لأن الرهن والكفالة قد يعقدان توثيقاً لدين ناشيء عن فعل لا عن عقد أصلاً، كالكفالة والرهن في مقابل ضمان الإتلاف.
2 - ولأن الديون الناشئة عن عقد كثمن المبيع وبدل الصلح وأجرة المأجور، لا يكون الرهن والكفالة في مقابلها تابعين للعقد الذي أنشأ الحق أو الالتزام، بل تابعين للحق الناشيء بذلك العقد. ولذا لو أبرأ البائع المشتري عن الثمن يبرأ الكفيل بالثمن ويسقط الرهن المأخوذ به أيضاً بقاء عقد البيع نافذ الحكم.
3 - ولأن العقد التبعي قد تكون تبعيته لحق سيوجد بعده، وليس من الضروري أن يكون قد وُجد قبله وذلك كالرهن لقاء الدين الموعود والكفالة بما سينشأ ويثبت للمكفول له على المكفول من حقوق، على نحو ما بيناه آنفاً. فالصواب تعريف العقد التبعي كما فعلنا بأنه هو العقد الذي يكون تابعاً لحق آخر».
(Twelfth) Considering Originality and Subsidiary:
Contracts are classified by this consideration into two categories:
A - Original contracts: every contract that is independent in existence, not linked to another matter as a subsidiary in existence and termination. Examples: sale, ijarah, deposit, loan, etc.
B - Subsidiary contracts: every contract that is subsidiary to another right and linked in its existence and termination like the connection of a branch to its origin. Examples: rahn (pledge) and kafalah (suretyship).
Both rahn and kafalah are documentation for something else; they do not initially conclude unless there exists another established or expected right in return.
Rahn can be concluded against a promised debt, as when a person promises to lend another an amount and takes a pledge in advance in return.
Kafalah can be concluded against a debt that will be established; as when one says to another: 'Sell to so-and-so, and whatever right you establish against him, I am the surety for it.' (reference: al-Durr 2/252 and 298).
Just as they do not initially conclude except in return for another right, they terminate with the termination of that corresponding right, just as every branch falls with the fall of its origin. So if the creditor releases the principal debtor, the suretyship also falls from the surety as a consequence of the fall of the debt. The same applies to rahn; it becomes void by the creditor's release of the debtor. (1)
(1) In the book 'Theory of Contract' by Professor Sanhuri (p. 150), the definition of a subsidiary contract is: 'It is the contract that is subsidiary to an original contract that existed before it.' From what we have explained, it is clear that this definition is not sound for the following observations:
1. Because rahn and kafalah may be concluded as documentation for a debt arising from an act, not from a contract originally, like kafalah and rahn in return for guarantee of tort.
2. Because debts arising from a contract, such as the price of a sale, the consideration of a settlement, and the rent of a leased item, the rahn and kafalah in return for them are not subsidiary to the contract that created the right or obligation, but rather to the right arising from that contract. Hence, if the seller releases the buyer from the price, the surety for the price is also released and the rahn taken for it also falls while the sale contract remains effective.
3. Because a subsidiary contract may be subsidiary to a right that will exist later, and it is not necessary that it existed before it, as in rahn for a promised debt and kafalah for rights that will arise and be established for the beneficiary against the principal, as we explained earlier. So the correct definition of a subsidiary contract, as we have done, is: 'It is the contract that is subsidiary to another right.'
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage classifies contracts into original (independent) and subsidiary (dependent on a right). Original contracts like sale, ijarah, deposit, and loan exist on their own. Subsidiary contracts like rahn (pledge) and kafalah (suretyship) are mere security for a right; they cannot exist without an underlying right that is either present or expected. The author then critiques a definition by Sanhuri that restricts subsidiary contracts to being dependent on a prior 'original contract'. The author provides three counterexamples: (1) a debt may arise from a tort, not a contract; (2) even when debt arises from a contract, the subsidiary contract is attached to the debt, not the contract itself (e.g., releasing the debt extinguishes the rahn/kafalah even if the original contract remains); (3) the subsidiary contract can secure a right that will arise in the future (e.g., a promised loan). Therefore, the correct definition is that a subsidiary contract is subsidiary to a 'right', not necessarily a contract. This clarifies Hanafi fiqh categorization, and the critique is from a modern perspective but consistent with classical Hanafi rulings.

Key Terms
عقد تبعي (aqd taba'i): A contract that is subsidiary to another right; its existence and termination depend on that right

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, what is the correct definition of a subsidiary contract?
A) A contract that is subsidiary to a prior original contract
B) A contract that is subsidiary to a right
C) A contract that is always accessory to a sale
D) A contract that cannot exist without a written document

Key Takeaway
A subsidiary contract in Hanafi fiqh is dependent on a right, not necessarily on a prior original contract.

Averting Hudud by Shubuhat: The Hanafi Maxim
Explains principle of shubuhat and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'Avert hudud by shubuhat', define shubha, and apply it to the four examples from the passage to determine whether hadd is averted.

Core Idea
Mere plausible claim of shubha drops hadd; no proof required.

This lesson explores the foundational Hanafi penal principle that fixed punishments are averted by the slightest doubt. Al-Zarqa presents the maxim, its textual basis, and four illustrative scenarios where shubha is deemed present. Understanding this principle is essential for any mufti dealing with hadd cases, as it reflects the school's priority of mercy over punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal maxim
Locate the phrase 'ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات' in the text and state its meaning.
The maxim is directly from a hadith.
2
Define shubha as a legal cause
From the legal definition dissector, understand shubha as a plausible excuse that makes the act less blameworthy.
Shubha must be plausible (mazannah), not fanciful.
3
List the four examples
The passage gives four cases: ignorance, creditor-debtor theft, spousal theft, and theft from mahram. Write each and identify the shubha type.
Each shubha relates to a condition of the crime.
4
Apply the maxim to each example
For each case, state that hadd is averted due to the specific shubha.
Use the furuat from the qawida panel.
5
Note the Hanafi expansion
The text says mere claim of shubha suffices without proof. Understand the difference from stricter schools.
This expansion is based on the hadith and the principle of forgiveness.

(۱) رواه الترمذي في الحدود (١٤٢٤) وضعف رفعه والحاكم في المستدرك ٣٨٤/٤ وقال : صحيح الإسناد وتعقبه الذهبي. ورواه الدارقطني في سننه ٨٤/٣، والبيهقي في سننه ۲۳۸/۸ وسنده ضعيف لضعف يزيد بن زياد الشامي وصح معناه عن جماعة من الصحابة. (ر ): المقاصد الحسنة» للسخاوي ص٣٠).
Chapter (49) Disciplinary Supports: Fixed Punishments. It is required for amputation that the other limbs be sound so that he does not remain without [limbs]. And exile is for one who is captured before he commits killing or robbery. This is also the Hanafi ijtihad, and they interpreted here exile from the land as imprisonment, considering it remoteness from life in the social environment. (See: Ahkam al-Quran by Abu Bakr al-Razi al-Jassas and Tafsir al-Fakhr al-Razi). The repentance of a highway robber before capture drops the hadd penalty. But if the penalty of retaliation applies as will be explained, even if he is not a killer, he must return the stolen property or its guarantee. There are some detailed conditions known by referring to what the jurists have established in the chapter on highway robbery. Detailed. 3/49 - Conditions of Crimes That Mandate Hadd Punishments: For crimes that mandate hadd, there are numerous conditions regarding the description of each and how they occur in order for the hadd to be obligatory; ijtihads differed on them. They are in their places in fiqh books: For example, the hadd of theft includes among its conditions that the stolen item be a certain nisab of wealth, that it be taken secretly from a secure place. Thus, usurpation, open taking, and denial of a trust or loan are excluded. 4/49 - Effect of Doubts in Averting Hadd Punishments: It is also required generally for all hadd punishments that the perpetrator not have a doubt that gives him an excuse or necessitates reduction of his responsibility, such as if he committed zina, theft, or drinking wine while ignorant of prohibition, or if a creditor steals from his debtor the equivalent of his debt even if the debt is deferred, or if one spouse steals from the other, or if a person steals from one of his close relatives, because mixing among them is customary and their entering each other's houses creates a doubt regarding the condition of secure place that averts the hadd. The basis for this is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Avert the hudud by doubts (shubuhat)' and he also said: 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can. If there is a way out, let them go. Indeed, the imam erring in forgiveness is better than erring in punishment.' The meaning is that when in doubt, the side of the accused is to be considered. This is what modern criminal jurisprudence and laws have affirmed. Our jurists expanded the scope of the doubt that drops the hadd due to the indications of the narrations from the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding that; so much so that they said: merely claiming a doubt in its likely occurrence by the perpetrator of the crime mandating hadd drops the hadd without needing to prove that claimed doubt. (See: al-Durr al-Mukhtar). 49/5 - Conditions of Proof in These Crimes: There are also special conditions required for proving these crimes that mandate hadd before the judiciary; they differ from one another and exceed what is required for proving other rights. They are detailed in the discussions of testimonies in fiqh books. The crime of zina with its descriptions and conditions that mandate hadd is very difficult to prove, especially since the accuser, if unable to prove it, is considered a defamer (qadif) and is given the hadd of qadhf, unless he is a husband and persists in accusing his wife and fails to prove it; then he is not given the hadd of qadhf, but li'an takes place between them before the judge upon the woman's request, and the result of li'an is the judge's separation between the two spouses. Li'an are specific multiple oaths that the man swears before the judge on the truth of his accusation of his wife of zina, and the woman swears similar oaths on his falsehood.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage is a legal discussion from al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm' covering the Hanafi theory of hadd punishments. It begins with specific rules on highway robbery: amputation requires other limbs to be sound (to avoid total disability), and exile is re-interpreted Hanafiyyan as imprisonment. Repentance before capture drops the hadd but does not affect property restitution or retaliation rights. The text then shifts to general conditions for hadd crimes: each crime (like theft) has its own conditions (e.g., nisab, secrecy, secure place). The core of the lesson is the principle of 'shubuhat' (doubts) averting hudud. The author lists examples: ignorance of prohibition, creditor-debtor theft, spousal theft, and theft from relatives due to customary mixing. The basis is the famous hadith: 'Avert the hudud by shubuhat' and the related narration about forgiveness. The Hanafi school, following these texts, expanded the scope: mere plausible claim of a doubt by the defendant drops the hadd without requiring proof of that doubt. This is a significant leniency compared to modern strict proof standards. Finally, the text addresses evidentiary rules: hadd crimes require extraordinary proof, especially zina, which needs four witnesses or confession. Failure to prove zina as a non-husband leads to hadd of qadhf; for a husband, li'an (mutual cursing oaths) is used, resulting in divorce. This passage illustrates the Hanafi methodology of prioritising avoidance of punishment over rigid application, in line with the maxim 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat'.

Key Terms
الشبهة (al-shubhah): A doubt or ambiguous circumstance that provides an excuse or reduces responsibility, sufficient to avert hadd punishment.
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by Shariah for specific crimes (e.g., zina, theft, drinking).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position explained in this passage, what is the effect of a plausible claim of shubha by the accused in a crime requiring hadd?
A) The hadd is still applied as long as the claim is not proven.
B) The hadd is averted without needing to prove the claimed shubha.
C) The hadd is reduced to a lesser punishment.
D) The case is postponed until the claim is investigated.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mere plausible claim of a shubha by an accused person drops the hadd punishment, without requiring proof of the doubt.

Evidentiary Rules for Hadd Crimes and the Procedure of Li'an
Explains evidentiary rules for hadd and the li'an procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why proving zina is extremely difficult, distinguish the consequences for a non-husband vs. husband when accusation fails, and describe the li'an procedure and its outcome.

Core Idea
Zina requires four witnesses; failure leads to qadhf for others, but li'an for a husband.

This lesson covers the strict evidentiary requirements for hadd crimes, particularly zina. Al-Zarqa explains that proving zina is exceptionally hard, and the accuser faces severe penalties if unable to prove it. However, for a husband who accuses his wife, a special procedure called li'an (mutual oaths) replaces both the hadd of zina and the hadd of qadhf, resulting in divorce. Understanding this framework is crucial for a mufti handling marital accusations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general evidentiary burden for hadd crimes
The passage states that hadd crimes require special, stringent proof conditions beyond those for other rights. Identify the implication: hadd is difficult to prove.
This protects defendants from easy punishment.
2
Focus on zina: the proof requirement
Zina requires either four male witnesses or a confession. Recognize why this makes zina 'very difficult to prove'.
Four witnesses is an exceptionally high bar.
3
Consequence of failed proof for a non-husband accuser
If a non-husband accuses someone of zina and fails to prove it, he is guilty of qadhf (false accusation) and receives the hadd of 80 lashes.
This deters frivolous accusations.
4
The exception: a husband's accusation
If a husband accuses his wife and cannot prove it, he does not get the hadd of qadhf. Instead, li'an takes place: mutual oaths before the judge.
The marriage bond provides a special exception.
5
Describe the li'an procedure and result
Li'an consists of specific oaths by both spouses. The husband swears four times that he is truthful, then a fifth invoking God's curse if he lies. The wife swears similarly that he is lying. After li'an, the judge separates them permanently.
This ends the marriage irrevocably.

٤/٤٩ - تأثير الشبهات في درء الحدود:
ويشترط أيضاً في الحدود جميعاً بوجه عام أن لا يكون لمرتكب جريمتها شبهة تجعل له شبه معذرة أو تقتضي تخفيف مسؤوليته، كما لو زنى أو سرق أو شرب الخمر جاهلاً بالتحريم، أو سرق الدائن من مدينه ما يعادل دينه ولو كان الدين مؤجّلاً ، أو سرق أحد الزوجين من الآخر؛ أو سرَقَ الشخص من أحد أقاربه المحارم، لأن الخلطة بينهم عادة، ودخول بعضهم بيوت بعض يجعلان شبهة في تحقق شريطة الحرز تدرأ الحد .

Chapter (49) Disciplinary Supports: Fixed Punishments. It is required for amputation that the other limbs be sound so that he does not remain without [limbs]. القذف (al-qadhf): False accusation of zina, punishable by 80 lashes.

The General Criterion for Ta'zir
Defining the criterion for ta'zir with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general criterion for ta'zir and apply it to determine whether a discretionary punishment is warranted in new cases.

Core Idea
Ta'zir applies to any wrongdoing (munkar) or unjust harm, by word, deed, or gesture.

Ta'zir is a discretionary punishment, distinct from fixed hudud and qisas. The Hanafi jurists established a clear general criterion (ḍābiṭ) to define the scope of ta'zir. This lesson unpacks that criterion, illustrating its breadth through examples of personal, property, religious, and educational violations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion
Find the exact wording of the general criterion in the passage: 'Anyone who commits a wrongdoing (munkar) or causes harm to another without right, by word, by deed, or by gesture, is subject to ta'zir.' Note the three key elements: 1) committing a munkar, 2) harming another unjustly, 3) the means (word, deed, gesture).
The word 'or' indicates two alternative triggers.
2
Understand the scope of 'munkar'
The passage explains that munkar includes not only direct harms but also religious violations (e.g., mocking religion) and neglect of religious duties (e.g., failing to teach or learn obligatory knowledge). Consider how each example fits the definition.
Munkar in Hanafi fiqh covers both haram and makruh tahriman acts.
3
Apply the criterion to examples
Work through the three categories given: aggression against person/property, violation of religious sanctities, and neglect of duties. For each, confirm that they fall under 'munkar' or 'unjust harm', thus warranting ta'zir.
Verify that the examples match the criterion's wording.
4
Recognize supporting evidence
The hadith about teaching and learning illustrates that ta'zir applies even for neglect of communal duties. This shows the criterion's foundation in the Sunnah.
The hadith also demonstrates the judge's discretion in enforcing religious obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٥٠) المؤيدات التأديبية : العقوبات المفوضة إلى رأي الحكام دون تحديد ثابت فإنَّ من اللازم تحديد السبب أي تحديد الحالة الجرمية التي يستحق الإنسان بها التعزير وهذا ما فعله الفقهاء، فقد نصبوا لما يوجب التعزير ضابطاً عاماً فقالوا الضابط أن كل من ارتكب منكراً، أو أذى غيره بغير حق، بقول أو بفعل أو إشارة يلزمه التعزير .
فيدخل في عموم هذا الضابط جميع الجرائم بالنظام، سواء أكانت عدواناً من بعض الناس على بعض في النفس أو المال، كالضرب والشتم والإرهاب والتزوير وشهادة الزور والغش والاحتيال وما شاكلها؛ أو كانت انتهاكاً للحرمات الدينية وجهراً بالمعاصي، كإفطار رمضان، والاستهزاء بالدين والدعوة إلى الزندقة والإخلال بالآداب العامة، وما أشبه ذلك .
ويدخل أيضاً في ارتكاب المنكر إهمال الواجبات الدينية، ومن جملتها التعليم والتعلم. فإذا قصر العالم في واجب التعليم، أو قصر الجاهل في تعلم القدر الواجب شرعاً من العلم استحقا عقوبة التعزير على التقصير، فإن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: طلب العلم فريضة على كل مسلم (۱) .
(۱) ذكر الحافظ المنذري في الترغيب والترهيب في كتاب العلم ٨٦/١، أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم خطب ذات فأثنى على طوائف من المسلمين خيراً، ثم قال: 
ما بال أقوام لا يفقهون جيرانهم ولا يعلمونهم ولا يعظونهم ولا يأمرونهم، ولا ينهونهم؟ وما بال أقوام لا يتعلمون من جيرانهم ولا يتفقهون، ولا يتعظون؟ والله ليعلمن قوم جيرانهم ويفقهونهم ويعظونهم ويأمرونهم وينهونهم، وليتعلمن قوم من جيرانهم ويتفقهون ويتعظون أو لأعاجلنّهم العقوبة في الدنيا . فقال قوم: مَنْ ترونه عنى بهؤلاء؟ قالوا عنى الأشعريين هم قوم فقهاء، ولهم جيران جفاة من أهل المياه والأعراب فبلغ ذلك الأشعريين، فأتوا رسول الله ، فقالوا يا رسول الله ذكرت قوماً بخير وذكرتنا بشر فما بالنا؟ فأعاد عليهم إنذاره. فقالوا يا رسول الله أنفطن غيرنا ؟ فأعاد قوله عليهم فأعادوا قولهم: أنفطن غيرنا؟ فأعاد أيضاً قوله عليهم فقالوا يا رسول الله أمهلنا سنة فأمهلهم سنة ليفقهوهم، ويعلموهم، ويعظوهم. ثم قرأ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قول الله تعالى في القرآن :
Chapter (50): Disciplinary Sanctions: Penalties left to the discretion of rulers without fixed determination. So it is necessary to determine the cause, i.e., the criminal state for which a person deserves ta'zir. This is what the jurists did: they set up a general criterion for what necessitates ta'zir. They said: The criterion is that anyone who commits a wrongdoing, or causes harm to another without right, by word, by deed, or by gesture, is subject to ta'zir. Included under this criterion are all crimes against the [public] order, whether they are aggression by some people against others in person or property, such as beating, insulting, terrorism, forgery, false testimony, fraud, deception, and the like; or whether they are violations of religious sanctities and open commission of sins, such as breaking the fast of Ramadan, mocking religion, calling to heresy, offending public morals, and the like. Also included under committing wrongdoing is the neglect of religious duties, including teaching and learning. So if a scholar falls short in the duty of teaching, or an ignorant person falls short in learning what is religiously obligatory of knowledge, they deserve the punishment of ta'zir for the shortcoming, for the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Seeking knowledge is an obligation upon every Muslim.' (1) (1) Al-Hafiz al-Mundhiri mentioned in al-Targhib wa al-Tarhib, in the Book of Knowledge, that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) delivered a sermon one day, praised some groups of Muslims, then said: 'What is the matter with people who do not teach their neighbors, nor instruct them, nor admonish them, nor command them, nor forbid them? And what is the matter with people who do not learn from their neighbors, nor gain understanding, nor take admonition? By Allah, some people will surely teach their neighbors, instruct them, admonish them, command them, and forbid them, and some people will surely learn from their neighbors, gain understanding, and take admonition, or I will hasten punishment upon them in this world.' The people said: 'Whom do you mean by these?' They said: 'The Ash'aris; they are a people of understanding, and they have harsh neighbors from the people of the water sources and Bedouins.' This reached the Ash'aris, so they came to the Messenger of Allah and said: 'O Messenger of Allah, you mentioned some people with good and us with evil; what is our situation?' He repeated his warning to them. They said: 'O Messenger of Allah, have we understood?' He repeated his statement to them, and they repeated their question. Then they said: 'O Messenger of Allah, give us a year.' So he gave them a year to teach them, instruct them, and admonish them. Then the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) recited the words of Allah Almighty in the Quran:
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage defines the general criterion for ta'zir punishment in Hanafi fiqh. Ta'zir is a discretionary punishment left to the judge's opinion, unlike fixed hadd punishments. The jurists established a clear criterion: anyone who commits a wrongdoing (munkar) or harms another unjustly, whether by speech, action, or gesture, is liable for ta'zir. This criterion covers all types of violations: personal offenses like beating and insulting, property offenses like fraud and forgery, religious offenses like breaking the fast publicly, and neglect of religious obligations. The text specifically highlights the duty to teach and learn knowledge as a religious obligation; failure in this duty also warrants ta'zir. The supporting hadith emphasizes communal responsibility for education, with the Prophet threatening punishment for those who neglect it.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): A discretionary punishment left to the judge's discretion for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or kaffara.
الضابط (al-dabit): A general legal criterion or rule that determines when ta'zir is applicable.
منكر (munkar): Any act that is religiously or legally disapproved, including sins and violations of public order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general criterion for ta'zir, which of the following actions would NOT warrant ta'zir?
A) Beating someone without right
B) Breaking the fast in Ramadan secretly without witnesses
C) Failing to teach obligatory knowledge despite being able
D) Testifying falsely in court

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi criterion for ta'zir is broad: it covers all forms of munkar and unjust harm, including religious violations and neglect of duties, by word, deed, or gesture.

Partial Nullity (Tajazzuʿ al-Butlān): The Hanafi Maxim and Its Applications
Hanafi legal maxim with classical examples and modern code

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'Nullity is divisible' to determine the validity of a mixed contract and identify the exception when nullity spreads.

Core Idea
Nullity can be partial if the void part is specific and separable; otherwise, it consumes the whole contract.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi legal maxim that governs mixed contracts: nullity is divisible. While other schools treat nullity as indivisible, Hanafis allow a contract to remain valid in part if the void portion can be isolated. Understanding this maxim and its exception prepares you for fatwa drafting in complex transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Identify the Arabic formulation 'البطلان قابل للتجزؤ' and its English equivalent 'Nullity is divisible.' This is the core legal principle.
Memorize the exact Arabic for fatwa usage.
2
Recognize the condition
The void part must be specific, separable, and identifiable. If not, nullity spreads to the whole contract.
This condition distinguishes valid partial nullity from total nullity.
3
Apply to the two furūʿ
Work through the classical examples: (1) sale of personal property mixed with waqf — valid for personal, void for waqf. (2) marriage of a lawful woman and an unlawful woman in one contract — valid for lawful, void for unlawful.
Note how the price is apportioned in the sale example.
4
Identify the exception
When the void part cannot be specified (e.g., marrying two sisters in one contract), nullity covers the entire contract.
The defect is the combination itself, not an attribute of one party.
5
Connect to modern codification
The Syrian Civil Code Article 144 adopted this Hanafi principle, showing its practical relevance.
Modern legislation often relies on this maxim for partial invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تجزؤ البطلان
والبطلان قابل للتجزؤ في نظر فقهائنا، فقد يكون العقد أو التصرف الواحد بعضه باطل وبعضه معتبر بالنسبة إلى ناحية دون أخرى من محله. وذلك لأن الاعتبار الشرعي قد تتوافر شرائطه في جزء دون آخر من المحل، فلا مانع من تجزؤ الاعتبار
(۲)
فقد نص الفقهاء على أنه إذا جمع إنسان بين ماله ومال موقوف فباعهما صفقة واحدة صح البيع في ماله بحصته من الثمن، وبطل في الموقوف (ر، الدر المختار ورد المحتار في البيع ١٠٤/٤).
وكذا لو جمع في النكاح بين امرأة تحل له وأخرى لا تحل فتزوجهما بعقد واحد، صح النكاح في حق من تحل له، وبطل في حق الأخرى. أما إذا كان البطلان في بعض غير معين ولا سبيل إلى تعيينه فإن البطلان يعم؛ وذلك كما لو جمع الرجل بين أختين في عقد زواج واحد. فإن كل واحدة منهما يصح زواجها منه على انفراد وإنما الممنوع الجمع بين أختين ومن
(1) من هذا القبيل اختلاف الاجتهادات في معلومية العوضين في عقود المعاوضات كالبيع والإجارة والصلح عن مال بمال. فقد ذهب الاجتهاد الشافعي إلى أن كل جهالة في العقد تورثه بطلاناً كلياً، لأن معلومية المحل والبدل وما يتصل بهما ناحية أساسية في نظره. وذهب الاجتهاد الحنفي إلى أن معلومية المحل والعوض المقابل في العقود إنما هي ناحية فرعية لا توجب مخالفتها بطلاناً كلياً، وإنما توجب الفساد.
(۲) وبهذا النظر الفقهي في قابلية البطلان للتجزؤ جاء القانون المدني السوري الذي صدر أخيراً، إذ نصت المادة / ١٤٤ / منه على أنه :
إذا كان العقد في شق منه باطلاً أو قابلاً للإبطال، فهذا الشق وحده هو الذي يبطل، إلا إذا تبين أن العقد ما كان ليتم بغير الشق الذي وقع باطلاً أو قابلاً للإبطال، فيبطل العقد كله .
Partial Nullity
Nullity is divisible in the view of our jurists. A single contract or disposition may be void in part and valid in part with respect to one aspect of its subject matter rather than another. This is because legal consideration may have its conditions fulfilled in one part but not in another, so there is no obstacle to the division of consideration. (2) The jurists have stated that if a person combines his own property and waqf property and sells them in a single bargain, the sale is valid for his own property in proportion to its share of the price, and void for the waqf property (see al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar on sale, 4/104). Similarly, if he combines in marriage a woman who is lawful for him and another who is not lawful, and marries both in one contract, the marriage is valid for the lawful one and void for the other. However, if the nullity is in an unspecified part and there is no way to specify it, then the nullity is general; for example, if a man combines two sisters in a single marriage contract. For each of them individually, marriage to him would be valid; what is prohibited is combining two sisters. And (1) Of this kind is the difference of ijtihad regarding the knowability of the countervalues in commutative contracts such as sale, lease, and settlement for property. The Shafi'i ijtihad holds that any uncertainty in the contract renders it completely void, because knowability of the subject matter and the countervalue and what pertains to them is an essential aspect in its view. The Hanafi ijtihad holds that knowability of the subject matter and the countervalue in contracts is only a subsidiary aspect, so violating it does not cause complete nullity, but only causes irregularity (fasād). (2) And it is in accordance with this juristic view of the divisibility of nullity that the Syrian Civil Code, recently issued, came, for its Article 144 states: 'If the contract is void or voidable in a part, then that part alone is void, unless it appears that the contract would not have been concluded without the part that is void or voidable, in which case the entire contract is void.'
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim 'Nullity is divisible' (البطلان قابل للتجزؤ). It explains that a single contract can be partially void if the void portion is specific and separable. Two illustrative furūʿ (applications) are given: (1) Selling one's own property mixed with waqf property in one sale; the sale is valid for the personal property and void for the waqf. (2) Marrying a lawful woman and an unlawful woman in one contract; the marriage is valid for the lawful one, void for the other. However, if the void part cannot be identified (as in marrying two sisters in one contract, where the prohibition is against combining them but each individually is permissible), then nullity spreads to the whole contract. The footnotes add two important points: (a) A madhhab difference regarding uncertainty (jahāla) in contracts – Shafiʿis consider it an essential defect causing total nullity, while Hanafis see it as subsidiary causing only fasād. (b) The modern Syrian Civil Code adopted the Hanafi view on partial nullity in Article 144.

Key Terms
تجزؤ البطلان (tajazzuʿ al-butlān): The legal principle that nullity can be limited to a specific part of a contract, leaving the remainder valid.
جهالة (jahāla): Uncertainty or ignorance regarding essential elements of a contract (e.g., subject matter or price).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a man marries a lawful woman and a woman who is his foster sister (milk sister) in one contract?
A) Valid for both.
B) Valid for the lawful woman, void for the foster sister.
C) Entire contract void.
D) Valid for the foster sister only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim of divisible nullity strikes a balance between upholding contracts and invalidating prohibited elements, provided the void part is identifiable.

Divisibility of Nullity in Contracts: Hanafi Principle
Explanation of partial nullity with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'Nullity is divisible' to classify contracts as partially valid or void, and identify when nullity becomes total.

Core Idea
A contract can be partially valid if the void part is identifiable; otherwise nullity covers the whole.

The Hanafi school uniquely recognizes that nullity in contracts can be divisible. Where the invalid portion is specific and separable, the contract remains valid for the rest. But if the invalid part cannot be determined, the entire contract collapses. This principle has practical implications in sales and marriage, and even influenced modern civil codes.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
The Hanafi maxim 'Nullity is divisible' applies to contracts and dispositions. Identify the core ruling: a contract may be partially valid and partially void when the legal conditions are satisfied for some components but not others.
This is a dabt (sub-maxim) restricting the general rule that nullity is total.
2
Distinguish determinate vs. indeterminate nullity
If the void part is specified and separable, nullity is limited. If the void part is unknown and cannot be determined, nullity covers the entire contract.
The key factor is whether the defective component can be identified and isolated.
3
Apply to sale: mixed personal and waqf property
If a person sells his own property and waqf property as a single lot, the sale is valid for his property in proportion to its share of the price, and void for the waqf.
The price is apportionable, making the void part determinate.
4
Apply to marriage: lawful and unlawful women
If a man marries a lawful woman and an unlawful woman in one contract, the marriage is valid for the lawful woman and void for the other.
Each woman is identifiable, so the void part is determinate.
5
Apply the exception: two sisters in one contract
If a man marries two sisters in a single contract, the entire contract is void because the void part (the combination) is not attributable to one sister over the other, and there is no benefit in validating an unspecified one.
This is the exception: when indeterminacy leads to total nullity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فقد نص الفقهاء على أنه إذا جمع إنسان بين ماله ومال موقوف فباعهما صفقة واحدة صح البيع في ماله بحصته من الثمن، وبطل في الموقوف (ر، الدر المختار ورد المحتار في البيع ١٠٤/٤).
وكذا لو جمع في النكاح بين امرأة تحل له وأخرى لا تحل فتزوجهما بعقد واحد، صح النكاح في حق من تحل له، وبطل في حق الأخرى. أما إذا كان البطلان في بعض غير معين ولا سبيل إلى تعيينه فإن البطلان يعم؛ وذلك كما لو جمع الرجل بين أختين في عقد زواج واحد. فإن كل واحدة منهما يصح زواجه منها على انفراد وإنما الممنوع الجمع بين أختين ومن
(1) من هذا القبيل اختلاف الاجتهادات في معلومية العوضين في عقود المعاوضات كالبيع والإجارة والصلح عن مال بمال. فقد ذهب الاجتهاد الشافعي إلى أن كل جهالة في العقد تورثه بطلاناً كلياً، لأن معلومية المحل والبدل وما يتصل بهما ناحية أساسية في نظره. وذهب الاجتهاد الحنفي إلى أن معلومية المحل والعوض المقابل في العقود إنما هي ناحية فرعية لا توجب مخالفتها بطلاناً كلياً، وإنما توجب الفساد.
(۲) وبهذا النظر الفقهي في قابلية البطلان للتجزؤ جاء القانون المدني السوري الذي صدر أخيراً، إذ نصت المادة / ١٤٤ / منه على أنه :
إذا كان العقد في شق منه باطلاً أو قابلاً للإبطال، فهذا الشق وحده هو الذي يبطل، إلا إذا تبين أن العقد ما كان ليتم بغير الشق الذي وقع باطلاً أو قابلاً للإبطال، فيبطل
العقد كله .
في حكمهما . ولا مرجّح لتصحيح العقد في واحدة بعينها، ولا فائدة لتصحيحه في واحدة غير معينة فيبطل العقد في حقهما. وذلك بخلاف ما لو تزوجهما بعقدين على التعاقب، فإنَّ العقد الثاني عندئذ يكون هو الباطل، إلا إذا نسي الأول منهما فيبطلان كلاهما أيضاً لعدم المرجح وعدم إمكان التنفيذ مع الجهالة (ر: «الهداية وشروحها في النكاح ٣ ۱۲۳ ، و«الدرر» ۳۳۱/۱ و ۳۳۳) .
The Doctrine of Invalidity (1). 3/52 - Divisibility of Nullity: Nullity is divisible in the view of our jurists. A single contract or disposition may be partly void and partly valid with respect to one aspect of its subject matter and not another. This is because the legal consideration may have its conditions fulfilled in one part of the subject matter but not in another, so there is no impediment to the divisibility of consideration (2). The jurists have ruled: If a person combines his own property with waqf property and sells them as a single lot, the sale is valid for his property in proportion to its share of the price, and void for the waqf (see al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar on sale 4/104). Likewise, if in marriage he combines a woman lawful for him and another unlawful, marrying them with a single contract, the marriage is valid regarding the lawful one and void regarding the other. But if the void part is unspecified and there is no way to determine it, then the nullity applies to the whole; for example, if a man marries two sisters in a single marriage contract: each one individually could be married validly, but the prohibition is the combination of two sisters and similar cases. There is no preference to validate the contract for one specific sister, and no benefit in validating it for an unspecified one, so the contract becomes void for both. This differs from marrying them in two successive contracts: then the second contract is the void one, unless the first is forgotten, in which case both are void due to lack of preference and impossibility of execution with uncertainty (re: al-Hidaya and its commentaries on marriage 3/123, and al-Durar 1/331 and 333).
Source pages p. 671

Commentary
This passage is a juridical exposition of the Hanafi doctrine of partial nullity (tajazzu' al-butilan). The author establishes that nullity is not necessarily all-or-nothing; a single contract can be partially valid and partially void when the legal requirement is satisfied for some components but not others. Two clear fiqh examples are provided: (1) mixing personal property with waqf property in one sale—the sale stands for the personal property and fails for the waqf share; (2) marrying a lawful and an unlawful woman in one contract—the marriage is valid for the lawful woman. However, if the void part cannot be identified, as in marrying two sisters (since each marriage is individually valid but simultaneous combination is prohibited), the entire contract becomes void because there is no basis to prefer one over the other. The footnote (1) contrasts the Shafi'i view (any uncertainty causes total nullity) with the Hanafi view (uncertainty only causes fasād, not total nullity). Footnote (2) notes that this Hanafi principle influenced the Syrian Civil Code (Article 144). The passage underscores the Hanafi school's pragmatic, nuanced approach to contract validity.

Key Terms
بطلان (buṭlān): Complete invalidity of a contract from its inception due to a fundamental defect.
تجزؤ (tajazzu’): Divisibility; the quality of a contract being separable into parts for legal consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in the passage, when does the nullity of a contract affect the entire contract rather than only part of it?
A) Always, whenever any part is void.
B) Only when the void part is specified and separable.
C) When the void part is not specified and there is no way to determine it.
D) Only in marriage contracts, not in sales.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'Nullity is divisible' allows partial validity when the void part is determinate; otherwise nullity is total.

Liability in a Void Sale: Trust vs. Guarantee
Presenting two juristic views on buyer's liability for goods in a void sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on buyer's liability in a void sale, explain their proofs, and apply the Majalla's adopted ruling to a hypothetical scenario.

Core Idea
In a void sale, the goods may be a trust or a guarantee; the Majalla chose trust.

This passage from al-Zarqa's Introduction presents a classical disagreement about the legal nature of possession under a void sale. Understanding this dispute is crucial for the mufti who must determine liability when goods are destroyed after a void transaction. The two views turn on whether the intent of exchange imposes liability even when the contract is null.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Positions
State clearly: (1) The buyer holds the goods as a trust (amanah) — no liability without fault. (2) The buyer holds the goods as a guarantee (daman) — liable for mithl or value.
Both positions are valid within the Hanafi school.
2
State Each View's Proof
First view: mere possession with permission does not create liability (like deposit). Second view: possession in exchange-based contracts creates liability; nullity removes ownership but not exchange intent.
The second view relies on the maxim 'qabd 'ala sabīl al-badal yūjib al-daman'.
3
Analyze the Key Difference
The crux is whether the contractual intent of exchange survives the contract's nullity for liability purposes.
4
Relate to the Majalla
The Majalla (Article 370) adopted the amanah view, probably because it is more protective of the possessor who did not commit fault.
5
Apply to a Hypothetical
Given a void sale of fungible goods (e.g., wheat) destroyed without fault: under amanah view, no liability; under daman view, the buyer must pay mithl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥/٥٣ - الحالة الثانية : في ضمان المبيع المقبوض في البيع الباطل : والبيع الباطل إذا قبض فيه المشتري المبيع فقد اختلف الفقهاء في حکمه :
- فمنهم من يرى أنَّ المبيع يكون أمانة في يد المشتري غير مضمون عليه إذا هلك دون تعد منه أو تقصير في حفظه، كالوديعة في يد الوديع . وحجة ذلك أن البيع لما كان باطلاً لم يبق سوى مجرد القبض بإذن البائع. وهذا لا يوجب الضمان على القابض إلا بالتعدي أو التقصير فيبقى المبيع على ملك البائع أمانة في يد المشتري. وبهذا الرأي أخذت المجلة (م/ ۳۷۰).
·
- ويرى آخرون أن المبيع يكون في يد المشتري مضموناً عليه بمثله إذا كان مالاً مثلياً، وبقيمته إن كان قيمياً (۱) . وحجتهم أنه مقبوض على سبيل المعاوضة المقصودة بالعقد. وبطلان البيع إنما يمنع انتقال الملكية به، ولكنه لا يلغي فكرة المعاوضة الثابتة فيه والتي لم يسلم البائع المبيع إلا على أساسها ومن المقرر أن القبض على سبيل البدلية يوجب الضمان (۲).
·
(۱) المال المثلي هو الذي له أمثال في السوق مساوية له في الصفات بحيث يمكن أن يقوم بعضه مقام بعض في الوفاء دون فرق يعتد به كالسكر والزيت والقمح من نوع واحد وكالورق وأواني البلور وألواحه ونحوها من المصنوعات المتماثلة اليوم. أما المال القيمي فهو ما ليس له أمثال مساوية كالحيوانات والدور والمصوغات، فلكل واحد منها قيمة تختلف عن الآخر من جنسه باختلاف الصفات المميزة له (المجلة / ۱۴۵ - ۱۴۶).
·
ویری تفصیل نظرية المثلية والقيمية في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الذي أسميناه المدخل إلى نظرية الالتزام العامة في الفقه الإسلامي ف/ ١/١٤ - ١٤ .
·
(۲) انظر ما تقدم في تصنيف العقود ف ١١/٤٧.
5/53 - Second case: Concerning the liability of the sold object taken possession of in a void sale. In a void sale, if the buyer takes possession of the sold object, the jurists have differed on its ruling. Some hold that the sold object is a trust (amanah) in the buyer's hand, not guaranteed (non-liable) if it perishes without transgression or negligence in its preservation, like a deposit with the depositary. Their proof is that since the sale is void, nothing remains except mere possession with the seller's permission. This does not impose liability on the possessor except through transgression or negligence, so the sold object remains the property of the seller as a trust in the buyer's hand. The Majalla adopted this view (Article 370). Others hold that the sold object in the buyer's hand is guaranteed (liable) by its equivalent if it is fungible property (mithli), and by its value if it is non-fungible (qimi). Their proof is that it is possessed on the basis of the exchange intended by the contract. Nullity of the sale only prevents transfer of ownership through it, but it does not cancel the idea of exchange established in it, and the seller did not hand over the sold object except on that basis. It is established that possession on the basis of substitution imposes liability. (1) Fungible property (al-māl al-mithlī) is that which has equivalents in the market equal in qualities such that one can replace another in fulfillment without appreciable difference, like sugar, oil, wheat of one type, and like paper, crystal vessels and sheets, and similar manufactured items that are identical today. Non-fungible property (al-māl al-qīmī) is that which has no equal equivalents, like animals, houses, and jewelry; each has a value that differs from another of its kind by differences in distinctive qualities (Majalla articles 145-146). The detailed theory of fungibility and non-fungibility is found in Volume Three of this series, which we called 'Introduction to the General Theory of Obligations in Islamic Jurisprudence' f/1/14-14. (2) See what has preceded in the classification of contracts, f/11/47.
Source pages p. 676

Commentary
This passage presents two juristic opinions on the liability of the buyer who takes possession of goods under a void sale. The first opinion (adopted by the Ottoman Majalla) considers the goods as a trust (amanah) in the buyer's hands; if they are destroyed without fault, no compensation is due. The reasoning is that the void sale leaves only permissible possession, which does not create liability. The second opinion holds the buyer liable for the goods as if they were taken under an exchange contract; the buyer must pay the equivalent (mithl) for fungible goods or the value (qīmah) for non-fungible goods. The reasoning is that the essence of the contract is exchange, and the seller only handed over the goods expecting compensation; nullity prevents ownership transfer but not the idea of substitutive possession which imposes liability. This disagreement shows how different interpretations of the nature of a void contract lead to different practical outcomes.

Key Terms
أمانة (amānah): Something held in trust; the holder is not liable for accidental loss unless negligent.
مضمون (maḍmūn): Guaranteed or liable; the holder must compensate if the item is lost.
المال المثلي (al-māl al-mithlī): Fungible property that has equivalents in the market, e.g., wheat, oil, identical manufactured items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling of the Majalla regarding the buyer's liability in a void sale?
A) The goods are a trust (amanah) and the buyer is not liable unless at fault.
B) The buyer is liable for the price.
C) The buyer is liable for the value of the goods.
D) The sale is valid.

Key Takeaway
In a void sale, the buyer's liability depends on whether possession is seen as amanah or as based on exchange; the Majalla adopted the amanah view.

Possession in Void Sale Creates Liability (Qawida)
Stating a rule of liability (daman) for possession in void sales and purchase inspection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida that possession in a void sale or purchase inspection with a stated price imposes guarantee, and identify the exception when no price is specified.

Core Idea
Possession with a stated price turns trust into guarantee – even without a valid contract.

This lesson examines a key legal maxim from Hanafi transaction law: if you take an item for inspection with a price mentioned, you are liable for it. The same rule applies to possession in a void sale. Understanding this helps distinguish between trust (amanah) and guarantee (daman) in possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
State the qawida in Arabic and English as given in the passage.
This is the core rule to memorize.
2
Understand the condition
The price must be specified during negotiation – this indicates exchange intent and triggers liability.
If no price, possession remains trust.
3
Apply to void sale
Possession in a void sale is analogous – the buyer is liable because the contract is void but possession was based on exchange.
The void sale still has a qualitative effect.
4
Note the exception
If no price is specified, possession remains amanah (trust) and no guarantee arises.
The default is trust.
5
Recognize the legal effect
This ruling is an athar wasfi (qualitative effect) – the void sale still has consequences for possession.
Not all effects of a void contract are null.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Supports in Fiqh. And it is also established that an item taken on purchase inspection (1) without a contract is guaranteed upon its possessor in kind or value if a price was specified during negotiation, because this specification indicates that its owner did not hand it over except on the basis of exchange and guarantee. So the sold item taken in a void sale is no less in status than this. And this view is the preponderant one in Hanafi ijtihad, and the other three imams also said it. And thus we see that the void sale, although its legal effect does not follow, yet it had a qualitative effect on the possession that made it guaranteed. (1) Taking on purchase inspection is when a person takes an item from its owner on the basis that if he likes it he will buy it.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) within the theory of legal supports. It establishes that possession in a void sale, or under purchase inspection with a stated price, creates liability (daman) for the possessor. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the owner only handed over the item on the basis of exchange and guarantee. The Hanafi school and the other three imams agree on this. The author notes that this is a qualitative effect (athar wasfi) of the void sale – despite the contract being void, possession takes on a legal consequence.

Nullity of Components and Actions Based on a Void Contract
Explanation of the first characteristic of nullity: voiding contained elements and subsequent actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'batalān al-shayʼ yastalzim batalān mā fī ḍimnihi wa batalān mā buniyā ʿalayhi' to void contracts and distinguish between execution as building upon the void contract and a new independent contract.

Core Idea
A void contract voids its contents and all actions based on it; only a fresh intention can create a valid new contract.

In this lesson, we explore the first characteristic of nullity in Hanafi law: nullity of a thing entails nullity of everything contained within it and everything built upon it. This principle governs void contracts, ensuring that no legal effects can arise from a void act. We will examine two key applications: conditions and price in a void sale, and subsequent delivery/receipt. Crucially, we also uncover an exception: if parties act with a new intention, a valid contract can emerge.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Learn the Arabic formulation: بطلان الشيء يستلزم بطلان ما في ضمنه وبطلان ما بني عليه. Recognize that it has two components: internal contents and subsequent actions.
This maxim is encoded in Majalla 52.
2
Identify the first application
Explain that if a void sale includes conditions and price, those conditions and price are also void. Neither party is bound by them.
This illustrates 'nullity of what is contained' (بطلان ما في ضمنه).
3
Identify the second application
Explain that if delivery and receipt occur to execute a void sale, that receipt is void. The property remains with its original owner, and the recipient holds it as a trust.
This illustrates 'nullity of what is built upon it' (بطلان ما بني عليه).
4
Recognize the exception
Note that if the parties later exchange the same items with a new intention (qasad jadid) to form a valid contract (by ta'ati or verbal renewal), that new contract is valid because it is not built upon the void contract.
The exception is governed by the maxim 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني' (effect is given to intentions in contracts).
5
Connect to other qawaid
Understand the parallel maxim 'الفرع يتبع أصله' (the subsidiary follows the original) and how it reinforces the same logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وعلى هذا قرّر الفقهاء أنه :
أ - إذا باع الإنسان شيئاً بيعاً باطلاً واشتمل البيع على شروط وحدد فيه الثمن بطلت مع البيع الشروط التي اشترطها العاقدان فلا تلزمهما، وبطلت أيضاً تسمية الثمن المسمى فهذا مثال لبطلان ما تضمنه الباطل.

Third Topic: Characteristics of Nullity (1): 7/53 - Verily, nullity has numerous characteristics, which are general rulings branching from that basic negative consequence of nullity as previously indicated. And these characteristics are the following: 8/53 - (First) Verily, the nullity of a thing necessitates the nullity of what is contained within it and the nullity of what is built upon it (see: Majalla, 52). And upon this, the jurists have determined that: A) If a person sells something in a void sale and the sale includes conditions and the price is determined, then along with the sale, the conditions that the contracting parties stipulated become void, and they are not binding upon them, and also the naming of the specified price becomes void. This is an example of the nullity of what is contained in the void (thing). B) And if mutual receipt (taqabud) of the sold item and the price occurs between the parties by way of execution of this void sale, this mutual receipt is not considered a sale by exchange (bay' bi al-ta'ati), because the parties only intended thereby to build upon the void contract, so it is also void. And what each of them has taken remains the property of the other. This is an example of the nullity of what is built upon the void (thing) (2). And we will see in the following characteristic the result of the nullity of the price naming and the result of the nullity of executing the void contract. If the contract is void or voidable, and the pillars of another contract are present, then it is valid as that contract whose pillars are present if it becomes clear that the intention of the contracting parties was directed towards it. (1) 'Characteristics': plural of 'characteristic' with a shaddah on the sad and ya, a relational derivation from 'al-khassah' with a shaddah on the sad. (2) As for if the mutual receipt was not by way of execution of the void contract and building upon it, but rather the parties carried it out with a new intention, turning away from the void contract to an exchange (ta'ati) fulfilling the conditions of formation, then this is a new contract effective in its ruling, just as if the parties renewed the void contract properly with words.

Commentary
This passage explains the first characteristic of nullity (خصائص البطلان) in Hanafi legal theory: nullity of a thing (e.g., a contract) entails nullity of everything contained within it and everything built upon it. The author provides two illustrations: (a) conditions and price specification in a void sale are themselves void and unenforceable; (b) any delivery and receipt that occurs to execute a void sale is also void—property does not transfer, and each party must return what they took. Footnote (2) adds an important exception: if the parties later exchange items with a fresh intention to form a new contract (by ta'ati or verbal renewal), that new contract is valid. This rule underscores the principle that a void act cannot produce valid effects, but a distinct valid act can supersede it.

Key Terms
بطلان ما في ضمنه (buṭlān mā fī ḍimnihi): Nullity of conditions, prices, and other elements included within the void contract.
بطلان ما بني عليه (buṭlān mā buniyā ʿalayhi): Nullity of any subsequent act that is based on and intended to execute the void contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The price is valid and must be paid.
B) The price is void and neither party can claim it.

D) The price becomes a gift.

Key Takeaway
The nullity of a contract extends to all its internal elements and to any actions taken to execute it; only a new intention can create a valid independent contract.

8 min
The Maxim of Nullity: What is Contained and Built Upon It
Expanding the first characteristic of nullity with furuat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'nullity of a thing necessitates nullity of what is contained and what is built upon it' to identify void components and subsequent transactions.

Core Idea
Nullity spreads: if the core contract is void, all its parts and follow-ups are void too.

This lesson introduces a fundamental legal maxim in Hanafi fiqh: when a contract is void, everything that depends on it—terms, price specification, and even physical execution—is also void. The maxim governs many areas of mu'amalat, especially sales and settlements.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Begin by reciting the maxim in Arabic and English: 'إن بطلان الشيء يستلزم بطلان ما في ضمنه وبطلان ما بني عليه' — 'The nullity of a thing necessitates the nullity of what is contained within it and the nullity of what is built upon it.'
Memorize the phrasing; it is article 52 of the Majalla.
2
Identify its scope
The maxim applies to all contracts and transactions that are void (bātil). It covers both the internal components (e.g., conditions, price) and subsequent acts built on the void contract (e.g., delivery, acknowledgement).
It does not apply if a later act is performed with a new independent intention.
3
Work through the furuat
Apply the maxim to the examples given: (a) void sale with conditions and price – conditions and price specification are void; (b) exchange of goods as execution – the exchange is void, not a valid sale by mutual delivery; (c) acknowledgement or release based on a void contract – the acknowledgement/release is void.
Each furua demonstrates either 'contained' (conditions/price) or 'built upon' (delivery/acknowledgement).
4
Identify the istithna'
The maxim has an exception: if the parties later perform a transaction with a fresh, independent intention (qasd jadid), that new transaction can be valid. This is governed by the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions).
The text's footnote (2) hints at this; it is not in the main passage but is essential for correct application.

الفصل (٥٣) نتائج البطلان
وكذلك لو أقر أحد لآخر بمبلغ، أو أبرأه ولو إبراءاً عاماً، وكان الإقرار أو الإبراء مبنياً على عقد أبرمه الطرفان كبيع أو صلح، ثم تبين بطلان العقد، فإنه يبطل الإقرار والإبراء المترتبان عليه . (ر: رد المحتار، باب الاستحقاق ٤/ ۱۹۹ - ۲۰۰ وكتاب الصلح ٤٧٣/٤ و ٤٧٩، والمجلة ١٥٦٦)

٩/٥٣ - (ثانياً) : إذا بَطَل التصرف الفعلي لزم نقضه وإعادة الأوضاع إلى سابق عهدها قبل التصرف.
وذلك لأن التصرف الفعلي يحدث أثراً مادياً على كل حال، سواء أكان التصرف صحيحاً أو باطلاً. وبذلك يختلف عن التصرف القولي الذي يكون أثره اعتبارياً محضاً فلا بد من إزالة الأثر الذي أحدثه التصرف الفعلي الباطل لتعود الأوضاع الحقوقية إلى ما كانت عليه من سابق حالها. وبناء على ذلك لو تقابض المتبايعان المبيع والثمن في البيع الباطل (1) يلتزم كل قابض برد ما قبضه لبطلان القبض المبني على عقد باطل. فلو استهلك المشتري المبيع في هذه الحالة يضمن للبائع مثله إذا كان مثلياً، وقيمته إذا كان قيمياً، سواءً أكانت قيمته في السوق أكثر أو أقل من الثمن المسمى في العقد. ذلك لأن البيع لما كان باطلاً كانت تسمية الثمن في ضمنه باطلة أيضاً. والأصل في ضمان الأموال التعادل. والمعادل للمال المستهلك هو مثله أو قيمته. وإنما حلَّ الثمن المسمى محلهما بسبب العقد. فإذا بطَلَ الاتفاق على الثمن لأنه جزء من العقد الباطل رجع إلى أصله بالمثل أو القيمة.
وهذا بخلاف ما لو كان البيع منعقداً صحيحاً، فقبض المشتري المبيع دون رضا البائع قبل دفع الثمن المستحق وهلك المبيع في يد المشتري أو (1) وهذا يدخل في نطاق نظرية الإثراء بلا سبب مشروع وهي نظرية تعبر عن مبدأ قد أقرّ الفقه الإسلامي فكرته في نطاق وشرائط أضيق مما في الفقه الأجنبي.
Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Confirmations in Fiqh. Third Section: Characteristics of Nullity (1). 7/53: Indeed, nullity has numerous characteristics, which are general legal rules branching from that fundamental negative result of nullity as previously indicated. These characteristics are the following: 8/53: (First) The nullity of a thing necessitates the nullity of what is contained within it and the nullity of what is built upon it (see: Majalla, article 52). Based on this, the jurists have decided that: (a) If a person sells something in a void sale and the sale includes conditions and specifies a price, then along with the sale, the conditions that the two contracting parties stipulated are void and are not binding upon them, and also the specification of the named price is void. This is an example of the nullity of what is contained in the void thing. (b) If the exchange of possession of the sold object and the price takes place between the two parties as a performance of this void sale, this exchange is not considered a sale by mutual delivery, because the parties only intended to build upon the void contract, so it is also void. What each of them has taken remains the property of the other. This is an example of the nullity of what is built upon the void thing (2). We will see in the following characteristic the consequence of the nullity of the price specification and the consequence of the performance of the void contract. ... (Page 680) Chapter (53) Results of Nullity. Similarly, if one person acknowledges a sum to another, or releases him even with a general release, and the acknowledgment or release was built upon a contract made by the two parties such as a sale or a settlement, then the nullity of the contract becomes apparent, then the acknowledgment and release that are consequential upon it become void. (See: Radd al-Muhtar, Chapter of Istihqaq 4/199-200, and Book of Sulh 4/473 and 479, and Majalla 1566). 9/53: (Second) If a physical transaction (actual act) becomes void, it is necessary to reverse it and return the situations to their prior state before the transaction. That is because the physical transaction produces a material effect in any case, whether the transaction is valid or void. Thus it differs from a verbal transaction whose effect is purely moral. So it is necessary to remove the effect produced by the void physical transaction so that the legal situations return to what they were before. Accordingly, if the two contracting parties exchange possession of the sold object and the price in a void sale (1), each holder is obliged to return what he took because the possession (qabd) built on a void contract is void. If the buyer consumes the sold object in this case, he is liable to the seller for its equivalent if it is fungible, and its value if it is non-fungible, whether its market value is more or less than the named price in the contract. This is because when the sale is void, the specification of the price within it is also void. The basis in liability for wealth is equivalence. The equivalent for consumed wealth is its like or its value. The named price only took its place due to the contract. So when the agreement on the price is void because it is part of the void contract, the matter returns to its origin: the like or the value. This is contrary to the case where the sale is validly concluded, then the buyer takes possession of the sold object without the seller's consent before paying the due price, and the sold object perishes in the buyer's hands. (1) And this falls within the scope of the theory of enrichment without lawful cause, a theory that expresses a principle whose idea Islamic jurisprudence has recognized within a scope and conditions narrower than those in foreign jurisprudence.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage presents two central characteristics of legal nullity (batalan) in Hanafi fiqh. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, is laying down general legal rules that flow from the fundamental negative result of nullity—that a void contract produces no legal effects. The first characteristic: nullity of a thing entails nullity of what is contained within it and what is built upon it. This is a general maxim (qawida). The text gives two applications: (a) In a void sale, all conditions and the price specification are also void; thus, the contracting parties are not bound by them, and the named price is not enforceable. (b) If the parties physically exchange the goods and price in execution of the void contract, that exchange is itself void (not a valid sale by mutual delivery) because it was built on the void contract. Hence, each party retains ownership of what he originally had; the exchange does not transfer ownership. The author also notes via a footnote (2) that if the parties later intend a fresh transaction, it can be valid. The second characteristic: if a physical transaction (tasarruf fi'li) is void, it must be physically reversed to restore the prior legal state, unlike a verbal transaction whose effect is merely legal. This leads to the rule that each party must return what he took, and if the subject matter is consumed, the liable party must pay the equivalent or value, regardless of the named price. The text contrasts with the case of a valid sale where possession is wrongful—different liability rules apply.

Key Terms
البطلان (al-butlan): legal nullity; a contract or transaction that does not produce any of its intended legal effects because it lacks an essential element or condition
التصرف الفعلي (al-tasarruf al-fi'li): a physical act or transaction that produces a material effect in the external world, as opposed to a mere verbal declaration
التصرف القولي (al-tasarruf al-qawli): a verbal act such as offer and acceptance, acknowledgment, or release; its effect is purely legal (i'tibari)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two parties execute a void sale and then exchange the goods and price, what is the status of that exchange?
A) It is a valid sale by mutual delivery.
B) It is void because it is built on the void contract.
C) It is valid only if the price is paid in cash.
D) It is a new contract requiring a fresh offer and acceptance.

Key Takeaway
The nullity of a contract spreads to all its components and to any subsequent transaction built upon it.

Void Physical Transactions Must Be Reversed
Explaining the second characteristic of nullity: reversal of physical acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that void physical transactions must be reversed and determine the liability for consumed assets.

Core Idea
Physical acts create real effects; voidness demands physical restoration.

When a transaction involves a physical act (like handing over goods), its voidness does not erase the material change. The law requires that the situation be restored to its prior state, and if the asset is consumed, the liable party must pay its equivalent or value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the physical act
Determine if the act is a tasarruf fi'li (physical transaction) such as delivery, possession, or consumption. A verbal act (tasarruf qawli) would not trigger the reversal rule.
In the example, the exchange of goods and price is a physical act.
2
Apply the rule: mandatory reversal
If the physical transaction is void (because it was built on a void contract), each party must return what they took. The material effect must be undone.
This differs from a void verbal act, which has only legal effects and may not require physical reversal.
3
Handle consumption
If the acquired asset is consumed, the liable party must pay the equivalent (mithl) if fungible, or the value (qimah) if non-fungible. The named price from the void contract is irrelevant because the price specification is also void.
The basis is the principle of equivalence in liability for wealth.
4
Distinguish from valid contract
In a valid sale, if the buyer takes possession wrongfully and the goods perish, liability is different (often the price is due). Here, because the contract is void, the price is not fixed.
The passage contrasts with the case of a valid sale to highlight the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وذلك لأن التصرف الفعلي يحدث أثراً مادياً على كل حال، سواء أكان التصرف صحيحاً أو باطلاً. وبذلك يختلف عن التصرف القولي الذي يكون أثره اعتبارياً محضاً فلا بد من إزالة الأثر الذي أحدثه التصرف الفعلي الباطل لتعود الأوضاع الحقوقية إلى ما كانت عليه من سابق حالها. وبناء على ذلك لو تقابض المتبايعان المبيع والثمن في البيع الباطل (1) يلتزم كل قابض برد ما قبضه لبطلان القبض المبني على عقد باطل. فلو استهلك المشتري المبيع في هذه الحالة يضمن للبائع مثله إذا كان مثلياً، وقيمته إذا كان قيمياً، سواءً أكانت قيمته في السوق أكثر أو أقل من الثمن المسمى في العقد. ذلك لأن البيع لما كان باطلاً كانت تسمية الثمن في ضمنه باطلة أيضاً. والأصل في ضمان الأموال التعادل. والمعادل للمال المستهلك هو مثله أو قيمته. وإنما حلَّ الثمن المسمى محلهما بسبب العقد. فإذا بطَلَ الاتفاق على الثمن لأنه جزء من العقد الباطل رجع إلى أصله بالمثل أو القيمة.
وهذا بخلاف ما لو كان البيع منعقداً صحيحاً، فقبض المشتري المبيع دون رضا البائع قبل دفع الثمن المستحق وهلك المبيع في يد المشتري أو (1) وهذا يدخل في نطاق نظرية الإثراء بلا سبب مشروع وهي نظرية تعبر عن مبدأ قد أقرّ الفقه الإسلامي فكرته في نطاق وشرائط أضيق مما في الفقه الأجنبي.
Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Confirmations in Fiqh. Third Section: Characteristics of Nullity (1). 7/53: Indeed, nullity has numerous characteristics, which are general legal rules branching from that fundamental negative result of nullity as previously indicated. These characteristics are the following: 8/53: (First) The nullity of a thing necessitates the nullity of what is contained within it and the nullity of what is built upon it (see: Majalla, article 52). Based on this, the jurists have decided that: (a) If a person sells something in a void sale and the sale includes conditions and specifies a price, then along with the sale, the conditions that the two contracting parties stipulated are void and are not binding upon them, and also the specification of the named price is void. This is an example of the nullity of what is contained in the void thing. (b) If the exchange of possession of the sold object and the price takes place between the two parties as a performance of this void sale, this exchange is not considered a sale by mutual delivery, because the parties only intended to build upon the void contract, so it is also void. What each of them has taken remains the property of the other. This is an example of the nullity of what is built upon the void thing (2). We will see in the following characteristic the consequence of the nullity of the price specification and the consequence of the performance of the void contract. ... (Page 680) Chapter (53) Results of Nullity. Similarly, if one person acknowledges a sum to another, or releases him even with a general release, and the acknowledgment or release was built upon a contract made by the two parties such as a sale or a settlement, then the nullity of the contract becomes apparent, then the acknowledgment and release that are consequential upon it become void. (See: Radd al-Muhtar, Chapter of Istihqaq 4/199-200, and Book of Sulh 4/473 and 479, and Majalla 1566). 9/53: (Second) If a physical transaction (actual act) becomes void, it is necessary to reverse it and return the situations to their prior state before the transaction. That is because the physical transaction produces a material effect in any case, whether the transaction is valid or void. Thus it differs from a verbal transaction whose effect is purely moral. So it is necessary to remove the effect produced by the void physical transaction so that the legal situations return to what they were before. Accordingly, if the two contracting parties exchange possession of the sold object and the price in a void sale (1), each holder is obliged to return what he took because the possession (qabd) built on a void contract is void. If the buyer consumes the sold object in this case, he is liable to the seller for its equivalent if it is fungible, and its value if it is non-fungible, whether its market value is more or less than the named price in the contract. This is because when the sale is void, the specification of the price within it is also void. The basis in liability for wealth is equivalence. The equivalent for consumed wealth is its like or its value. The named price only took its place due to the contract. So when the agreement on the price is void because it is part of the void contract, the matter returns to its origin: the like or the value. This is contrary to the case where the sale is validly concluded, then the buyer takes possession of the sold object without the seller's consent before paying the due price, and the sold object perishes in the buyer's hands. (1) And this falls within the scope of the theory of enrichment without lawful cause, a theory that expresses a principle whose idea Islamic jurisprudence has recognized within a scope and conditions narrower than those in foreign jurisprudence.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage presents two central characteristics of legal nullity (batalan) in Hanafi fiqh. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, is laying down general legal rules that flow from the fundamental negative result of nullity—that a void contract produces no legal effects. The first characteristic: nullity of a thing entails nullity of what is contained within it and what is built upon it. This is a general maxim (qawida). The text gives two applications: (a) In a void sale, all conditions and the price specification are also void; thus, the contracting parties are not bound by them, and the named price is not enforceable. (b) If the parties physically exchange the goods and price in execution of the void contract, that exchange is itself void (not a valid sale by mutual delivery) because it was built on the void contract. Hence, each party retains ownership of what he originally had; the exchange does not transfer ownership. The author also notes via a footnote (2) that if the parties later intend a fresh transaction, it can be valid. The second characteristic: if a physical transaction (tasarruf fi'li) is void, it must be physically reversed to restore the prior legal state, unlike a verbal transaction whose effect is merely legal. This leads to the rule that each party must return what he took, and if the subject matter is consumed, the liable party must pay the equivalent or value, regardless of the named price. The text contrasts with the case of a valid sale where possession is wrongful—different liability rules apply.

Key Terms
البطلان (al-butlan): legal nullity; a contract or transaction that does not produce any of its intended legal effects because it lacks an essential element or condition
التصرف الفعلي (al-tasarruf al-fi'li): a physical act or transaction that produces a material effect in the external world, as opposed to a mere verbal declaration
التصرف القولي (al-tasarruf al-qawli): a verbal act such as offer and acceptance, acknowledgment, or release; its effect is purely legal (i'tibari)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The value as determined by the seller.
D) No liability because the sale was void.

Key Takeaway
A void physical transaction must be reversed; if consumption occurs, the liable party pays the equivalent or value, not the contract price.

Guarantee After Void Physical Disposals: The Maxim of Equivalence
Explains effects of void physical disposals and the guarantee rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-asl fi daman al-amwal al-ta'adul' to void physical disposals and distinguish the guarantee from valid contracts.

Core Idea
After a void physical disposal, compensation is by mithl or value, not the named price.

This lesson unpacks a key maxim of Hanafi fiqh governing liability for property after a void physical act. The passage distinguishes between verbal and physical acts, and shows how a void contract voids the agreed price, reverting to the default rule of equivalence. Understanding this rule is essential for muftis handling cases of void sales or gifts where the property has been consumed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its nature
Determine whether the disposal is physical (fi'li) or verbal (qawli). Physical acts produce material effects that must be undone if the legal basis is void.
Examples of physical disposals: taking possession (qabd), delivery, consumption.
2
State the ruling for void physical disposals
The act must be annulled and the situation restored to its prior state. If the object is consumed, the consumer guarantees by mithl (if fungible) or value (if non-fungible), regardless of any named price.
The named price in a void contract is itself void.
3
Apply the maxim 'al-asl fi daman al-amwal al-ta'adul'
The default rule for property compensation is equivalence: mithl for fungibles, value for non-fungibles. The named price only replaces this equivalence due to a valid contract.
This is the core qawida being illustrated.
4
Contrast with valid sale with unauthorized possession
In a valid sale, even if possession is unauthorized, the buyer guarantees the agreed price because ownership transferred by the contract. The named price is valid and replaces mithl/value.
This is the istithna (exception) to the maxim.
5
Extend to void gift (hiba)
Unauthorized possession in a void gift is analogous: if the property perishes, the donee guarantees by mithl or value.
Gift is a 'aqd 'ayni requiring authorized possession for completion.

(1)
