الضمان
وهذا بخلاف ما لو كان البيع منعقداً صحيحاً، فقبض المشتري المبيع دون رضا البائع قبل دفع الثمن المستحق وهلك المبيع في يد المشتري أو
(1) وهذا يدخل في نطاق نظرية الإثراء بلا سبب مشروع وهي نظرية تعبر عن مبدأ قد أقرّ الفقه الإسلامي فكرته في نطاق وشرائط أضيق مما في الفقه الأجنبي.
استهلكه، فإنما يضمن للبائع الثمن المتفق عليه في العقد، لا قيمة المبيع لأن المبيع قد انتقلت ملكيته إلى المشتري بمجرد تمام البيع الصحيح لقاء ثمن معين حل محل القيمة بالتراضي. ولم يبطل هذا التعيين للثمن، وإنما الذي بطَلَ هو القبض غير المستحق فيهلك المبيع في يد المشتري وهو مملوك له بالثمن المسمى لا بالقيمة التي قد تكون أكثر أو أقل منه وعلى هذا الأساس يتنزل أيضاً ما لو قبض الموهوب له المال الموهوب دون رضا الواهب صراحة أو دلالة فإنَّ هذا القبض باطل، لأن الهبة من العقود العينية التي لا تتم إلا بالقبض المأذون. فلو هلك المال المقبوض في يد الموهوب له في هذه الحال كان مضموناً عليه بالمثل لو مثلياً، وبالقيمة لو قيمياً.
Second: If a physical disposal becomes void, it must be annulled and the situation returned to its state prior to the disposal. This is because a physical act produces a material effect in any case, whether the act is valid or void. Thus it differs from a verbal act, whose effect is purely conceptual. Therefore the effect produced by the void physical act must be removed so that the legal circumstances return to what they were previously. Based on this, if the buyer and seller in a void sale have exchanged the sold item and the price, each party is obliged to return what he received because the possession is based on a void contract. If the buyer consumes the sold item in this case, he guarantees to the seller its mithl if it is fungible, or its value if it is non-fungible, whether its market value is more or less than the price named in the contract. This is because when the sale is void, the naming of the price within it is also void. The principle in guarantee of properties is equivalence. The equivalent for consumed property is its mithl or its value. The named price only replaces them due to the contract. So when the agreement on the price is void because it is part of the void contract, one returns to the original principle of mithl or value. This is contrary to the case if the sale was validly concluded, and the buyer takes possession without the seller's consent before paying the due price, and the sold item perishes in the hands of the buyer or he consumes it: then he guarantees to the seller the agreed price in the contract, not the value of the sold item, because ownership of the sold item transferred to the buyer upon the completion of the valid sale in exchange for a specific price that replaced the value by mutual agreement. This specification of the price is not void; what is void is the unauthorized possession. So the sold item perishes in the hands of the buyer while it is owned by him for the named price, not for the value which may be more or less. On this basis also falls the case where the donee takes possession of the gifted property without the donor's express or implied consent: this possession is void, because gift is one of the chattel contracts that are not completed except with authorized possession. So if the property taken perishes in the hands of the donee in this case, it is guaranteed by him by mithl if fungible, and by value if non-fungible.
Source pages p. 680

Commentary
This is an exposition of the effects of void physical disposals, including the principle of guarantee. The author distinguishes between physical and verbal acts: physical acts produce material effects that must be undone. In a void sale, each party must return what was received; if the buyer consumes, he guarantees by mithl or value regardless of the named price, because the price is void. The author states the underlying maxim: 'The principle in guarantee of properties is equivalence.' In a valid sale, if possession is unauthorized, the buyer guarantees the agreed price because ownership transferred by the valid contract. The same principle applies to void gifts: unauthorized possession leads to guarantee by mithl or value. The footnote mentions the theory of unjust enrichment.

Key Terms
التصرف الفعلي (al-tasarruf al-fi'li): A physical act that produces a material effect, as opposed to a verbal act.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession of an object physically or legally.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability to compensate for loss or destruction of property.
مثلي (mithli): Fungible goods that can be substituted by similar items (e.g., grains, liquids).
قيمي (qimi): Non-fungible goods with unique value (e.g., real estate, animals).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
After a void physical disposal, compensation defaults to mithl (for fungibles) or market value (for non-fungibles), not the agreed price.

A Void Contract Cannot Be Validated
Presents the rule that a void contract cannot be validated by subsequent ratification, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-`aqd al-batil la yaqbil al-ijazah' to identify when a contract can or cannot be ratified, and distinguish void contracts from suspended contracts.

Core Idea
A void contract is legally non-existent; ratification cannot revive it—only a new contract can.

This lesson examines a key maxim in Hanafi contract law: a void contract cannot be cured by later ratification. You will learn the difference between void contracts (batil) and suspended contracts (mawquf), and see how the rule applies in scenarios involving capacity, guardianship, unauthorized agency, and offer-acceptance.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Repeat the maxim in Arabic and English: 'العقد الباطل لا يقبل الإجازة' meaning 'A void contract does not accept ratification'.
This is a universal maxim (kulliya) in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Identify the scope
The maxim applies to all void contracts (batil). A void contract is one that is fundamentally defective from its inception, such as lacking essential pillars or involving prohibited subject matter.
Contrast with suspended contracts (mawquf) which are valid but dependent on ratification.
3
Work through the furuat
Apply the maxim to each example in the source: lunatic's sale, guardian's fraud, fuduli after rejection, and rejected offer. In each case, the contract is void and cannot be later ratified.
The furuat confirm the maxim's operation.
4
Identify the istithna
Note the exception: irregular (fasid) contracts can sometimes be cured by removing the defect, but void (batil) contracts cannot.
The distinction is unique to Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٠/٥ - (ثالثاً : العقد الباطل لا يقبل الإجازة : فالإجازة إنما هي في الحقيقة إرادة منفردة يقرر بها الإنسان ويُمضي تصرفاً قائماً متوقفاً جعل الشرع له فيه حق التقرير والرفض. فالنظر الفقهي أن محل الإجازة إنما هو العقد المنعقد الموقوف الذي يمس حقاً لغير العاقدين. فلذلك الغير أن يجيزه إن شاء فينفذ عليه، أو أن يرفضه فيبطل. وعلى كل حال يعتبر العقد الموقوف عقداً صحيحاً لكنه معقول، أي محتبس عن السريان. فبإجازة صاحب العلاقة يحل عقاله فيجتاز محبسه وينفذ إلى هدفه. فكأنه رسول ساع إلى غاية بينه وبينها أرض محمية ،الحدود، فإذا أذن له صاحبها اجتازها بالإجازة وإن منعه عاد أدراجه. (ر: «البدائع» ١٤٨/٥ - ١٤٩). والعقد الباطل، كما رأينا ليس قائماً متوقفاً ليمكن إنفاذه إلى غايته بالإجازة، بل هو لغو عديم الاعتبار في نظر التشريع، فكأنه لم يوجد أصلاً ر البدائع (۳۰٥/٥ فإذا ما أريدت إجازته لينفذ إلى الغاية الحقوقية المقصودة به يجب إيجاده بإنشاء جديد على وجه معتبر (1) وعلى هذا قرّر الفقهاء أن العقد إذا بَطَل لعدم أهلية العاقد، كما لو باع المجنون أو وهب الصغير شيئاً من ماله ثم عقل المجنون أو بلغ الصغير فأجاز العقد لا يجوز لأنه صَدَر باطلاً فلا يقبل الإجازة، فعليه إن شاء أن يجدّد إنشاءه (2) وكذلك لو تبرع من مال الصغير وليه أو وصيه، أو باعا شيئاً بغبن فاحش ثم بلغ الصبي فأجازه وأمضاه فإنه لا يمضي، لأنه صدر من أصله باطلاً. وعلى هذا أيضاً لو باع فضولي أو اشترى لغيره كان عقده موقوفاً على الإجازة. فلو رفض ذلك الغير العقد عندما بلغه ثم عاد فأجازه لا يعود منعقداً، لأنه قد بطل بالرفض السابق فلم يعد يقبل الإجازة، بل يجب إنشاؤه من جديد إن أريد ( الدر المختار فصل الفضولي ١٤١/٤). ونظيره ما لو أوجب أحد المتعاقدين العقد فرفض الآخر، ثم عاد فقبل، فإنَّ العقد لا ينعقد، لأن الإيجاب بَطَل بالرفض فلم يعد يلحقه قبول. بل يعتبر القبول إيجاباً جديداً يحتاج إلى قبول (ر: ف ٣/٣٠ و ۸/۳۱).
10/5 - Thirdly: A void contract does not accept ratification. For ratification is in reality a unilateral will by which a person confirms and executes an existing suspended transaction for which the Lawgiver has given him the right of confirmation and rejection. The juristic view is that the subject of ratification is the concluded suspended contract that affects the right of a third party. Therefore, that third party may ratify it if he wishes, making it binding on him, or reject it, voiding it. In any case, the suspended contract is considered a valid contract but suspended, i.e., prevented from taking effect. By the ratification of the concerned party, its impediment is removed, it passes its barrier, and reaches its goal. It is like a messenger heading to a destination, with protected land (boundaries) between them; if the owner of the land gives permission, he crosses it by ratification, and if he prevents him, he returns back. (Ref: al-Bada'i' 5/148-149). And a void contract, as we have seen, is not an existing suspended contract that can be executed to its goal by ratification; rather, it is worthless and devoid of consideration in the eyes of the Law, as if it never existed. Ref: al-Bada'i' 5/305. So if one wishes to ratify it to reach the intended legal goal, it must be brought into existence by a new creation in a valid manner (1). Based on this, the jurists have ruled that if a contract becomes void due to lack of capacity of the contracting party, such as if a lunatic sells or a minor gives away something from his property, then the lunatic recovers sanity or the minor reaches puberty and ratifies the contract, it is not permissible because it was void from the start and does not accept ratification. He must, if he wishes, renew its creation (2). Similarly, if the guardian or executor of a minor gives away something from the minor's property, or sells something with gross fraud, then the minor upon puberty ratifies and confirms it, it is not effective because it was void from the beginning. Based on this also, if an unauthorized person (al-fuduli) sells or buys for another, his contract is suspended pending ratification. If that other rejects the contract when he learns of it, then later returns and ratifies it, it does not become valid because it was voided by the prior rejection and no longer accepts ratification. Rather, it must be newly created if desired. (al-Durr al-Mukhtar, chapter on al-Fuduli, 4/141). And analogous to this is if one of the two contracting parties makes an offer and the other rejects, then later returns and accepts, the contract does not conclude because the offer was voided by the rejection and no longer can be followed by acceptance. Rather, the acceptance is considered a new offer needing acceptance (Ref: F 3/30 and 31/8).
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This passage establishes a key principle in Hanafi contract law: a void contract (al-'aqd al-batil) cannot be validated by subsequent ratification (ijazah). Ratification applies only to suspended contracts (al-'aqd al-mawquf), which are valid in themselves but depend on the consent of a third party. A void contract, however, is legally non-existent and cannot be cured; it must be re-enacted. The author provides several examples: a contract by a lunatic or minor before capacity, a guardian's transaction with gross fraud, and an unauthorized agent's contract after it has been rejected. In all cases, ratification after the defect is not possible; a new contract is required. This rule is based on the nature of void contracts as legally irrelevant. The passage also draws an analogy (nazir) to offer and acceptance: once an offer is rejected, it cannot be accepted later; a new offer is needed.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification; a unilateral expression of will that confirms and executes a suspended contract.
عقد موقوف (aqd mawquf): A suspended contract; a contract that is valid in itself but depends on the consent of a third party for its effectiveness.
عقد باطل (aqd batil): A void contract; one that is legally non-existent from the start due to a fundamental defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following contracts can be validated by subsequent ratification (ijazah)?
A) A sale made by a lunatic before recovering sanity
B) A sale made by an unauthorized person (fuduli) that was rejected then later ratified by the owner
C) A sale made by a minor's guardian with gross fraud that the minor ratifies upon puberty
D) A sale made by an unauthorized person (fuduli) that the owner ratifies before any rejection

Key Takeaway
A void contract cannot be validated by subsequent ratification; it must be re-enacted as a new contract.

A Void Contract Does Not Need Rescission and Cannot Be Argued in Court
States the fourth principle: a void contract needs no rescission and cannot be argued in court.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ʿaqd al-bāṭil lā yaḥtāj ilā faskh' to distinguish between void and irregular contracts, and identify when renewal versus ratification is appropriate.

Core Idea
A void contract is legally dead from birth—no need to kill it, and it cannot be summoned in court.

In Hanafi contract law, the distinction between void (bāṭil) and irregular (fāsid) is crucial. This lesson explores a key maxim: a void contract requires no rescission because it never existed legally. We will also see how this maxim interacts with the related concepts of ratification (ijāzah) and renewal (tajdīd), and how modern codifications have adopted this principle.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Identify the Arabic formulation: 'العقد الباطل لا يحتاج إلى فسخ، ولا يحتج به أمام القضاء' and its plain meaning: a void contract needs no rescission and cannot be used as evidence.
This is the core principle.
2
Distinguish batil from fasid
Understand the Hanafi classification: batil contracts are void ab initio, legally nonexistent; fasid contracts are valid in essence but defective, requiring rescission (faskh) to be terminated.
Fasid contracts are the istithnā' (exception) to the maxim.
3
Apply the furu
Consider a void sale contract: no rights or obligations arise from it, neither party can claim anything, and it cannot be presented in court as evidence.
This is the practical effect.
4
Understand the istithnā'
The irregular (fasid) contract is excluded from the maxim; it does need rescission because it has a legal existence but with a defect. This is governed by the complementary maxim 'العقد الفاسد يفسخ'.
The dābṭ of this maxim.
5
Explore modern application
The Syrian Civil Code adopted this rule (Article 142: 'al-butlan la yazul bil-ijazah'). The passage also distinguishes between renewal (tajdīd, requires mutual consent, prospective effect) and ratification (ijāzah, unilateral, retroactive effect).
Reinforces that void contracts cannot be ratified; they must be renewed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١/٥٣ - ( رابعاً : العقد الباطل لا يحتاج إلى فسخ، ولا يحتج به أمام القضاء . (1) وبهذا أخذ القانون المدني السوري الذي صدر حديثاً إذ صرحت المادة / ١٤٢/ منه بأن البطلان لا يزول بالإجازة. (2) والفرق في النتيجة بين تجديد العقد وإجازته ظاهرة : ا - فإن تجديد العقد يتوقف على توافق الإرادتين، بخلاف الإجازة فإنها إرادة منفردة. ب - والعقد المجدد إنما يبدأ أثره من تاريخ تجديده، أما الإجازة إذا صحت فتستند إلى تاريخ العقد المجاز، فيعتبر بها حكم العقد المجاز بأثر نافذاً من تاريخ صدوره، كما تقدم بيانه في نظرية العقود.
11/53 - Fourthly: A void contract does not need rescission, nor can it be argued before the court. (1) And the Syrian Civil Code which was recently enacted has adopted this, as its Article 142 states that voidness (al-butlan) is not removed by ratification. (2) And the difference in consequence between renewing a contract and ratifying it is apparent: First, renewing a contract depends on the agreement of both wills, unlike ratification which is a unilateral will. Second, the renewed contract takes effect from the date of its renewal, whereas if ratification is valid, it relates back to the date of the ratified contract, and thus the ratified contract is considered effective from its date, as previously explained in the theory of contracts.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth principle: a void contract requires no formal rescission (faskh) because it is legally non-existent from the start. Therefore, it cannot be used as evidence or argument in court. The author notes that the Syrian Civil Code has adopted this rule. Then he distinguishes between renewing a contract (tajdid) and ratifying it (ijazah). Renewal requires mutual consent and is effective from the renewal date. Ratification, on the other hand, is unilateral and, if valid, retroactively makes the contract effective from its original date. This distinction reinforces the earlier rule that void contracts cannot be ratified; they must be renewed.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission; judicial or mutual cancellation of a contract that is valid or irregular.
تجديد العقد (tajdid al-'aqd): Renewal of a contract; creating a new contract to replace a void or lapsed one.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification; unilateral approval of a contract, making it retroactively effective.
باطل (batil): Void; a contract that is legally nonexistent from the start due to a fundamental flaw.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule in this passage, what is the status of a void contract in court?
A) It can be argued if the parties agree
B) It must be rescinded by a judge to be invalid
C) It cannot be argued because it is legally non-existent
D) It can be presented but has no effect

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-ʿaqd al-bāṭil lā yaḥtāj ilā faskh' is a cornerstone of Hanafi contract law, reinforcing that voidness is absolute and cannot be cured.

Void Contract Cannot Be Validated by Subsequent Approval
Rule-based expansion of the qawida with applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'العقد الباطل لا يقبل الإجازة' to identify when subsequent approval cannot validate a contract and distinguish between renewal and ratification.

Core Idea
A void contract is legally dead; no amount of later approval can resurrect it.

This lesson explores a fundamental Hanafi maxim: a void (bāṭil) contract cannot be made valid through subsequent approval (ijāzah). The principle distinguishes between a contract that was void from inception and one that was merely suspended (mawqūf). You will learn to apply this rule through several classical cases and understand its modern codification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا قرّر الفقهاء أن العقد إذا بَطَل لعدم أهلية العاقد، كما لو باع المجنون أو وهب الصغير شيئاً من ماله ثم عقل المجنون أو بلغ الصغير فأجاز العقد لا يجوز لأنه صَدَر باطلاً فلا يقبل الإجازة، فعليه إن شاء أن يجدّد إنشاءه. وكذلك لو تبرع من مال الصغير وليه أو وصيه، أو باعا شيئاً بغبن فاحش ثم بلغ الصبي فأجازه وأمضاه فإنه لا يمضي، لأنه صدر من أصله باطلاً. وعلى هذا أيضاً لو باع فضولي أو اشترى لغيره كان عقده موقوفاً على الإجازة. فلو رفض ذلك الغير العقد عندما بلغه ثم عاد فأجازه لا يعود منعقداً، لأنه قد بطل بالرفض السابق فلم يعد يقبل الإجازة، بل يجب إنشاؤه من جديد إن أريد. ونظيره ما لو أوجب أحد المتعاقدين العقد فرفض الآخر، ثم عاد فقبل، فإنَّ العقد لا ينعقد، لأن الإيجاب بَطَل بالرفض فلم يعد يلحقه قبول. بل يعتبر القبول إيجاباً جديداً يحتاج إلى قبول. (۱) وبهذا أخذ القانون المدني السوري الذي صدر حديثاً إذ صرحت المادة / ١٤٢/ منه بأن البطلان لا يزول بالإجازة. (۲) والفرق في النتيجة بين تجديد العقد وإجازته ظاهرة : ا - فإن تجديد العقد يتوقف على توافق الإرادتين، بخلاف الإجازة فإنها إرادة منفردة. ب - والعقد المجدد إنما يبدأ أثره من تاريخ تجديده، أما الإجازة إذا صحت فتستند إلى تاريخ العقد المجاز، فيعتبر بها حكم العقد المجاز بأثر نافذاً من تاريخ صدوره.
And upon this, the jurists decided that if a contract becomes void due to lack of capacity of the contracting party, such as if a mad person sold or a minor gave away something from his property, then the mad person recovers sanity or the minor reaches puberty and they approve the contract, it is not permissible because it was issued void and thus does not accept approval. So if they wish, they must renew the contract. Similarly, if the guardian of a minor donates from his property or sells something with gross deception, then the minor reaches puberty and approves and ratifies it, it is not effective because it was void from its origin. And upon this also, if a fuduli (unauthorised agent) sells or buys for another, his contract is suspended pending approval. If that other refuses the contract when it reaches him, then later returns and approves it, it does not become concluded because it became void by the prior refusal and no longer accepts approval; rather, it must be created anew if desired. An analogous case is if one of the two contracting parties makes an offer, the other rejects it, then later returns and accepts it: the contract is not concluded because the offer was voided by the rejection and acceptance cannot attach to it afterwards. Rather, the acceptance is considered a new offer that requires acceptance. (1) This is what the recently issued Syrian Civil Code adopted, as its Article 142 states that nullity is not removed by ratification. (2) The difference in consequence between renewing the contract and approving it is apparent: a) Renewing the contract depends on agreement of both wills, unlike approval which is a unilateral will. b) The renewed contract takes effect from its renewal date, whereas approval, if valid, is retroactive to the date of the approved contract, so the approved contract is considered effective from its issuance date.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage is a rule-based expansion showing that a void (bāṭil) contract cannot be made valid by subsequent approval (ijāzah). The author presents four applications: (1) incapacity (madness or minority) – if the person later becomes capable and approves, the contract remains void; (2) guardian’s transaction with gross deception – even if the minor later approves, it is void; (3) fudūlī (unauthorised agent) – if the principal first rejects, later approval does not cure the contract; (4) offer and rejection – if the offeree rejects, later acceptance is a new offer. The key principle: a void contract is legally non-existent, so approval is meaningless. The Syrian Civil Code (Art. 142) is cited as contemporary application. The author distinguishes renewal (tajdīd) from approval: renewal requires mutual consent and takes effect prospectively, while approval (if it were valid) would be retroactive.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijāzah): Subsequent approval or ratification of a contract by the person who has the right to do so.
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who contracts on behalf of another without prior authorisation, creating a suspended contract.
بطلان (buṭlān): Nullity; a contract lacking a condition or pillar, making it legally non-existent from the start.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fudūlī sells someone else's property, the owner first refuses, then later approves the sale. What is the ruling according to this passage?
A) The sale becomes valid because approval is retroactive.
B) The sale remains void because it was voided by the refusal.
C) The sale becomes valid if the buyer also agrees.
D) The sale is suspended again and requires a new approval.

Key Takeaway
A void contract is a legal nullity; subsequent approval (ijāzah) cannot validate it; the only remedy is to create a new contract.

Scope of the Distinction between Fasād and Buṭlān
Defining the area where the Hanafi theory of fasād vs. buṭlān applies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion to classify any transaction as having a fasād/buṭlān distinction or not.

Core Idea
The distinction between fasād and buṭlān only applies to financial contracts that create reciprocal obligations or transfer ownership.

The Hanafi school uniquely distinguishes between an invalid (fāsid) contract that nonetheless has some legal effects and a void (bāṭil) contract that is entirely nugatory. This distinction, however, is not universal. The following passage extracts a controlling rule (ḍābiṭ) that tells you exactly where this nuanced theory applies and where it does not, based on an inductive survey of Hanafi fiqh texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two domains
Realise that some acts (like prayer) have only two categories: valid or void. Others (like sale) have three: valid, fāsid, or bāṭil. The passage tells you which is which.
Acts of worship are binary; financial contracts with reciprocal obligations are ternary.
2
Identify the criterion
The passage states one clear rule: 'إنَّ التمييز بين الفساد والبطلان لا يجري إلا في العقود المالية التي تنشىء التزامات متقابلة، أو تنقل الملكية.' That means the distinction only runs in financial contracts that create reciprocal obligations or transfer ownership.
This is a ḍābiṭ (controlling rule), not a general maxim.
3
Apply to included contracts
List the contracts that satisfy the condition: sale, lease, pledge, assignment, settlement over property, sharecropping, partition, partnership, loan (qarḍ), gift (hiba). These are termed munʿaqid (concluded) even when fāsid.
For these contracts, if they are fāsid, they are still considered formed and may produce limited effects until rescinded.
4
Apply to excluded actions
Identify what is excluded: actual actions, unilateral declarations (divorce, waqf, etc.), non-financial contracts (marriage, agency), and financial contracts without reciprocal obligations or ownership transfer (deposit, loan for use without compensation). For these, no intermediate category exists.
Even if a marriage has defects, its fasād and buṭlān are not distinguished in formation, though effects may differ (see footnote).
5
Note the marriage exception
Marriage is excluded from the main rule, but some later Hanafis distinguish fāsid from bāṭil marriage for the purposes of lineage, dower, and ʿidda when there is shubha (doubt). This is an exception in effects, not in formation.
This nuance is important for fatwa, especially in cases of irregular marriages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٥٤) نظرية الفساد : نظرة عامة
التصرفات من فعلية ،وقولية عقدية كانت أو غير عقدية. (ر: ٣/٥١ - 60/01)
أما الفساد فمجاله أضيق من ذلك، فإنَّ التمييز بين البطلان والفساد لا يشمل جميع التصرفات بل إن منها ما ليس له إلا مرتبتان الصحة والبطلان، فلا فرق فيه بين فاسد وباطل في عدم الاعتبار الشرعي. غير أن فقهاء المذهب الحنفي الذي أسس نظرية الفساد إلى جانب البطلان في نطاق المؤيدات الشرعية لم يضع فقهاؤه إلى اليوم ضابطاً يقيم حداً واضحاً لما يجري فيه هذا التمييز بين البطلان والفساد وما لا يجري، ولكنهم قد فصلوا أسباب الفساد وأحكامه تفصيلاً مستوعباً في الفصول التي ميزوا بين بطلانها ،وفسادها كالبيع والإجارة والهبة والمخارجة وغيرها : وهم يصرحون في بعض الفصول الأخرى أنها لا فرق فيها بين فاسد وباطل كما في العبادات من صلاة وصيام وحج وإيتاء الزكاة. فإنها جميعاً تكون إما صحيحة مبرئة لذمة المكلف، أو غير صحيحة فلا يسقط بها الواجب، وفي هذه الحال يقال إنها فاسدة أو باطلة بمعنى واحد.

ضابط ما تجري فيه نظرية الفساد ومالا تجري .
هذا وباستقراء نصوص الفقهاء والأصوليين وتتبع مباحثهم في التمييز بين البطلان والفساد في شتى الفصول الفقهية من الأحكام المدنية يظهر لنا في محل هذا التمييز الضابط التالي :
إنَّ التمييز بين الفساد والبطلان لا يجري إلا في العقود المالية التي تنشىء التزامات متقابلة، أو تنقل الملكية.
أ - فيدخل في هذا الضابط عقود البيع والإجارة والرهن والحوالة والصلح عن المال والمخارجة والقسمة والشركة والمزارعة وأمثالها، لأنها تنشىء التزامات متقابلة .
ويدخل أيضاً عقد القرض والهبة، لأنهما ينقلان الملكية.
فكل ذلك، مما يتميز فساده عن بطلانه يعتبر مع الفساد منعقداً.
ب - ويخرج عن هذا الضابط جميع التصرفات الأخرى التالية :
١ - التصرفات الفعلية مطلقاً.
٢ - التصرفات القولية التي ليست من قبيل العقود، بل من تصرف الإرادة المنفردة كالطلاق ،والإعتاق والوقف ،والإبراء، والكفالة، وكذلك الإقرار
٣ - العقود غير المالية كالزواج والوكالة والوصاية والتحكيم، فإنَّ الوكالة والوصاية والتحكيم عقود تفويض.
٤ - العقود المالية التي لا تنشىء التزاماتٍ متقابلة ولا تنقل الملكية كالإيداع والإعارة. حتى إن الفقهاء لا يصورون الفساد في الإعارة إلا عندما تكون لقاء عوض فتأخذ حكم الإجارة.
فكل هذه الأنواع من التصرفات التي تخرج عن ذلك الضابط لا يعتبر لها إلا مرتبتا وجود وعدم أو صحة وبطلان وليس لها بينهما مرتبة ثالثة الفساد. بل إن فسادها وبطلانها بمعنى واحد في الدلالة على عدم وجودها الاعتباري في نظر الشرع (۱)
(۱) يلحظ هنا ما سبق بيانه في بحث البطلان من أن النكاح الباطل قد تترتب عليه بعض آثار النكاح الصحيح من نسب ومهر وعدة إذا أعقبه دخول، ذلك لأنه يعتبر فيه شبهة كافية لإثبات النسب الذي يتوسع الشرع في إثباته احتياطاً ويتبع ذلك المهر والعدة (ر ف ٤/٥٣). وهذا الأثر هو ما دعا بعض الفقهاء إلى أن يميزوا بين فاسد النكاح وباطله، لا من : حيث الانعقاد ،وعدمه، فكلاهما غير منعقد، بل من حيث ثبوت هذه الآثار وعدمها. - فالنكاح الفاسد عندهم هو ذلك الذي يصلح أن يكون فيه شبهة استحلال للمتعة يثبت : معها النسب والمهر والعدة وإن لم يكن النكاح منعقداً، كالنكاح بلا شهود ونكاح المحرم.
Chapter (54): Theory of Invalidity (Fasād): General Overview
Actions, whether actual or verbal, contractual or non-contractual. (See: 3/51 - 60/01)
As for fasād, its scope is narrower than that; the distinction between buṭlān and fasād does not include all actions but rather some of them have only two categories: soundness (ṣiḥḥa) and voidness (buṭlān), so there is no difference between fāsid and bāṭil in terms of not being legally recognized. However, the jurists of the Ḥanafī school, which established the theory of fasād alongside buṭlān within the scope of legal sanctions, have not to this day set up a precise criterion delimiting where this distinction between buṭlān and fasād applies and where it does not. But they have detailed the causes and rules of fasād comprehensively in the chapters where they distinguished between buṭlān and fasād, such as sale, lease, gift, sharecropping, etc. And they explicitly state in some other chapters that there is no difference between fāsid and bāṭil, as in acts of worship like prayer, fasting, Hajj, and paying Zakāh; all of these are either valid, discharging the obligation of the liable person, or invalid, not fulfilling the duty, and in this case it is said they are fāsid or bāṭil with the same meaning.

Criterion for where the theory of fasād applies and where it does not:
Now, by surveying the texts of jurists and uṣūlīs and following their discussions on the distinction between buṭlān and fasād in various fiqh chapters on civil rulings, the following criterion appears to us regarding the place of this distinction:
The distinction between fasād and buṭlān only applies in financial contracts that create reciprocal obligations or transfer ownership.
A - Included in this criterion are contracts of sale, lease, pledge, assignment, settlement over property, sharecropping, partition, partnership, sharecropping (muzāraʿa), and the like, because they create reciprocal obligations.
Also included are the contracts of loan (qarḍ) and gift (hiba), because they transfer ownership.
All these, in which fasād is distinguished from buṭlān, are considered concluded (munʿaqid) despite being fāsid.
B - Excluded from this criterion are the following other actions:
1 - Actual actions absolutely.
2 - Verbal actions that are not contracts, but rather unilateral expressions of will, such as divorce, manumission, endowment (waqf), release (ibrāʾ), surety (kafāla), and also acknowledgment (iqrār).
3 - Non-financial contracts such as marriage, agency (wakāla), guardianship (waṣāya), and arbitration (taḥkīm); indeed, agency, guardianship, and arbitration are delegating contracts.
4 - Financial contracts that do not create reciprocal obligations nor transfer ownership, such as deposit (wadīʿa) and loan for use (iʿāra). Indeed, the jurists only conceive of fasād in iʿāra when it is for compensation, in which case it takes the ruling of lease.
All these types of actions that fall outside this criterion have only two degrees: existence and non-existence, or validity and voidness, and there is no third degree of fasād between them. Rather, their fasād and buṭlān are synonymous in indicating their non-existence legally in the view of the Lawgiver (1).
(1) Note here what was previously explained in the discussion of buṭlān: that a void (bāṭil) marriage may produce some effects of a valid marriage, such as lineage, dower, and waiting period (ʿidda) if consummated, because it involves sufficient doubt (shubha) to establish lineage, which the Law expansively establishes out of precaution, and this is followed by dower and waiting period (see F. 4/53). This effect is what led some jurists to distinguish between fāsid marriage and bāṭil marriage, not in terms of formation (inʿiqād) and non-formation—both are not formed—but in terms of the establishment of these effects or their non-establishment. Thus, according to them, a fāsid marriage is that which can contain a doubt (shubha) of lawful enjoyment, with which lineage, dower, and ʿidda are established even if the marriage is not formed, such as marriage without witnesses or marriage to a woman in her ʿidda.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Hanafi theory of invalidity (fasād) and its scope. The author begins by noting that the distinction between fasād (invalidity) and buṭlān (voidness) is not universal in fiqh. In some areas, especially acts of worship (ʿibādāt), there are only two categories: valid (ṣaḥīḥ) and invalid (bāṭil), with fasād and buṭlān being synonymous. However, in financial transactions, the Hanafi school recognizes a third intermediate category: a contract may be fāsid (invalid but concluded) rather than bāṭil (void ab initio). The author then extracts a general rule (ḍābiṭ) based on inductive examination (istiqrāʾ) of Hanafi texts: the distinction between fasād and buṭlān only applies in financial contracts that either create reciprocal obligations (like sale, lease, partnership) or transfer ownership (like gift, loan). For these contracts, if they are defective but have the essential elements, they are considered concluded (munʿaqid) but fāsid, meaning they may have some legal effects until rescinded. All other actions—actual acts, unilateral declarations (like divorce, waqf), non-financial contracts (marriage, agency), and financial contracts without reciprocal obligations or ownership transfer (like deposit, loan for use)—do not admit of this distinction; they are either valid or void, with no intermediate stage. The footnote adds a nuance: marriage, though excluded from this rule, may have a practical distinction between fāsid and bāṭil regarding effects like lineage and dower if there is a shubha (doubt), but this is not a distinction in formation but in effects.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): A legal category in Hanafi fiqh for contracts that are defective but considered concluded (munʿaqid), distinguished from void (bāṭil) which is not concluded.
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Complete invalidity; a contract that is not legally recognized and has no effect from the outset.
الضابط (al-ḍābiṭ): A controlling rule or criterion extracted by the author to delimit where the distinction between fasād and buṭlān applies.
العقود المالية (al-ʿuqūd al-māliyya): Contracts that involve financial consideration or property, as opposed to personal status contracts like marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions does the Hanafi school consider to have a distinction between fasād and buṭlān?
A) A contract of sale (bayʿ)
B) A marriage contract (nikāḥ)
C) A unilateral divorce (ṭalāq)
D) An act of worship like prayer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between fasād and buṭlān only operates within the sphere of financial contracts that create reciprocal obligations or transfer ownership; outside that sphere, fāsid and bāṭil are synonymous.

Jahala Fahisha: The Hanafi Maxim on Excessive Ignorance in Contracts
Definition and examples of the Hanafi maxim on excessive ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim on jahala fahisha to determine whether a contract is invalid due to excessive ignorance, and distinguish cases where minor ignorance is tolerated.

Core Idea
Excessive ignorance that creates an irresolvable dispute invalidates any commutative contract.

In Hanafi contract law, not every unknown detail ruins a deal. The key is whether the ignorance is so deep that it fuels an equal and irresolvable disagreement between the parties. This lesson unpacks the maxim 'al-jahala al-fahisha tufsid al-aqd' and its practical applications, including the exception of a specifying container and the permissibility of khiyar al-ta'yin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of ignorance
Determine what element of the contract is unknown (e.g., price, subject matter, shares). This is the starting point for applying the maxim.
The ignorance must be related to an essential term of the contract.
2
Assess if the ignorance leads to a problematic dispute (niza mushkil)
A problematic dispute is one where both parties have equally strong arguments based on the ignorance, making it impossible to resolve. If the ignorance can be resolved by a clear external factor (like a container), it is not fahisha.
Hanafis define 'niza mushkil' as a dispute where arguments are equal (tasawi al-hujja).
3
Apply the maxim
If the ignorance is excessive and leads to an irresolvable dispute, the contract is invalid (fasid). If the ignorance is minor or can be resolved, the contract remains valid.
The maxim is: 'al-jahala al-fahisha allati tufdi ila niza mushkil tufsid al-aqd'.
4
Check for the exception
If the unknown item is enclosed in a specific container (hadd hasir) that provides a clear boundary, the ignorance is not fahisha because the container itself is a valid argument to settle disputes.
Example: selling the contents of a chest – the chest is the hadd hasir.
5
Recognize the extension to khiyar al-ta'yin
The same principle validates the option of specification (khiyar al-ta'yin), where a party can later specify which item the contract applies to, as it prevents irresolvable disputes.
This is permitted by qiyas on khiyar al-shart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولاً : الجهالة
55/2 - الجهالة المفسدة للعقود في الاجتهاد الحنفي إنما يراد بها الجهالة الفاحشة وهي التي تفضي إلى نزاع مشكل، والنزاع المشكل هو الذي يتعذر حسمه لتساوي حجة الطرفين فيه استناداً إلى تلك الجهالة. (ر: البدائع في الإجارة 207/4).
وذلك كما لو باع إنسان شاة غير معينة من قطيع غنم : فالبائع قد يريد إعطاء الرديئة بحجة عدم التعيين والمشتري كذلك يريد الجيدة بحجة عدم التعيين أيضاً.
وكذا لو تعاقدا إجارة بأجرة مجهولة أو مزارعةً دون بيان حصة كل منهما من الحاصل ولا يوجد عرف في مكان العقد ليرجع إليه. وكذا لو عقد أشخاص شركة دون بيان كيفية تقسيم الربح بينهم (ر: المجلة / 1336).
فكل ذلك وأمثاله من صور الجهالة مفسد للعقد. أما الجهالة التي لا تؤدي إلى نزاع مشكل فلا تضر بالعقد. وذلك كما لو باع الإنسان كل ما في صندوقه أو في بيته دون معرفة ما فيه. فيصح العقد لأن المبيع، وإن كان مجهولاً هو معين بالذات بحد حاصر له متفق عليه وهو الصندوق أو البيت وهذا التعيين حجة صالحة لإلزام كلا المتبايعين وحسم النزاع : فإنه إذا ظهر أكثر مما يظن البائع فحجة المشتري عليه راجحة؛ وإذا ظهر أقل مما يظن المشتري فحجة البائع عليه راجحة (ر: رد المحتار 21/4).
وعلى هذا جوز الاجتهاد الحنفي أيضاً اشتراط خيار التعيين في عقود المعاوضة، كما لو بيع أحد أشياء معينة على أن يكون لأحد المتعاقدين من بائع أو مشتر حق تعيين الواحد الذي ينصرف إليه البيع.
وقد أقر الاجتهاد الحنفي اشتراط خيار التعيين هذا قياساً على خيار الشرط (ر: ف 7/3 ثامناً) لحاجة المتعاقد إلى التروّي والمشورة فيهما.
First: Ignorance (Jahala). 55/2 - The ignorance that invalidates contracts in Hanafi ijtihad is only meant to be excessive ignorance (jahala fahisha), which is that which leads to a problematic dispute (niza mushkil). A problematic dispute is one that is impossible to resolve because the arguments of both parties are equal based on that ignorance. (See: al-Bada'i' on Ijarah 4/207). That is like if a person sells an unspecified sheep from a flock of sheep: the seller may want to give the inferior one on the grounds of non-specification, and similarly the buyer wants the good one on the grounds of non-specification. Likewise, if they contract an ijarah with an unknown wage, or a muzara'ah without specifying the share of each from the produce, and there is no custom (urf) in the place of the contract to refer to. Likewise, if people contract a partnership without specifying how to divide the profit among them (see: al-Majalla 1336). So all this and similar examples of ignorance invalidate the contract. As for ignorance that does not lead to a problematic dispute, it does not harm the contract. That is like if a person sells everything in his chest or in his house without knowing what is in it. Then the contract is valid, because the sold item, although unknown, is specifically identified (muan bi al-dhat) by a containing boundary agreed upon, namely the chest or the house. This specification is a valid argument to bind both parties and resolve the dispute: for if more appears than the seller thinks, the buyer's argument prevails over him; and if less appears than the buyer thinks, the seller's argument prevails over him (see: Radd al-Muhtar 4/21). And on this basis, Hanafi ijtihad also permitted stipulating the option of specification (khiyar al-ta'yin) in commutative contracts, such as when selling one of specific items with the condition that one of the contracting parties, seller or buyer, has the right to specify the one to which the sale pertains. And Hanafi ijtihad approved stipulating this option of specification by analogy with the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart) (see: F 7/3 eighth), due to the contracting parties' need for deliberation and consultation in both.
Source pages p. 700

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the impact of ignorance (jahala) on contract validity in the Hanafi school. The author defines 'jahala fahisha' as excessive ignorance that leads to a dispute that cannot be resolved because both parties have equally strong arguments based on that ignorance. He gives examples: selling an unspecified sheep (each party wants a different one), hiring with an unknown wage, sharecropping without shares, partnership without profit division. In all these, the contract is invalid. However, if the ignorance is minor and does not lead to an irresolvable dispute, the contract is valid. The example is selling the contents of a box or house: although the contents are unknown, the container is a 'hadd hasir' (encompassing boundary) that allows resolution. The author then extends this principle to validate the option of specification (khiyar al-ta'yin), where a party can later specify which item is sold. This is permitted by qiyas on khiyar al-shart, as it serves the need for deliberation.

Key Terms
جهالة فاحشة (jahala fahisha): Excessive ignorance that leads to a dispute where the arguments of both parties are equal and irresolvable.
خيار التعيين (khiyar al-ta'yin): The option to specify which item among several the contract applies to, stipulated in a commutative contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, which type of ignorance invalidates a contract?
A) Any ignorance regardless of degree
B) Ignorance that leads to an irresolvable dispute (jahala fahisha)
C) Ignorance that is not excessive
D) Ignorance only about the price

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school invalidates contracts only when ignorance is so severe that it creates an irresolvable dispute; minor ignorance is tolerated, especially if there is a specifying boundary.

The Four Aspects of Invalidating Ignorance
Classification of the four aspects of invalidating ignorance in contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four aspects of invalidating ignorance in Hanafi contracts and apply the qawāʿid to determine whether ignorance about a particular element invalidates a contract.

Core Idea
Four types of ignorance invalidate: subject matter, consideration, binding time, and security instruments.

This passage categorizes the sources of invalidating ignorance (jahālah mufsidah) in contracts. As a mufti, you must recognize which contractual elements require specification and which do not. The author lists four aspects, with a critical distinction between binding and non-binding terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching rule
The maxim that ignorance of certain aspects invalidates the contract. The text lists four categories, so the rule is: ignorance about any of these four renders the contract void (fāsid).
These categories are exhaustive for invalidating ignorance in muʿāwaḍāt.
2
Distinguish the four categories
List them: subject matter (maʿqūd ʿalayh), consideration (ʿiwaḍ), binding time periods (ājāl mulzima), and security instruments (wasāʾil al-tawthīq). Each category has examples in the text.
Memorize the four categories; they appear frequently in fatwa work.
3
Examine the binding vs non-binding distinction
Only binding terms require knowledge; non-binding terms (e.g., partnership duration) do not invalidate. The Hanafi view that partnership is non-lāzim (revocable) is the basis.
This is a key nuance: not all time periods are treated equally.
4
Apply to examples
For each category, note the examples: sold item (subject matter), price (consideration), lease duration (binding term), and guarantor/pledge (security instrument). Confirm that ignorance in these cases invalidates.
Work through the examples to solidify the categories.
5
Connect to fatwa practice
When a mufti receives a case about contract validity, check these four aspects. If ignorance is present in any, the contract is fāsid unless it falls under the non-binding exception.
This taxonomy streamlines case analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣/٥٥ - الجهات التي تتعلق بها الجهالة المفسدة:
هذا، وإن الجهالة المفسدة للعقد إنما ترجع غالباً إلى أربع جهات :
١ - الجهالة في المعقود عليه (محل العقد) وذلك كجهالة المبيع في عقد البيع أو المأجور في الإجارة، أو المُصالح عنه في الصلح، أو الموهوب في الهبة، وهكذا.
۲ - جهالة العوض في عقود المعاوضات المالية وذلك كجهالة الثمن في عقد البيع، وجهالة البدل المصالح عليه في عقد الصلح (۱).
٣ - جهالة الآجال في كل ما يجري فيه أجل ملزم وذلك كجهالة المدة المتعاقد عليها في عقد الإجارة، وجهالة موعد استحقاق الثمن المؤجل في عقد البيع، أو موعد استحقاق البدل المؤجل في عقد الصلح. أما الأجل غير الملزم فلا تضر جهالته كمدة استمرار عقد الشركة . فإنَّ الشركة عقد غير لازم في الاجتهاد الحنفي. فلو حدد لها أجل جاز للشريك فسخها قبله فلا حاجة إلى معرفة أجلها سلفاً، بل تستمر حتى يوجد ما ينهيها من فسخ قصدي أو موت.
٤ - جهالة وسائل التوثيق المشروطة في العقد وذلك كما لو اشترط البائع على المشتري تقديم كفيل أو رهن بالثمن المؤجل. فيجب أن يكون الكفيل والرهن معينين، وإلا فسد البيع.
(۱) بالنظر القانوني يعتبر كل من العوضين المبيع والثمن) محلاً للعقد.
3/55 - The aspects to which invalidating ignorance relates:
Now, invalidating ignorance of the contract generally pertains to four aspects:
1- Ignorance about the subject matter (locus of the contract), such as ignorance of the sold item in a sale, or the leased item in ijarah, or the item of compromise in sulh, or the gifted item in hibah, etc.
2- Ignorance about the consideration in financial commutative contracts, such as ignorance of the price in a sale, or ignorance of the countervalue in sulh (1).
3- Ignorance about time periods in everything that involves a binding term, such as ignorance of the contracted period in ijarah, or ignorance of the due date of deferred price in a sale, or the due date of deferred countervalue in sulh. As for a non-binding term, its ignorance does not harm, such as the duration of a partnership contract. For a partnership is a non-binding contract in Hanafi ijtihad. If a term is set, a partner may dissolve it before that term, so there is no need to know its term in advance. Rather, it continues until something terminates it, such as intentional dissolution or death.
4- Ignorance about the security instruments stipulated in the contract, such as if the seller stipulates on the buyer to provide a guarantor or pledge for the deferred price. The guarantor and pledge must be specified; otherwise, the sale is invalid.
(1) From a legal perspective, each of the two considerations (sold item and price) is considered the subject matter of the contract.
Source pages p. 701

Commentary
This passage, continuing from the previous, lists the four aspects to which invalidating ignorance relates. The first is ignorance about the subject matter (ma'qud 'alayh), which is the object of the contract (e.g., sold item, leased item, subject of compromise, gift). The second is ignorance about the consideration (al-'iwad) in commutative contracts (e.g., price, countervalue). The third is ignorance about binding time periods (al-ajal al-mulzim), such as the duration of an ijarah or the due date of a deferred payment; however, ignorance of a non-binding term, like the duration of a partnership (which is not binding in the Hanafi school because partners can dissolve at will), does not invalidate the contract. The fourth is ignorance about security instruments (wasa'il al-tawthiq), such as a guarantor or pledge, which must be specified. The footnote clarifies that legally, both the sold item and the price are considered the subject matter. This categorization helps muftis identify which types of ignorance are problematic.

Key Terms
المعقود عليه (al-ma'qud 'alayh): The subject matter of the contract, such as the sold item, leased property, etc.
العوض (al-'iwad): The consideration or countervalue in a commutative contract, e.g., price in sale.
الآجال (al-ajal): Time periods or terms, specifically binding terms in contracts.
وسائل التوثيق (wasa'il al-tawthiq): Security instruments like guarantor (kafil) or pledge (rahn) stipulated in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four aspects of invalidating ignorance according to the Hanafi school?
A) Ignorance about the subject matter.
B) Ignorance about the consideration.
C) Ignorance about the capacity of the parties.
D) Ignorance about security instruments.

Key Takeaway
When examining a contract for invalidating ignorance, check four aspects: subject matter, consideration, binding terms, and security instruments; non-binding terms are exempt.
